The announcement of American victories over Spanish warships was received by the public with
cheers and congratulations. The American is of the true British stock, and there is a quality
about the British breed of sailors that comes out in times of emergency.
The same fact applies to quality the world over —quality will tell, even if it is only
... CAMEO CIGARETTES ...
The pure and true quality of “CAMEOS” is a guarantee that they are made from the right
stock, and by people who know how to make Cigarettes.
THE RED PAGE.
NIGHT.
(AFTER ALL.)
When the time comes for me to die,
To-morrow, or some other day,
If God should bid me make reply,
•What would’st thou ? ’ I shall say,
O God, Th v world was great and fair;
Yet give me to forget it clean !
Vex me no more with things that were,
And things that might have been.
I loved, I toiled, throve ill or well,
Lived certain years and murmured nOtt
Now grant me in that land to dwell
Where all things are forgot.
For others. Lord, Thy purging fires,
The loves reknit, the crown, the palm.
For me, the death of all desires
In deep, eternal calm.
T. W. Rolleston.
The Triumph of Death.
The keynote of the story is Death. And the
book is “morbid,” therefore? No, indeed ! That
is a quaint, traditional, superstitious fancy which
marks the close of life with sadness and only the
beginning of it with joy : as if Life were not for
very many of the living, from any exalted point
of view, a thing infinitely sadder thaq Death.
Life harmonises with one set of conditions ; Death
with another set ; but both Life and Death are
natural, may be beautiful, are inspiring themes for
music, painting, literature. The one, as a fact, is no
more “morbid” than the other ; the contemplation
of the one is no more to be sliunned than the con-
templation of the other ; and though Death come
often by disease, has not Life itself been held to be
a disease? We swing from Night into Day, and
from Dly into Night again ; and there is in the
fitful fever no more (it may be less) artistic sanity,
completeness, than in the eternal calm.
The keynote of the story is Death—death by
violence—death by falling from a shattering
height. The lovers are fascinated, and look over
the parapet of the steep Roman street. Far
down, they see a vague, horrible stain.
Giorgio broods. His uncle, the relative whom
he best knew and best loved, was a suicide.
“He died because he could not make his life
conform to his ideals.” Ippolita is sad, feeling
the omen evil. Yet, as they part, the instinct of
vitality swells up defiant, and they separate on
the edge of an intoxicating promise to meet later
in the evening.
But the keynote of the story is Death : from the
keynote the theme of Love rebounds, rises, falls,
wavers ; to the keynote m the end it triumphantly
returns.
The book is a wonderful analysis of character
and passion ; and the force, the sustained force of
it has not been paralleled in recent English. To
the fashion of treatment there is no parallel in
any English: Hardy comes nearest in his later
novels, with the philosophic idea shown in the
working of life; but Hardy’s philosophy is a stiff,
antique thing beside this of D’Annunzio, a modern
of moderns. And in any case Hardy has not the
virile pulsation of matter, the sweeping eloquence
of style, which mark D’Annunzio’s book. The
Hardy of “Teas ” and the Meredith of “ Richard
Feverel ” might together write something of
kindred potency, not the same.
The usual sardonic reflections about one’s ignor-
ance rise with the appearance of an English
translation of “II Trionto della Morte.” The
original seems to have been written seven or
eight years ago, published in Italian, and sent
through the Revue des Deux Mondes in what is
called an admirable translation. America had
the book last year, in an unexpurgated version
which was (unsuccessfully) prosecuted by a New
York “social purity” society : England gets it
this year in a much expurgated version issued by
publisher Heinemann. Yet through all the blots
and omissions of the translation D’Annunzio’s
genius shine* like midsummer sun through a cob-
web.
D’Annunzm resides always in Italy. “ The
Triumph of Death ” is only one of his half-dozen
novels : some say it is not his best, in the artistic
sense. But it seems to be his most powerful;
and last year there was a story current that
some burglars, who broke into D’Annunzios
house by peradventure, melted to warm Southern
tears on learning that it was “ The Triumph’s ”
author who came in his nightshirt to expostulate
with them. And to the versatility of the man
(lie is recognised as one of the finest con-
temporary Italian poets) testifies the enthusiastic
reception given by literary Paris to his written-in-
French play of “La Ville Morte,” produced three
months ago. D’Annunzio’s pictures give him a
diminutive air, with a smallish head set on stoop-
ing shoulders, and thick up-twirling moustaches.
He looks warm and vital, yet weary and potenti-
ally ascetic. His age is near 30.
D’Annunzio himself regards “The Triumph of
Death ” as a moral work, with the kind of shock-
moral whioh Daudet attributed to “Sapho.”
But in D’Annunzio’s book there is little of
the “Sapho” revulsion. The character of
Giorgio is presented less as insane than as
inevitable; and there is ample room for sympathy
with his course and conclusion. Throughout the
pregnant passages more comes to agree with than
to disagree with. Giorgio strives, like all of us,
for the ideal Love, and is continually cheated by
the inadequacy of the real. He harps on Brown-
ing’s “So the good moment goes.” Ippolita is
everything to him, and yet, at the utmost —noth-
ing. “ How much of you can I call my own ?” he
asks. “All,” replies Ippolita, who indeed loves
him fondly. “Nothing—or next to nothing,” he
says sadly. “You are practically an unknown
person to me. The soul is incommunicable —you
cannot give me your soul. Even in our supremest
raptures we are and remain apart —solitary.”
Early in the book the lovers celebrate the
second anniversary of their loving by a holiday
in the country. Their happiness satisfies the
woman: the man, looking before and after,
is never able to evade the obsession of
his melancholy thoughts. He is cultured, ner-
vous, sensitive tuned to the most delicate
emotions ; and at every moment the crude things
of life discord in him. Ippolita, more sensual,
less intellectual is content with the present ;
Giorgio is toroured by the past, terrified at the
future. The holiday ends—“ another fragment of
their life and love is broken off and dropped into
the fathomless abyss of Time.” “So this too is
over,” said Giorgio.
To make clear this complex character, D’An-
nunzio takes him to his home, where are shown
his parents—gross sensuality on the one side,
nervous refinement on the other. The keynote
returns, more dominating, when Giorgio is
tempted to kill himself in the room and in the
manner of his uncle Demetrius. But the time is
not yet. “A thousand invisible threads bound him
still to life * Ippolita ! ’ He turned impetu-
ously towards the window —to the light.
The distant landscape, nebulously blue and
mysterious, melted out of sight with the languor-
ous day. La Majella, all bathed in the liquid
gold of the setting sun, leaned her rounded bosom
against the sky.”
In the next phase the lovers, hitherto only in-
termittently together, resolve to be'joined for
always. They choose a country hermitage where
Love and Nature are to lead them to the
higher life. Giorgio goes first to make all
ready, and is powerfully impressed by the magic
which the motherhood of wild Earth has for all
high and romantic spirits. He foresees “ a bound-
less liberty, a noble and fruitful solitude. Oto
walk amongst these children of the earth . . .
to contemplate Nature with such uninterrupted
attention as to succeed in reproducing in myself
the rhythmic palpitation of all created life, and,
finally, by a laborious metamorphose of the ideal,
identify myself witli the tall tree which with its
roots absorbs the invisible juices of the earth
and imitates with its topmost boughs the
rushing of the sea! ” Ippolita comes, after
days of longing, to a night of ecstasy. At
the meeting “ she held his face between her
hands, and drawing him to her, passed her soft,
warm lips all over his face in one long-drawn kiss.
Giorgio recognised the divine, the incomparable
month whioh had seemed to him so often to rest
on t'ie surface of his soul, as if in a rapture sur-
passing all carnal emotion, and communicating
itself to some supersensitive element of his Spiri-
tual personality.”
And now begins once more the age-long con-
flict between soaring spirit and tethering flesh. In
Giorgio’s experience is repeated the truism that
Woman is the death of the Individual, the life
of the Race. For the woman sums birth and
death ; she rocks the cradle and she digs the grave;
creator and destroyer is she, and her left hand
knows not what her right hand doeth. There are
times when every intellectual lover sees in her
true essence the woman he loves —when he recog-
nises that her delicious caresses are sapping his
strength, that each of her pleasures hides its pre-
ponderant pang, that the light of her eyes, the
music of her voice, the seduction of her body, are
flowers spread on the edge of the precipice which
invites him. And a great hatred may rise in him
against this murderess who visibly draws life from
his veins, who emerges ruddy and joyous from
bathing in his blood, by whom, at the bidding
of incomprehensible aboriginal forces, he is seduced
to sacrifice his ideals and possibilities, his dreams
and exaltations, on the altar ol a physical lu3t.
Poignancy comes with the consideration that
ideals and exaltations are the efflorescence of lust
unattained.
The story delays not, nor hurries, but passes
inevitably to its close. Passages of beauty and
suggestion crowd closely on each other. There is
a marvellous description of a pilgrimage to a
shrine, conceived antecedently to that in Zola’s
“ Lourdes,” and surpassing it in picturesque in-
tensity. And through all goes the motive of
Heredity, swaying Giorgio hither and thither
through his sensual father, his sensitive mother.
“ He saw himself in the distant future fettered to
his carnal lust like a slave to the galley, deprived
of all will and thought, degraded, unmanned ”
and gradually his resolution takes shape and
strengthens. And as he loses force he sees
enraged how his mistress gains it —she, “ the
woman of sensual delight, the ‘ flower of concupi-
scence,’ the Enemy.” And ever as he shrinks,
she pursues. “You are afraid of my kisses!”
And his mingled love and hate, his heredity and
history, draw him irresistibly to the formidable
crisis. “ Nec sine te nec tecum vivere possum.”
The last scene comes.
The promontory fell sheer down to the desolate black
rocks round which the sluggish water heaved with a
faint splash, rocking the reflection of the stars upon
its bosom. . . . He turned and came towards her with
outstretched hands.
With a sudden rapid movement he clutched her wrists
and dragged her forward, then, throwing his arms round
her, made a dash for the edge of the precipice.
“No—no —no!” She made a frantic resistance, and,
succeeding in getting herself from his grasp, sprang back
breathless and trembling.
“ Are you mad ? ” she cried, half choked with rage and
fear. “ Are you mad ? ”
But when she saw him seize her again without a word,
felt herself dragged in a more brutal grasp towards the
abyss, she understood —in a sudden baleful flash that
struck terror to her soul.
“No, Giorgio no—let me go—let me go. One minute
—listen, listen—just one minute —I want to tell you ”
Mad with fright, she writhed and supplicated, hoping
against hope to stop him—to soften him.
“One minute—listen to me—l love you! Oh, forgive
me—forgive me ! ”
She stammered wild, incoherent, despairing words—
feeling her strength fail her, losing the ground beneath
her feet—death staring her in the face.
“ Murderer ! ” she shrieked, defending herself desper-
ately—with her teeth, her nails, like a wild beast.
“Murderer!” she shrieked again, seized by the hair,
thrown on her knees at the very edge of the abyss—lost.
The dog harked furiously at the tragic group.
There was a brief but savage struggle, as between two
mortal foes who Jiad nourished a secret and implacable
hatred in their souls up to that hour. . . .
Then they crashed down headlong into death, locked
in that fierce embrace.
. . . the rough thought that shakes my soul
I sing.
But unluckily there is no evidence that his
soul has been shaken; and to be poet a fellow
must not only be shaken : he must actively
shake. Apollo’s priestess had to writhe in
public: a private tussle with the god in the
back parlour of the temple was of no use to Delphi
or anybody. When Inspiration gets hold of a
man and shakes him as a terrier shakes a rat you
expect to see teeth-marks in the back of his
poetical neck. Where are the teeth-marks in Mr.
Evans’s verses? Well, Brunton Stephens sees
them :he ranks himself as one of vans’s ad-
mirers, and thinks the new book confirms his
judgment. “A Dream Flight” seems to B.S.
“really exquisite.” Randolph Bedford says that he
sees the marks ; and Bedford has some fibres tuned
to fine emotions. The Queenslander sees them :
it considers Evans’s performance most admirable.
Here you have in harmony a modest man of letters,
a mining speculator, who isnot modest, and a moral
weekly newspaper. And with the best will in the
world, and m spite of the decent and complimentary
premisses, the local voice is discordant. So what
is a critic to do? Perhaps the best way out of
the difficulty is to frankly Btate it.
Low at our dear God’s feet.
But at p. 41, “ On Tennyson’s Death,”
The angels rise %n beauties sweet
And bear his soul to Jesus’ feet.
After this, the introduction of Tennyson s own
feet (p. 42)
Why weep ? his aced pure feet have trod
The wondrous path to Lteav'n and God
rather spoils the effect; though there is virtue in
the corroboration of the general belief that
Tennyson washed ’em. On p. 62, the olimax
adorns Cleopatra’s feet —
Oh ! Cleopatra — o’er Time’s heaving waves
I stretch my hand unto thy spirit fleet
That I may tell thee of the God that saves,
And lay this loving tribute at thy feet.
Possibly, however, Cleopatra has fallen victim to
the necessity for a scientific and harmonious
rhyme : people with a “ spirit fleet ” must expect
to suffer so. At p. 82, “earth’s waking prayer”
is “ wafted ” “ thro’ the heaven blue and bare ”
Unto God’s feet above;
but the counterwaft follows on p. 117, when “ eerie
Death ” is invited to “ waft me above,” and (with
a superabundance of commas),
Bear thou, my soul, so sinful, home
To Jesus’ feet.
At p. 120, “Change” culminates on Time’s
“lightning speed and padded feet”; and at p.
134 those who knew “ Our Darling ” laid their
hearts “at his wee feet.” Somehow there is a
flat fall about “fairy feet” (p. 167), “thy dear
feet ” (p. 175), and all the other feet. Besides the
feet there are dingoes, curlews, and visions of the
Tomb—
Byron, Jonson, Leigh Hunt, Keats, Beethoven,
Charlotte Bronte and Chopin are there,
Marie BashKirtseff, whose hopes were woven
With the wan strands of Death’s dusky hair.
Jenny Lind, Marcus Clarke, and dear Newman [? NoggsJ
Whose face hears the imprint of prayer.
Pickles couldn’t be mixeder. But enough
has been said to show what ornaments
“ A Twilight Teaching and Other Poems” would
be to the “Poet’s Corner” of the country
newspaper. The astounding thing is that they
didn’t stay there. Possibly there were too many
feet to the inch of Corner, and Tennyson’s and
Cleopatra’s and the rest simply had to walk out
into notoriety, much to the disgust of Tennyson’s.
Capable Leslie Stephen recently agreed Tenny-
son was greater artist than poet, “ ascribing far higher
praise to the technical perfection of the Laureate’s verse
than to his thought.” A Lend. Author anecdote: Ten-
nyson, dining with John Sterling, said to have said, “I
don’t think that since Shakespeare there has been such a
master of the English language as I am. To he sure, I’ve
got nothing to say.” And we all have lucid moments.
The “feature” of a reported*new Lond. jour-
nal, The Quill Driver, is that every book review is written
by the book’s author. Amer. Bookman first utters the
salient comments: (1) After all, the author knows his
book best; (2) nobody can say the reviewer never read the
book.
Little rills of notice keep trickling in to show
that Henry Lawson's vogue is growing. From Chambers’s
and Blackwood’s Maga, publisher Methuen, and others,
he has had invitations to write. And the manager of
Boston (U.S.) National Magazine sends on March 11,
offering five or ten dollarsfa pound or two) for permission
to collar some of L’s best yarns. The cheerful Yankee
person points out that he could steal ’em if he liked, but
he prefers to salve his conscience to the extent of five or
ten dollars. This keen sense of honor should boom the
stock of five-dollar consciences quite a piece.
Tite cover to April Scribner’s Magazine (Gordon
and Gotch) has as pretty a bit of decoration as one
remembers: coloring and working are admirable.
Scribner’s illustrations are always noticeably good, and
the editorial policy se ms to please a foreign palate better
than that of Harper’s or the Century.

W.G.T.: You can’t get Stephen Phillips’s poems in Syd.
or Melbourne at date of writing. The average bookseller
is acquainted only with the outsides of books, and when
he orders a large quantity of poems from England he
generally gets left with a stock of rubbish. So even * the
leading firms ’ now get driblets of three copies or so.
They’re safe on the duffers ; and the public sadly waits
weeks and months for repeat-orders of good stuff—

Perhaps the best way out of the difficulty is to
frankly state it. George Essex Evans, a writer of
verse whose lot has fallen in Queensland, issues
his second book, “Loraine and Other Verses ”
(Geo. Robertson and Co.). Evtins is a good
fellow, and writes good verse (some of which has
appeared in Bulletin), and he sends acomplimen-
tary copy with a complimentary inscription. Now,
as previously explained, the newspaper-appeal is
one thing and the book-appeal is another tiling;
also, reprint-justification is another thing and
print-justification is one thing. And the Red-
Page opinion of Mr. Evans’s verses is that, con-
sidered as Poetry with a P, they are readable and
regular enough, but essentially uninspired. Often
neatly didactic or pleasantly descriptive, they
have no wings—are always on the pedestrian level.
In bis “ Dedication ” Mr. Evans says
Lala Fisher, author of “ A Twilight Teaching,
and Other Poems ” (Fisher Unwin), is a God’s-
Feet kind of local rhymer who discredits Aus-
tralia by inviting critical attention in a neat bind-
ing at 6s. There are, on a loose calculation, from
80 to 90 “ Other Poems ”in the neat binding; and
one slides through their greased commonplace
with the impression that from 80 to 90 per cent,
of them end by taking judicious refuge somewhere
near God’s Feet —which are theoretically unable
to kick anything. Probably God’s Feet make an
impression out of proportion to their numbers,
and the percentage is not really so large ; but this,
one feels sure, is the author’s fault, not her mis-
fortune. For she has artlessly tried to vary her
climaxes. The x which resulted “On Hearing
Florrie Sohmidt Sing ” does indeed leave the song

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Amusing to notice how G. R. Sims, writ-
ing as “ Dagonet ” in London Referee, still does
his best to ridicule the County Council and ob-
struct municipal reform. Years ago, Sims ob-
tained a sinecure civic billet for his father. The
“old man” was appointed to the utterly sinful-
waste-of-money position of Lord Mayor’s Remem-
brancer. There are no gimcrack remembrancers
or things attached to the County Council form of
government, seemingly. Anyways, G.R.S. speaks
of Ben Tillett and co. as a disgraceful crowd—
“extreme Radicals, Socialists, and self-seekers,”
and his indignation is truly funny.
Dear B.—ln a paragraph on the “Vic-
torian Ladies’ Work Association,” running a
geographical word-competition, you remark —
“ Where is Kioto, and if it be the name of a
town, is it well known in comparison with Tokio ? ”
Considering that Kioto is the capital of Japan, I
should imagine it was the name of a town, and a
very well-known one too, to anyone whose know-
ledgeextendsbeyondParramatta. Ask Jack Want.
—HX. [Kioto was the ancient capital of Japan,
but Tokio is the capital. When the Emperor’s
court was removed to Tokio in 1869, old Kioto
was renamed Saikio. The “Ladies’Work Asso-
ciation ” forwarded a. £5 prize to a word-transposer
who sent along Kioto and St. Cloud (a village) as
the names of well-known towns, and the par.
suggested that the award was unsatisfactory.—
Ed. B.]
The Silent War.
H. G. Turner : “ But even if our history had been
pregnant with the subliniest material, instead of being
hopelessly commonplace” . . . (p. 24). — The Development
of A ustralian Literature.
Away through the gaps and the valleys, alone
with the desert and drought,
Is spreading the Army of Silence ; dead fiercely
they battle it out
With hunger and mirage and madness, an eerie
invisible doom,
With phantoms that flaunt in the daylight and
shadows that haunt in the gloom.
No ring of exulting admirers is firing the braves
with a cheer ;
No volleys are heard in the vanguard, no thunder
applauds from the rear;
No leader is urging them onward, no sound of the
bugle and drum
Is thrilling their manhood with daring—for death
in the desert is dumb.
The lines of the fighters are severed by mountain
and forest and mead,
With never the love of a woman to tend in the
hour of their need,
With seldom the voice of a comrade to mutter a
prayer at the last,
When faint and compelled to surrender they pass
where so many have passed.
They fought for the cosy selections that nestle on
river and lake;
They wakened the riches that slumbered for those
that have come in the wake.
They died, but they were not rewarded ; they suf-
fered, and never a word
Is said for the life they have given, the fond things
they never have heard.
Still loveless and lonely they wander, grim truly
they stick to the ranks :
A sneer for their hire and a shrug of the shoulders
for pension and thanks.
Behind them are thronging the others that thrive
on the spoil of the fight;
But they—they press onward and outward, and
vanish in silence and night.
Go, ask them the Why and the Whither, the gold
and the glory they gain !
A shake of the head is the answer : They know,
but they cannot explain.
For strange as the depth of the heart is the love
for the bush and the track,
But only the eye of the artist can picture the
beauty out back.
The night wind to them is the bringer of many a
rapturous note,
But only the ear of a singer can fancy the music
afloat.
The prize is to others. They know it. Yet ever
they follow the call —
And only the heart of a poet can feel for the joy
of it all!
N.Q.
VON KOTZE.
“Jane.”
The wind is like a “willy-willy ” t
W’irlin’ round upon the plain,
For frettin’s sent me fairly silly
Since you’ve thrown me over, Jane !
About twel’ months ago, las’ seedin’,
Down the road by Thompson’s gate,
You said, in answer to me pleadin’,
“Yes,” an’ sealed me bloomin’ fate !
Oh, Jane ! Jane ! Jane !
You’ve broke me ’eart in two with pain !
Me believin’! you deceivin’!
Fickle, false, an’ foolish Jane !
The little Baptist gospel-skillion
Clangs its weary little bell,
A-tellin’ how the worldly million
Keeps a-goin’ straight to Hell !
I uster love to go to meetin’
W’en it meant a-meetin’ you —
The bell upon me ’eart a-beatin’
Sittin’ in the corner pew !
Oh ! Jane ! Jane ! Jane !
The bell may ring for me in vain !
You forsake me ! Devil take me !
You’ve to answer for it, Jane !
I’d like to know w’at Sunday’s made for !
Sunday ! called the “ day of rest ” !
It’s not the kind of rest. l’ve prayed for
Since you’ve gone an’ give me best !
Through muggy night an’ meltin’ day-time,
S’elp me ! How I’ve swat an’ swore,
A-thinkin’ of the Sunday playtime,
Kissin’ you be’ind the door !
Oh ! Jane ! Jane ! Jane!
W’en shall I ever smile again!
You’ve bereft me ! Nothin’s left me !
’Eedless, ’ollow-’earted Jane!
S.A.

SATURDAY, MAY 14, 1898.
One-Man-One-Vote.
The ghost of the old war-cry, “ One-man-
one-vote,” rises again to do duty in the Pro-
vincial interest. Almost everything under
the sun can be impressed, by ridiculous
misrepresentation, to do duty in almost any
kind of interest, and in New South Wales
“ One-man-one-vote ” is still a term to con-
jure with. A few years ago, in the days of
the brick-and-mortar suffrage, it was the
loudest war-whoop in the Cornstalk land,
till at last the Dibbs Government abolished
the property suffrage, so far as the Assembly
was concerned, and supplied the country
with some part of the reform it was craving
for. Whereupon the country promptly fired
out the Dibbs Ministry, and the unfinished
reform rests to this day where Dibbs left it.
Now the Provincial party appeals to the
country to rise up and defend its hard-won
privilege, on the ground that Federation will
wreck it utterly and beyond repair, and that
the iron heel of the Fat Man and the yoke of
the brick-and-mortar vote is again about to
be placed on the necks of the masses. In a
recent manifesto the case is set forth thus :
To-day every man in New South Wales stands as far as
legislation will permit on a footing of political equality
with every other man. Each person has a vote, and no
more than one. And a vote at Bourke is of no more
value and no less t han a vote in Sydney. But under the
wretched Convention Bill everything will he changed.
The battle that was fought in New South Wales for years
against privilege and monopoly and close corporation for
the eradication of political inequality will count for no-
thing if the Convention enthusiasts should have their
wav. From one man one political power, there is to be
one man with the political power settled according to the
province in which he may live. What is the use of Conven-
tion advocates talking of One-man-one-vote when under-
neath the clause that grants it is another that renders it
nugatory? In a word, the Bill, instead of preserving
One-man-one-vote, abolishes it. Instead of maintaining
equal political rights it absolutely sweeps them away.
• * * *
All this sounds very convincing on the
surface, and the loud advertising mob which
lives upon shibboleths and war-whoops—
the Rosa and Foran and Tommy Dodd
crowd of crude destructionists—mostly takes
this view. And yet the very first trouble is
that New South Wales isn’t governed on the
principle of One-man-one-vote, and never
has been, and that a vote at Bourke isn’t of
the same value as a vote at Sydney, and the
country isn’t ruled by manhood suffrage, and
the great principle of majority rule is not
being sacrificed because there is no such
thing, and there never was any such thing,
and there is no immediate prospect of there
being such a thing, or anything like it.
* * * *
At present New South Wales is governed
by two Houses of Parliament, which have
an equal right to make laws or reject them.
One House—the ephemeral one which comes
and goes at every election like the brown
leaves of autumn—is elected by the people.
The other one lasts for ever, and its members
hold their billets for life, no matter how much
they may betray their trust, and over it the
people have no lawful control whatever.
Therefore, the alleged Government by One-
man-one-vote goes broke at the very be-
ginning of the argument. There are 187
members of the New South Wales Parlia-
ment, and the people have control over 125
of them. But the 62 that the people don’t
control have power to block the will of the
125 that they do control, and if the 125 say
“ yes” and the 62 nominees answer “no” the
majority doesn’t carry it—the 62 minority,
if it likes to stand firm on its gouty feet,
has its way. The 62 are almost exclusively
men of wealth—it isn’t provided by the
Constitution that they should be, but they
are. Members of the New South Wales
Council being unpaid legislators, it naturally
follows that only men of some affluence can
afford to occupy the posts, and, therefore,
the Upper House is likely to continue to be
a House of Rich Men. And there is no
sign on the furthest horizon of any reform
worth having.
* • * *
The vote of a man at Bourke and the vote
of a man at Sydney are not equal ; it maybe
intended that they should be, but they are
not. The constituencies are of many different
sizes, therefore at last election 3000 voters
at Lismore and Moree had two members,
and 3200 at Marrickville had only one ! This
is a comparatively common case of one man
two votes, or else one man with a vote and
another with a bit of one. As a further dis-
crepancy, Sydney and its immediate en-
virons have a House practically to them-
selves ; the Council being a place where
there are few pickings, no salary, and practi-
cally no prospects, it has to be recruited
mainly from the men in the next street, who
can drop in and reject a Bill any time
without sacrificing their other avocations.
Further, the constituency system and the
residence clauses regularly disfranchise from
five to ten per cent, of the electors—at least
it is commonly so estimated. And, though
every man has a vote Tor half the Legislature,
if he isn’t accidentally disfranchised, and if
the fact is overlooked that his vote may be
worth only half that of the man in the next
electorate, the use of that vote is strictly
limited. He can’t vote for the man he
wants to get in ; only for the man—possibly
an utterly useless lout—who is standing in
his electorate. The majority doesn’t decide ;
if there are 1000 votes polled, and six can-
didates, the winner may be chosen by only
250 electors. The man who represents the
biggest vote doesn’t even get in ; at last
New South Wales election Tiro meson was re-
turned by 329 votes, and Nelson by 333.
But Copeland got 876 votes ; Goodlet, 718 ;
Bowman, 680; Dobbie, 727 ; Taylor, 748 ;
Gilbert, 738 ; Holden, 751 ; Kidd, 828 ;
and so on—and all these men were defeated.
This may be One-man-one-vote in theory,
but when things are so arranged that the
vote might often be as well thrown into
the dust-bin as the ballot-box it isn’t
One-man-one-vote in practice. And, in
addition to all this, things are so arranged
that if 248,000 out of 250,000 electors would
rather see Geo. Reid hanged than in Parlia-
ment, and yet the other 2000, in some small
constituency which is grateful for roads and
culverts conferred upon it by G. Reid at the
public expense, chooses to put him in, there
is no lawful process on earth by which these
248,000 can prevent him having as much say in
governing them as any of the men they have
elected. The Victorian franchise is built the
same way, only Victoria doesn’t even pretend
to have One-man-one-vote, and the Victorian
Council is elective—with the proviso that
only rich landlords are eligible.
* * * *
The Federal Constitution comes very
much nearer to the One-man-one-vote idea
than the present New South Wales
method, despite the vast amount of pride
wasted on the latter by misguided Demo-
crats, and despite the fact that, barring
South Australia, New South Wales has the
most liberal constitution in Australia. The
Federal Convention provides that members of
both Houses shall be paid ; therefore both
Houses are open to men of little or no means,
and can be captured by the Democracy. There
is no property-qualification, therefore the
Democrat won’t be forced to vote for the
Fat Man, as in the Victorian House of 48
Rich Landlords. It is also provided that
no elector shall have more than one vote,
and, in addition, every person who has
NOW OR WHO ACQUIRES IN THE FUTURE A
VOTE FOR THE ASSEMBLY IN New SOUTH
Wales, Victoria, S.A., Tasmania, or
Westralia shall have a vote for both
Houses of the Federal Legislature.
This magnificently liberal proviso (which is
embodied in clauses 30 and 41 of the Federal
Constitution) can only be annulled if the
majority of the voters in general and also the
majority of the voters in three States deli-
berately resolve to deprive themselves of
their privilege, which contingency is beyond
all imagining. Therefore, so far as concerns
the Federal Legislature, the plural vote is
abolished in all the States. And, in addition,
if the Federal franchise wants widening, and
the Federal Senate is the Tory institution
which some alarmists imagine it will be, and
refuses to widen it, the democracy of the
separate States can do the work themselves.
All they have to do is to enlarge their own
State franchises, and the Federal franchise
enlarges automatically.
* » * *
The only point on which the Federal Con-
stitution departs from the principle of One-
man-one-vote is that the representation is
not equal, and one Tasmanian has between
and three times the voting power of one
inhabitant of Victoria or New South Wales.
There are so few Tasmanians that their vot-
ing power isn’t much, after all, and things
are so arranged that the disproportion is
likely to diminish—still, for the present
there is just as bad a disproportion as be-
tween an elector in Lismore, N.S. Wales,
and one in Marrickville, N.S. Wales. But
the people are to have the most absolute
control over both Houses ; the Federal Par-
liament is to be elected entirely by the
masses ; and members of both Houses being
paid, the Democracy can not only elect both
Houses but capture all the seats in both
Houses. The one discrepancy is that the
Democrat in the two smallest provinces has
not quite three times the voting power of the
Democrat in the two biggest ones, while the
fifth province has about its fair proportion.
And, though Labor claims to be a universal
brotherhood, and the horny toiler in one
province extends his most cordial sympathy
and confidence to the horny toiler in the
State across the border, yet in this particular
case he is informed that it is his duty to see
the other toiler darned first. On the other
hand, the kind of One-man-one-vote which
exists in New South Wales is that one-
half the legislative authority rests in
practice with the nominee Fat Man;
that 62 nominated Fat Men holding
their billets for life can overrule (if
they choose to do so) 125 popular repre-
sentatives ; and that, in theory, every man
has half a vote, or a vote for one-half the
Legislature—only by reason of the unequal
constituencies some men have only a quarter
of a vote. In actual voting power Salo-
mons, M.L.C. (who represents nobody),
counts for quite as much as Carruthers,
M.L.A. (who represents 1380 St. George
electors), and Salomons has his own vote as
an ordinary citizen in addition. Therefore,
the system in New South Wales is some men
J-vote and the rest of the men except-
ing the 62 ancient fossils who equal anything
up to 1380 votes each. The Federal Consti-
tution comes far nearer to One-man-one-vote
than this, and because The Bulletin has been
for One-man-one-vote first and last and all
the time, therefore it is for the Federal Con-
stitution, as being the biggest single step in
that direction ever yet heard of in Australia.
And though there is a discrepancy in the re-
presentation it must be remembered that
the Democrats of Victoria and New South
Wales (the two provinces which have less
than their share of the voting power) will be
making concession only to their fellow-
Democrats of Westralia and Tasmania, and
making far less concession to them than they
now do to the Fat Men of their own pro-
vinces. And The Bulletin would rather
trust the horny-handed Democrat of Cool-
gardie than Solomon Hyam, M.L.C., of Syd-
ney, or Cornelius Job Ham, M.L.C., of
Melbourne, any day.
Bounties.
The reasons assigned by the small anti-Federal
party in Sydney for the alleged faith that is in
it change with remarkable rapidity. Jack
Want’s wonderful invention about Victoria
charging duties upon N.S.W. coal and shutting
it out of the province has been dropped ; and
the impudent fiction of Hughes, M.L.A.,
about the Federal Senate being practically
free from dissolution has fallen out of sight ;
and the fiction about “ Braddon’s Biot ”
having committed the Commonwealth to raise
an enormous customs revenue, or any customs
revenue at all, having worn threadbare, is
being gradually abandoned. But last week
one Smith, an obscure member of the Reid
Government, and the Sydney Telegraph
started a perfectly new ghost on the subject of
bounties, and raged at large in a fearsome and
irresponsible fashion that was deafening to
witness.
* • • •
To start at the beginning, a bounty is a grant
given by the State to aid or encourage some
industry—generally a new industry. The
Victorian export butter business was created by
the policy of the Gillies Government in grant-
ing a bonus of so much per lb. on all first-class
butter exported, and a few other undertakings
have been assisted, at various times and in
various provinces, after the same fashion.
Bounties never attained to any size in any part
of Australia, but it is casually mentioned in the
Convention Bill that, after 30th June, 1898,
the power of the States to grant bounties at all,
except with the consent of the Federal Govern-
ment, shall absolutely cease. The sudden roar
of the Telegraph on this very small and thin
subject is interesting :
What he (Mr. Wish) had to deal with was the possibility
of N.S. Wales being indirectly taxed to subsidise the pro-
duction of Victorian coal. The Bill provides for this by
giving Victoria the right to squander her funds in
bounties. ... A bounty on Gippsland coal would, so far
as shutting the N.S. Wales article out of Victoria, have
just the same effect as a customs-tax. The* jiounty-
fed coal of Victoria might, if of good enough ’ quality,
be sold in Sydney for less than the product of the un-
subsidised mines in N.S. Wales. . . . This applies to
every commodity as well as to coal, but the bounty
hunger of the Victorian collieries serves to give their
ease a special prominence. It is only by State coddling
that the Gippsland mines have been developed to their
present stage. That coddling may be continued under
Federation either by differential railway rates or by a
bounty, if the latter is agreed upon by the Government
of Victoria before 30th June next. ... It only requires
the consent of the Federal Legislature, in which N.S.
Wales could easily be out-voted, to enable Victoria at any
time to grant any bounty she pleases to coal or any
other product. . . . Mr. YVisk stated that a bounty given
by the Commonwealth would have to apply to all States
alike. No doubt it would. — Telegram, May h.
» * * •
Another, and quite a different howl, from the
same paper :
Now the word “ promised ” is of special significance.
It is not necessary that the Victorian Parliament should
have ratified such promises. The Ministerial undertaking
is itself sufficient, or, as the Bill puts it, any “ AGREE-
MENT for any such bounty lawfully made by or under
the authority of the Government of any State shall be
taken to be good ” if made before June 30 next. But, if
Victoria were chargeable with those payments, she would,
at any rate, be only impoverishing her own people for the
purpose. That, however, is very far from being the inter-
pretation placed upon the Bill. It is the Commonwealth
which pays them, and the payments must be “ treated as
Federal expenditure.” This means simply that N.S.
Wales producers must contribute a larger sum annually
towards the payment of those bounties in Victoria than
the Victorians themselves, although their money will be
used in driving the N.S. Wales producers out of the
market. This is, indeed, a marvellous arrangement.
What is Victoria doing at the present time ? On this
point, Mr. Sydney Smith is in a position to enlighten us.
It is not only a question of unexpended grants, though
they are considerable. Nor yet a question of the coal
bonus, though that has reached, we believe, twice the
sum of £45,967 actually paid from the Treasury to the
Railway Commissioners during the past four years, and
will be much larger in years to come. It appears the
Victorian Government contemplate “promises” of some
£60,000 to new wineries, and other amounts, and who
can tell what sums may not be appropriated before that
dividing line, the 30th day of June next, is reached? All
this is to be “ Federal expenditure,” forsooth ! Was any
more preposterous arrangement ever entered into?—
Telegraph, May 6.
* * * •
This self-convicted perjurer, therefore, states
on May 4 that Victoria has the right to squander
her funds in bounties, and on May 6 that it is
the funds of the Commonwealth that are going
to be squandered. This is an awful discrepancy
in the fable, and badly wants explanation.
• * • *
Despite all this amazing row there is no
bounty on Victorian coal, and there never has
been one. All the tale about the alleged bounty
is invention from the first letter to the last.
Also Victoria doesn’t levy a duty on coal, and
never has done so. But both the N.S. Wales
and Victorian railways carry coal at the same
low rate—a rate which the N.S.W. railway
authorities regard as profitable, while the Vic.
railway authorities hold a different opinion.
At all events the Vic. Treasury pays the rail-
way department the amount of the estimated
loss, and the N.S.W. one doesn’t, therefore if
there is a loss it is included in the railway
shortage in N.S.W. and in the ordinary deficit
in Victoria. The results to the people and to
the coal-owners are exactly the same in each
case, and if there is a bounty on coal in Vic-
toria there is exactly the same bounty in
N.S.W"., and the whole affair is as broad as it
is long. The statement that there has been a
bonus in the past, and the other statement that
it will be much larger in the future, are both
the same clumsy fable, and there is nothing in
either of them.
* » * •
The announcement that the Convention Bill
“gives Victoria the right to squander her
funds in bounties ” is, no doubt, partly true.
It is also true that the Bill gives exactly the
same right to N.S.W., Tasmania, S.A., and
Westralia, though the Foreign-trade organ
doesn’t mention this. But the contradictory
statement that it is the Federal Government
which will have to pay these bounties, so that
Victoria won’t be squandering “ her ” funds at
all, is absolutely false, and the further state-
ment that the Federal Government must
recognise bounties which are not legally
granted, but merely promised on the unsup-
ported assertion of the Ministry, has no basis
at all. As the awful daily misrepresenter states,
“the word ‘promised’ is of special signifi-
cance.” But there is no such word or anything
like it in the Bill. The exact words of the Bill
are these :
86. On the establishment of the Commonwealth the
collection and control of the duties of customs and of
excise, and the control of the payment of bounties, shall
pass to the Executive Government of the Commonwealth.
90. On the imposition of uniform duties of customs the
power of The Parliament to impose duties of customs and
of excise and to grant bounties on the production or
export of goods shall become exclusive.
On the imposition of uniform duties of customs all laws
of the several States imposing duties of customs or of
excise, or offering bounties on the production or export
of goods, shall cease to have effect; but any grant of or
agreement for any such bounty lawfully made by or
under the authority of the Government of any State shall
be taken to be good if made before 30th June, 1898, ana
not otherwise.
91. Nothing in this Constitution prohibits any State
from granting any aid to or bounty on mining for gold,
silver, or other metals, nor from granting, with the con-
sent of both Houses of The Parliament of the Common-
wealth expressed by resolution, any aid to or bounty on
the production or export of goods.
It is also expressly stated that “ The Parlia-
ment ” means the Commonwealth Parliament
only.
« * * *
Therefore, in the first place, there is not a
single word about the Federal Treasury paying
bounties previously granted by the States ; it
is merely provided that the Federal Executive
shall control bounties in general. Of course
clause 86 doesn’t say that the Federal Govern-
ment shall not pay them, and it also quite omits
to state that the editor of the Telegraph shall
not be burnt alive without trial by Police-
Magistrate Smithers. If the absence of the
one negative means that the Federal Govern-
ment is to pay the bounties, then the absence
of the other proves as conclusively that
the D.T. editor may bo burnt by Smithers,
P. M. There are only two or three very small
bounties existing in Australia, and they all
expire within a year or two, and no Legis-
lature is making any present effort to pro-
vide for their renewal. The statement that
new bounties may be granted before June
30 on the mere promise of any Ministry is a
glaring falsehood ; the Commonwealth Consti-
tution distinctly provides that they must be
made or granted “ lawfully ” (no such word as
“ promised ” appears at all), and the only lawful
way of doing it is by drafting a Bill, and reading
it three times in the Assembly and three times
in the Legislative Council, and putting it through


committee in both Houses, and getting the
Governor’s assent. Tasmania has closed its
Parliament till June 7; South Australia to
June 23; and Victoria has just adjourned its
Legislature till May 31, with promise of a fur-
ther adjournment till about the middle of June.
This doesn’t look at all as if they were rushing
to pass bounty laws before June 30. And if
they did “lawfully ” pass any such “grants or
agreements” after N.S. Wales had accepted
the Federal Constitution, and it was considered
that they were thereby taking an unfair ad-
vantage, the N.S. Wales Parliament would still
have abundance of time to block the Bill
unless it was too busy passing bounty laws itself.
* • • •
The Federal Parliament, in which N.S.
Wales has the largest vote, may, of course, grant
bounties if it pleases, but they will apply equally
all over the Commonwealth, and come out of
the common funds with the common consent.
Further, the Act specially reserves to N.S.
Wales, as to all the other provinces, the right to
pay bounties to assist mining for gold, silver,
copper, lead, iron, tin, antimony, bismuth,
platinum, quicksilver, and other metals.
Victoria has the same right, of course,
but as it has practically no known metal
deposits apart from gold, silver, tin, and a trace
of antimony, N.S. Wales is here a long way
ahead. N.S. Wales, Victoria, and all the other
provinces have exactly the same right to grant
bounties at their own expense after next June—
if they can get the permission of the Federal
Parliament. Only N.S.W., with 31 votes, has a
rather better show to get that permission than
Victoria with only 28. Therefore, there is a
larger risk of the bounty-fed coal of Newcastle
wrecking the Victorian industry than the other
way about. The N.S. Wales and Victorian
coal-mines have been coddled in exactly the
same way—by low railway rates, only N.S.
Wales has coddled rather more than Victoria
by also spending vast sums on making harbours
and erecting coal-shoots, &c. Both provinces,
and the other three as well, retain equally the
right to go on coddling by low railway rates,
only N.S.W. preserves the right to coddle over
four times as much territory as its neighbor.
The horrible fact sticks out conspicuously that
N.S.W. has reserved power to put on a coal
bounty between now and 30th June of Is. or
2s. 6d. per ton or more, and thereby blot out
the Vic. industry altogether. And, as to
N.S. W. being “easily out-voted ” in the Federal
Parliament, Victoria, with three less members,
is still more easily outvoted. Anyhow,
N.S. W., having less than half the population
of the Commonwealth, must be capable of being
out-voted by a combination of the other four
States, and every individual State must be in
the same situation. The only way to avoid
that is not to federate at all. Yet, strangely
enough, the Telegraph says it wants to
federate.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Infamous Steal.
Syndicate grab seems to have reached an abso-
lutely transcendental state —the fourth plane, in
fact—in the just-ratified land transaction in
British Papua, the Legislative Council (ap-
pointed by Governor M'Gregor) of that peaceful
country duly consenting. A company named
the British New Guinea Syndicate has acquired
the fee simple of 250,000 acres (390 square
miles) of land in Papua at 2s. per acre, and,
within eight years, a further sum of 2s. per
acre must be spent on improvements. The
syndicate has, during eight years, the first re-
fusal of all land declared available for occupa-
tion, and if, at the end of that period, 250,000
acres have not been thrown open, which,
on account of the dense settlement and
distinctly regulated land-ownership of the
aboriginals, is highly probable, this glorious
privilege runs on till the time the syndicate is
gorged. Even then it will still be allowed
to purchase further lands in the open market.
It intends to tackle anything, from mission-
work to rubber-getting, and has proposed a
scheme the comprehensiveness of which leaves
nothing to be desired. It can purchase agri-
cultural, pastoral, timbered or mineral lands
(at 2s. an acre). It can sit down at its ease and
wait till somebody drops on to a choice morsel
of property, and then step in and claim it (at 2s.
an acre). The boss syndicators will watch
with a fatherly eye over the doings of the
ragged, fever-racked prospector, and, should
that deluded individual find, by some mishap,
a great gold-field, they will arise and claim it
(at 2s. an acre). They encourage with a bene-
volent smile the struggles of the selec-
tor near the coast, the cocoanut-planter,
the coffee-grower, the sugar-grower and
when the time has come they will choose
the best harbor or river frontages and all the
rich alluvial flats, and claim them (at 2s. an
acre). The pioneer may find vast rubber
forests, or coal deposits, and the syndi-
cate solemnly arise and claim them too
(at 2s. an acre). In fact, the syndicate does
absolutely nothing; promises nothing, pays
next to nothing, and collars the lot. It only
binds its honor as a syndicate, in a many-
worded, pompous style (as per B.N.G. Govt.
Gazette) to thoroughly, carefully and com-
prehensively squeeze revenue out of anything
and everything payable.. It will lend money
to settlers and obtain subsidies from the Govt,
for every adult introduced, borrow on deben-
tures, buy, sell, breed, grow, crush, refrigerate
(to keep the roast missionary in the hot weather),
build, mine, etc., etc. In short, the boss
grabbers assure whomsoever it may concern, that
what they don’t touch is worth leaving alone.
And this incredibly immoral, economically
idiotic, and politically criminal proposal is com-
fortably ratified by a more or less irresponsible
Imperial Governor “with the advice and con-
sent of the Legislative Council of 8.N.G.”
(appointed by the Governor aforesaid). The
arrangements, of course, date from stinkful
Brisbane, but apparently the contributing Aus-
tralian provinces, bar a few politicians, knew
nothing of this gigantic steal till it was calmly
proclaimed as “ratified.” Magnificent! The
broad wholesaleness of the transaction stamps
it as facile, princeps amongsimilar undertakings;
but the joyful irresponsibility, the utter abso-
lution from all bodily and financial risk to the
shareholders ! are—no, language is not equal to
it! What does Australia propose to do about
this infamous steal ?
The Four*" Wolves.”
There are no professed anti-Federalists now
in New South Wales. There were some before
the Convention election, and they nominated a
ticket of their own, and the Five Stupid Men
who formed about the tail of the list when the
numbers went up represented that faith, and
went to the poll with the cry that the whole
idea had to be Put Down. Since then the
Convention has sat, and—so the old anti-
Federal “push” asserts —it has produced a
ruinously bad scheme, and the other four pro-
vinces Dave conspired to swindle and rob and
defraud New South Wales. And the alleged
failure and conspiracy and fraud, instead of
making the anti-Federal push more anti-
Federal than ever, has resulted in its unani-
mous conversion. Its members all allege now
that they want Federation, only of a better
kind than the present sample; and promise
that if the people will only reject this Bill and
throw away this opportunity, they (the breth-
ren of the same old push) will labor incessantly
to get another and a better one. It is a miser-
ably thin pretence, anyhow. The tale of the
Want crowd is to the effect that the other four
provinces are “ wolves ” —and that it (the said
crowd) desires to federate with the wolves. Also,
that the other provinces are leagued together
to rob N.S.W. —and, at the same time, the
firm of Want and co. adds that it has a great
yearning to federate with the robbers, and
anyone who says that it doesn’t desire to
federate with the robbers is a liar. Then,
again, the same old Cabbage-tree Mob claims
that what it wants is representation according
to population in both Houses, in which case the
alleged league of the four other provinces, hav-
ing the larger population, would always have a
majority as against N.S.W. Next it demands
that there should be a simple process under
which the Constitution—now said to be “ cast-
iron ” —could be altered by a mere majority of
the people instead of a majority both of people
and provinces being required; and, in that
case, the alleged conspiracy of the other four
provinces could alter the Constitution as it
darned well pleased. The Want and Heydon
crowd asserts that the other provinces have
dark designs to seize the Riverina and its trade,
the western country and its trade, the rivers,
the imaginary N.S.W. surplus, and the capital,
and still it adds that it is a sincere believer
in Federation, and will leave no stone unturned
to bring about a union with the pirates
who, it says, are planning this tremendous
steal. Its one great desire is to place the power
of altering the Constitution in the hands of a
bare majority, so that the league of the other
four provinces can carry any alterations that
are necessary for the steal —the Want crowd
still solemnly alleging that the league exists
and that the steal is contemplated. Which
leaves the three alternatives—that the Cabbage-
Tree Mob is an Ananias, or a great fool, or a
traitor to its country. Either it doesn’t believe
that the other provinces are scheming to rob
N.S. Wales, and so is passing a miserable fic-
tion and slander on the people; or it doesn’t
want to federate as it says it does, in which
case it is also guilty of gross untruth ; or it
believes the other provinces are planning to rob
N.S. Wales, and it does want to federate all the
same, in which case it is a lunatic, or it has sold
itself to the alleged enemy. Its plea that it
only wants to federate with the four alleged
robbers “on equitable terms ” is an absurdity
—the four robbers have the majority of popula-
tion, and would have the biggest vote, and,
if they were the unprincipled crowd which the
WANT gang affects to believe they are, they
would alter the terms afterwards to suit them-
selves, and go right on with the robbery. The
Bulletin’s idea is that the Cabbage-Tree Mob
is merely a rather unscrupulous and rather
stupid provincial bigotthat it is against
Federation on any terms ; that it invents its
facts to make the people reject the present Bill ;
that it calls itself an advocate of Federation and
promises to start the cause afresh if the people
will only lose this opportunity, because that is
the only way to make them sacrifice a bone for
a shadow ; and that if this opportunity is lost
N.S.W. will never get another so long as the
firm of Want, Heydon and co. can prevent it.
4.
The Two Horrors.
The latest favouriteargument of theanti-Federal
crowd is a table showing how, under certain
incredible circumstances, the Federation Bill
allows a very small minority to override a very
large majority. Supposing an amendment of
the Constitution is passed by both, Houses, it
must next be assented to by a majority of the
States and by a majority of the persons voting.
And supposing the vote was like this:
For Against
Amendment. Amendment.
N.S.W ... 100,000 ... 1
Victoria ... 100,000 ... 1
Tasmania ... 1,000 ... 1,001
S.A. ... 1,000 ... 1,001
W.A. ... 1,000 ' ... 1,001
203,000 3,005
In these truly painful circumstances there
would be 203,000 votes for the amendment
whatever it might be (call it the proposal to
boil Jack Want or anything else), and only
3005 against it, and yet because three States
were against it by a very small majority
the amendment would be lost. And the
Want-Tommy Dodd crowd asks the electors
of N.S.W., as a free people, inhabiting a coun-
try where the majority always rules, and where
one man’s vote is of the same value as that of
another man, to resent this proposed outrage,
and drop the Federation Bill into the ash-
barrel. And yet, strangely enough, in N.S.W.,
with one man one vote, and with the rule of the
majority fully recognised, there is exactly the
same danger of a small minority running the
show. For instance, there are 125 electorates
in N.S.W. with, on an average, 2000 voters in
each electorate. And there is nothing to pre-
vent such a thing as this happening : In each
one of 95 electorates only 241 people vote; 80
for the Labor candidate, 80 for Protection,
for Freetrade ; result, C 5 Free
returned. In each one of 30 other electorates
2000 people vote ; 1999 for Protection, and 1 for
Freetrade ; result, 30 Protectionists re-
turned. Total : 7600 Labor votes have no
representatives at all ; 67,570 Protectionist
voters have 30 representatives ; 7725 Freetrade
voters have 95 representatives. Ihus there is
in N.S.W., with one-man-one-vote and majority
rule and all the other advantages, the horrible
possibility that, simply through the constitu-
ency system, 7725 voters might have over
three-fourths of the power in the Assembly,
while over 75,000 had less than one-fourth
among them. Of course these things have
never yet happened, but they are about as liable
to happen at any moment as the other calcula-
tion. And, supposing the Labor-party dropped
out, and in each of 100 constituencies two
people voted for Protection and only one for
Freetrade—these 100 constituencies being par-
ticularly fast asleep at the moment; and in the
other 25 there were 1999 votes for Freetrade
and only one for Protection, then N.S.W.
would run against the still more awful
fact that 225 votes returned 100 Protec-
tionist members, while 50,075 votes only
returned 25 Freetrade members. And then
that vast Protectionist majority might
swamp the Legislative Council with 140
nominees of its own party, and, having
thus the command of both Houses, it could put
a duty of 15,000 per cent, on everything, and
make it impossible for the people to take it off
again by the simple process of disfranchising
everybody except Dibbs and O’Sullivan. The
dreadful thing which might happen to the
Commonwealth on paper in the way of minority-
rule isn’t a circumstance to the dreadful things
that might happen to N.S.W. on paper any
day. And the grief of the Want-Lyne-Tommy
Dodd crowd over the horror that may happen
to United Australia in the future looks a purely
bogus emotion when compared with its dead
silence about the worse horror that is quite as
liable to happen to disunited N.S.W. in the
present.
“Braddon’s Blot.”
The grave objection to the amendment, in my judgment,
is that it fixes for all time an arithmetical proportion be-
tween annual expenditure and the surplus to be returned
to the States. It is quite conceivable that a period of,
say, 25 years in the future history of Australia, with the
certainty of great developments that inevitably await us
when federated, may necessitate an annual expenditure
of three or four millions. Assuming the latter figure to
be the correct amount, the federal treasurer would have
to raise £16,000,000 from a customs and excise, and return
to the States £12,000,000, a sum, probably, far in excess
of their necessities.— John Henry, Tasmanian Convention
Delegate.
Of the net revenue of the Commonwealth from duties
of customs and excise, not more than one-fourth shall be
applied annually by the Commonwealth towards its ex-
penditure. The balance shall, in accordance with this
Constitution, be paid to the several States, or applied
towards the payment of interest on debts of the several
States taken over by the Commonwealth. — Braddon’s
famous clause (No. 87) of the Convention Bill.
In all the row and clamor about the Convention
Bill, the above clause has probably done more
to advertise the stupidity of people in general
than any other. The clause itself is practi-
cally meaningless. It was doubtless meant
to have a definite meaning, but Braddon’s
clause was badly devised, and didn’t achieve
his purpose or any other purpose. It doesn’t
commit the. Commonwealth to a high or a
low tariff, or any tariff at all. It doesn’t
compel the Commonwealth to pay all its ex-
penses, or any part of them, out of the fourth of
the customs and excise which it is permitted to
retain. Also, it doesn’t compel it to spend
one fourth of the customs and excise, or one-
tenth or one-thousandth, or any other pro-
portion. Almost everybody, including stupid
old John Henry, as quoted, and the
Daily Telegraph, and Lyne, and many
other people who ought to know better, keep
on asserting that the Federal Government is
pledged to pay all its ex.’s out of one-fourth of
the customs and excise, and therefore, if its
ex.’s amount to £2,000,000 a year, it must
raise ,£8,000,000 by a tariff that is protectionist
and prohibitive, and also impossible, and many
other things as well; and that it must return
more money than the States require, and so on.
And yet the clause is simple enough to any one
who can read ordinary print. If the Com-
monwealth’s expenses (which will consist
almost wholly of liabilities formerly paid by
the States) equal £2,000,000 a year, it may
raise £8,000,000 by customs and excise, and
take its two millions out of that, and return
the other six to the States. Or it may take one
million out of it, and pay the other out of direct
taxation, and return £7,000,000 to the States.
Or it may raise £4,000,000 by customs and
excise, and take one million out of it, and raise
the balance by income or land-tax, poll-tax,
probate (or stamp-duty, or anything else. It
may abolish customs and excise altogether, and
pay all its ex.’s out of other forms of taxation.
It may raise £10,000,000 in customs and excise,
and return it all to the States, and pay its own
costs by running a Federal drink and tobacco
monopoly. In fact, Braddon’s clause leaves
everything almost entirely where it was before
—except that it will make the Federal Govern-
ment much more anxious to go in for direct
taxation than for customs and excise, because
if it raises £1,000,000 by direct taxation, it can
keep the whole of it, while if it raises
£1,000,000 by a tariff, it can only keep
£250,000 out of it. Considering how many
public men have climbed on to platforms and
explained this harmless and meaningless clause
every way except the .simple and obvious
right way, there must be an incredible number
of muddlers or wilful deceivers in Australian
politics. Braddon’s clause is a “ Blot ” simply
because it is useless, and a waste of words, and
a monument to a fine old English gentleman
who thought he could draft a clause but
couldn’t. But it will probably be amended out
of the Constitution at an early date, though
there is really very little reason why any one
should trouble to shift the harmless object.
The Fat Man’s Idea.
The Melbourne Chamber of Commerce had an
idea last week. What it desires is that the
British or other foreign person who invests
money in Victoria shall be exempted from
income-tax, and that the burden shall be
dumped down exclusively on the Victorian
investor. The latter, being only a beastly
colonial, is of no account, even in his own
country, and it is quite obvious, in the opinion
of the Chamber of Commerce, that he should
stand all the racket. The Chamber reported
seriously that the British investor is very much
annoyed at having to pay income-tax like any-
body else, and that capital is really “ going to
leave the country” this time. Also, a great
many people who would otherwise condescend
to send money here, and carry away the inter-
est, and spend it in England, are so much hurt
that they intend to stay away. As matters
stand, the British investor in Victoria escapes
customs, excise, and other duties, and as he
invests mostly in mortgages and city properties
he also dodges the land-tax very unanimously.
Likewise the local holder of State securities
pays income-tax on his dividends, while the
foreign one doesn’t. Except in this one
matter of the income-tax on his invest-
ments other than those in State securities,
the absentee has the blessed privilege of know-
ing that the local person pays the whole
expense of maintaining police and soldiers to
defend his property, building railways to im-
prove it, running after and arresting and trying
the man who injures it, supporting him in gaol
after conviction, paying the British squadron
which prevents it being looted by the Russian
or the Little Brown Heathen, and otherwise
being a father and a protector and a big brother
to the Bull absentee. And the Melbourne
Chamber of Commerce is of opinion that if the
one miserably small contribution which Bull
does pay was abolished, and all these things
were done for him absolutely gratis, instead of
very nearly gratis, he would be quite content,
and wouldn’t want anything more. He wouldn’t
ask that the Government should guarantee his
mortgage interest, or throw in free drinks ; all
he wants is that the absentee shall be wholly
free from taxation, and that the degraded Vic-
torian shall pay everything. It is alleged that
a deputation is going to wait on Premier
Turner in the matter. If such a deputation
had waited on old Parkks in his fighting days
it would have come down the stair head first,
and Parkes would afterwards have opened the
back door and thrown its ear into the ash-
barrel.
Letters of gold for recent W.A. Supreme
Court pronouncement (by Judge Hensman)
that the sly-grog informer who himself induces
breach of the law should be considered as an
accomplice, and consequently an unreliable wit-
ness, and that the informer-system generally
—besides being intrinsically infamous —is en-
tirely opposed to the intention and spirit of the
law. Not much cogitation is needed to see that
the putting of one man on to seduce another
into illegally providing him with a glass of
grog is equivalent exactly, in principle, to the
(say) employment of an attractive woman to
fascinate a “ right-thinking,” “ GoD-fearing ”
father of a family into his “ fall.” “ Lead us
not into temptation ” should be the concrete
letter of the law instead of merely the supposed
vaguely-pervading “ spirit.”
Some little time ago N.S.W. Legislature
passed a Coal Mines Act which provided, among
other things, that all coal coming from a mine
must be weighed, so that the men may be paid
for the exact amount produced. This reading
of the Act has been recently confirmed by the
N.S.W. courts. At present, not one mine-
owner in the province obeys the law. The
mine-owning class not only defies it openly,
but threatens the men with wholesale dis-
missal and the closure of all the mines in
N.S. W. if they attempt to have it enforced. A
still more interesting feature of the case is
that the Government makes no effort to enforce
the law, or to punish the individuals who have
thus openly conspired together to break it, and
who threaten other people with pains and
penalties if they don’t connive at the conspiracy
and the breakage ? By what possible right do
Reid and Sunday BRUNKERand their colleagues
unmake the laws of the people’s Parliament at
their own pleasure ?
In the Know ” writes as follows :
You, and a number of your correspondents who have
been writing on “blackmailing” by horse-owners of the
drawers of tickets in Tattersall’s sweeps, appear to
entirely lose sight of the fact that in the majority of cases
it is the drawer who approaches the owner with an offer
and not vice versa. And, in the great majority of cases, it
is in the interest of the drawer to so approach the owner.
I’ll tell you why. Jones, we’ll say, has a horse which re-
repeated time tests has shown him can cut out a mile in
1.42 with Bst. up. This is good enough to win at nearly
every suburban meeting. However, Jones want to make
assurance doubly sure, so he runs three or four “byes”
and gets the weight down a stone, and, providing the
horse keepswell, he has then something fast enough to win
in the very best of company. But he wants more than
the mere stake when he lets loose his carefully-bottled-
up racer in a race on which there is a sweep, and if the
drawer comes along and lays him a third of the prize, his
horse tries, and in all probability wins. If, however, the
drawer makes no advances the prad is scratched, and
these tactics continue race after race until a drawer does
come along with a suitable offer. This applies to nearly
every race in Australia, with the exception of the Mel-
bourne and Caulfield Cups, and I have known owners
reputed to be millionaires who “ wait for a bit of the
sweeps.” Patrons of Tatt’s consultations may take it as a
general rule for their guidance that owners will not run
without douceur from the sweep drawer. Owners who
race for pure sport may be counted on your thumbs.
Of course it is often the drawer who
“ approaches ” the owner, because he knows
that he won’t get a run for his money if he
doesn’t. But if sweeps were not drawn till the
day of the race then he couldn’t approach the
owner, and the owner couldn’t approach him.
Therefore the kind of owner whq enters his
horse on the chance of blackmailing the drawer
would immediately become extinct, and the
only owner left in the race would be the one
who was willing to run his animal without the
blackmail. Consequently, if a man drew a
horse under the proposed new system he
would have just as good a chance of winning
the whole money as he now has. of winning a
part of it. Also, in quite a number of cases
where the betting public don’t get a run for
their money because the horse is scratched at
the last moment, the scratching arises through
the ticket-holder’s refusal to be blackmailed,
and so a considerable part of another very large
stink arises through Tattersall’s present
system.
