For some unaccountable reason or other
the imported British company in Australia mostly
yearns after plays that are sodden with grief and
moanful to their inmost soul. Therefore, when
two imported companies put up “Moths” and
“ Ben-My-Chree ’’ on the same night, and a sad,
wet night at that, it was quite in keeping with
the tearful cussedness of things in general.
“ Moths ” is grief all through, barring a cheerful
remark made by a dying man at the end. It is
the class of play which is considered artistic
mainly because the characters are mostly be-
spangled with titles, and the scenes are laid in
expensive drawing-rooms, and the evening-dress
Eeople utter cheap cynicisms (not the brilliant
ind which Oscar Wilde used to grind out of the
mill of his intelligence, but the common sort at
three a penny, and a ticket which gives you a
chance of winning a picture of a dog given in if
you take 12). Apart from this gilding, it is a story
of how a woman urges her saint-like daughter into
marriage with a brutal Russian ; and the Russian
Bmacks his saint often and hard ; and keeps his
mistress in the same house with her ; and he also
insists on the wife receiving that magnificent
Flossie as a friend and a sister. And when she
refuses she is exiled to a lonely castle on a lonely
frozen plain, where she droops by the fire
through a long, sad act, while the snow falls
outside because it has nothing else to do, and the
wind, not being otherwise employed, howls like a
lost soul among the pines. The man she should
have married breaks in and asks her to fly with
him, but the pallid saint (aged 22) replies that she
will live out her life in frozen purity, and hear the
wind shrieking on the plains through the slow
procession of the years, and if her lawful Russian
comes round to her place of exile to spank her
again he will find her there waiting in the dignity
of her spotless innocence to be spanked as per
contract. The “ Moths ” heroine is painfully
orthodox. Having been induced by false pre-
tences to lease her body and soul to the Russian
horror, a logical brand of religion would make her
break her marriage vow rather than let that bright
particular ruffian paw her flesh and her character
any longer—but she isn’t built that way. So the
sickly tale comes to a sickly conclusion, and the
wind wailing over the frozen wastes of Boland
says “Rats” in a minor key, or words to that
effect.
* * • *
“ Moths ” started its depressing career on
a wet and windy Hospital Saturday, in the pre-
sence of an audience that was largely absent on
account of the weather. Excepting Miss Gethyn
Darragh, who has the one pleasant part,
and plays it remarkably well, there is no
cheerful person in it. Miss Myra Kemble,
shunted at the last moment into the character of
Lady Dolly, is a clever and finished actress, as
she always is, but on the first night she was neces-
sarily very much at sea in her dialogue. Cart-
wright has flashes of absolute brilliance, and also
flashes of the other fellow, and the rest of him is
respectable, anyhow. Miss Beatrice Lamb can’t
possibly look the ingenue of only 18, though she
does the saintly, passionless, frozen heroine well
enough. Only her heroine is more a tall, holy
statue than a real meat woman. Also, on the
first night, when she went out and turned pale
with powder at the prospect of wedding her coarse
Russian, she turned altogether too pale and came
back looking like a girl with lib. of flour on her
visage. Arthur Styan, Thursby, and Miss Gwen
Dallas are three satisfactory characters, and that
about sums up the crowd. The opening of
“ Moths ” left an impression of a sad, unhealthy
play joylessly slumped on in the wet amid many
adverse circumstances, and The Bulletin de-
parted in sorrow.
** * *
“Ben-My-Chree,” another drama of the
strange little Isle of Man, where a primitive
race lives principally by fishing and growing the
tailless cat, should prove the biggest success of
the Barrett season. There is no single character
in the new drama who can compare with old Pub-
lican Cregeen, of the “Manxman,” but it is
stronger throughout ; also, like most of Barrett’s
productions, it is full of grief and misery, and dead
body and loud wail and tears. The bishop’s scape-
grace son brings disgrace on his golden-haired
cousin and sweetheart, one particularly pleasant
summer night, through taking a short cut out of
her bedroom-window at a late hour, and her
brother is killed, on a beautiful afternoon, while
remonstrating with him, with a large weapon, on
the damage done to the honor of the family. This
brother refuses to remain buried ; he is planted
miserably, deep down in the sea, to be prawn-bait
and society for the conger-eel; but his per-
sistent coming to the surface in the moon-
light, and his determined piling of himself
on the beach on a bright summer morning,
draw attention, and there is an inquest.
Then a weeping bishop sentences his son to be an
absolutely speechless outcast on the most lonesome
part of the island ; and the restless dead brother
is buried ; and the social leper departs to catch
fish and go slowly mad by himself on the shore ;
and the golden-haired cousin is ejected by her
father, and she and the good old bishop break
their hearts together. The only change in their
lives is when the Governor calls and makes brutal
remarks about her character. Finally, after several
beautiful sunsets and about the most realistic
thunderstorm this paper has yet seen, the forlorn
girl falls down dead while publicly taking oath
that she is not the Tottie she is commonly sup-
posed ; and the outcast son, who has returned to
help swear to her innocence, is hauled away to be
executed for breaking silence. The poor old
bishop is left tottering on two thin legs through
his diocese, but the interesting characters perish
grievously after being miserable all their days,
and now the tailless local cat steps lightly on their
graves.
* * * *
The processes of law in Man 150 years ago
were peculiar, but, anyhow, Hall Caine swears to
the correctness of his version, so that may pass.
Even now, Man is a primitive spot where the
statutes are kept in a carpet-bag under the Go-
vernor’s bed ; and the curious little tribe with the
three legs and the abbreviated feline is a proud,
exclusive race little understood by outsiders.
“ Ben-My-Chree ” is superbly mounted ; there is
a lot of splendid weather painted on the back-
cloth, and it leaves an impression that Man is all
tremendous mountain, and blue sea, and red sun-
set. It is full of action, which is some alleviation
of its grief; the average grief-stricken play is apt
to consist too exclusively of a dying girl moaning
piteously in a chair by a dying fire. Barrett’s
Dan Mylrea is probably the best thing he has yet
done ; though old Hommey Begg, represented by
Horace Hodges, and Ambrose Manning’s Davy,
both lay over the hero in an artistic sense. Old
Hommey Begg, in particular, is a quaint, comedy
ancient who deserves to be handed down to
history and held in reverence by many genera-
tions. After due consideration of Ewan Mylrea
this paper is inclined to think that his death was
necessary and advisable, and that the Manx jury
should have found a verdict that deceased was
justifiably slain by reason of his hat being a blot
on the picture. Miss Maud Jeffries keeps her
place reasonably well in public estimation by her
rendering of Mona Mylrea ; and the only other
nows is that “Othello” is to be staged on Satur-
day, and still another baleful tragedy, “ Vir-
ginius,” is due on Wednesday following.
** * #
Bland Holt has at last been persuaded to
shift the weary “Cotton King,” and a “Life of
Pleasure ” has taken his place at Sydney Royal.
The present show is gratefully remembered as a
play with a deafening battle in it, also a terrific
horse, on which animal Bland rides off for help
under circumstances too complicated for full ex-
planation. A little band of British heroes is
beset in a jungle by the innumerable brown pagan,
and its only possible way of escape is to build a
bridge over a large gulf, and haul the artillery
across, with only a faint hope that anyone will ever
get home and see mother again. Things come out
all right in the end, but the Royal is one dense
cloud of smoke by the time the army of the brown
transgressor is fully and completely licked.
Bland’s picturesque exit in search of help while
this unpleasantness is going on involves a big
jump and a wild scramble up a precipice ; twice
already the horse has nearly slid down again and
flattened out Bland in the gully, and it looks as
if that tragedy would really come to pass shortly.
The slaughter of the brown transgressor has no
direct bearing on the play, but it is a pleasing
episode in its own irrelevant fashion. The rest of
the story mostly concerns a girl who is led away
into a life of dazzle and champagne and cigarettes
by the base captain who promises to marry her
but doesn’t. Long experience has convinced this
paper that the dramatic captain never does marry
the girl, and her folly in continuing to fly with
that utterly fishy bounder is inexcusable.
* • * •
There are 39 characters in this generally
pleasing drama, and the bulk of them must needs
be taken as read. This doesn’t include the 200
soldiers and the artillery and the great pomp of
war generally. The appearance of Miss Frances
Ross is a gratifying event; that clever actress is a
picturesque feature in any drama, and many
respectable citizens with a grievous scarcity of
hair have noted her long absence with regret.
Otherwise, things may be summed up with a
general statement that the old familiar co. is as
good in a general way as usual, and that Cosgrove
and Charles Brown are rather better than usual.
Bland Holt’s rendering of the other captain also
deserves special mention, and his appalling deeds
on top of the gee-gee are sufficient in themselves
to make the drama go, unless, as already suggested,
the animal should fail to negotiate the gully some
night. In that case, there will be a horse sitting
on an unpleasant squashed hat in the Burmese
vale, and a hurried change of bill will be an-
nounced at the Royal.

At Sydney Lyceum the “White Squad-
ron ” has been extended much beyond the date
originally fixed. An abnormal public demand for
the big Brazilian war-drama is the cause of this
unexpected dragging out, but the wild horse-tail
on top of General Romacio’s helmet will stream on
the free republican atmosphere for the very last
time on Friday night. On Saturday a new drama
with a name that sounds something like “ The
Damned Shock on the Nose ” is to be produced at
the Lyceum, and this paper hopes to say some-
thing nice about it in next issue.

** # *
Buonaparte and his Josephine came back
to Melb. Princess’s on Saturday, and got another
warm greeting from a crowded audience. A good
many people believe in “A Royal Divorce.”
Tears come into their eyes when the low-spirited
Emperor takes an unhistorical farewell of a lady
who was actually buried at the time. Probably the
most realistic character is the one that doesn’t
claim to be historical—the innkeeper, who sits
lapping up his stock amidst the roar of cannon,
too drunk to care whether the allied armies wipe
out the tiresome Napoleonic dynasty, or die in the
attempt. Sufficient for his eventful night is the
bottle thereof. Grimaud, the innkeeper, is the
brightest joke in the proceedings, and J. B. Athol-
wood plays it exceedingly well. The Moscow
and Waterloo tableaux are much the same as
they were last season, which is equivalent to
saying that they are still full of gorgeous battle
and sunset effects, but the St. Helena rock
has been altered, or maybe Julius Knight
doesn’t stand where he did. This depar-
ture from the original effect is not an improve-
ment. Julius Knight looks thinner in the face,
whilst W. F. Hawtrey sounds thinner than ever
in the voice. Miss Ferrar, Miss Page, and Miss
Raymond resume their parts with undintinished
success, and Cyril Keightley, as a youthful Mar-
shal Ney, lends an air of distinction to his brother
officers.
** * *
“The Work Girl,” which is a wild variety
melodrama in a flaming red hat, re-appeared at
Melb. Royal on Saturday. It was there some two
or three years ago, with Florrie Forde in the name-
part, but the first production was rather less joy-
ously larrikinesque than this, and didu’t seem to
hit the popular-priced fancy so fairly in the middle
of the bull’s-eye. “ The Work Girl ” has a casual
murder by way of prologue, and subsequently it
runs on “ Silver King ” lines for a short distance.
Then the hero’s miserable failures to back the
winner are ended by an unjust arrest on a charge
of forgery, or something, whereupon his wife
drops dead, and the widower comes back, after
doing 14 years, to find his child suffering from an
illicit baby on account of a gloomy young man
in an elongated coat. Also, the same villain
who pursued the hero and his wife at the outset is
now laying plans to destroy their daughter and
her unstable admirer, and a lot of tangled woe is
finally pulled straight by a friendly outcast who
had been present at the original murder. The
troubles of the several victims in this somewhat
complex drama are not allowed too much space.
About 50 per cent, of the show is devoted to work
girls, and vegetable barrows, and a large, oily Jew
named Genesis. The girls, as a rule, are good-
looking items apart from their red hats and
feathers, and they seem to struggle for a living on
a system of their own. Perhaps they are em-
ployed in making jokes by the piece. Sometimes
they vary the monotony of toil by assaulting
Genesis. They don’t drive him under a table and
affect to despoil the sweater of his trousers as in
former days, but they tear his shirt sleeves in full
view of the audience, and leave a good deal to be
imagined after a drop-scene has cut them off from
observation. “ The Work Girl ”is a very accept-
able thing of its kind, and quite handsomely
mounted. Willie and Amy Gourlay, King Hed-
ley, Charles Holloway, Miss Alice Deorwyn,
George Chalmers, Miss Duggan, Hans Phillips,
Miss Leighford, and Miss Rose Conroy under-
take most of the business, and the rest of the co. is
numerous and fairly up to the mark. At present,
however, the attack on Genesis can only last for
one consecutive week.
* He * *
On Saturday the Holloway management
(by arrangement with W. and M.) will put Up
“ Two Little Vagabonds,” and the Firm’s touring
company, headed by Titheradge, will take the
Royal floor for 12 nights. The “ Vagabonds ”,
fell rather flat at the Princess’s last year for
several reasons, the uncongenial character of the
theatre being one reason. The Royal is the right
place for the revival, anyhow. Also, the prices
are suitable, and Titheradge, the well-beloved,
threatens to make his last appearance in this good-
hearted drama. Taking one consideration with
another, The Bulletin will be more than sur-
prised if “ Two Little Vags.” doesn’t boom at its
new pitch.
** * *
At Melb. Opera House the polished agility
of the Kellino Family shines in the centre of a
substantial programme. Austin Rudd came along
again on Saturday to fill a small gap, and Rudd
was more than equal to the gap. Just now, too,
the unique Whitburn is funnier than usual, also
Winton’s latest monstrosity lights its pipe with
much success, and Bellman and Lottie Moore,
who were always a good turn, have acquired new
interest from the announcement that their last
nights are now on. Meantime, another batch of
importations from Hingland is half-way across
the bounding wave.
** * *
Phil Newbury’s series of Saturday Shilling
“Pops” at the Town Hall has been very success-
ful thus far. The crowd at the last concert over-
flowed into the organ-gallery, and a large amount
of multitude had to be left outside with its shilling
in its pocket. As the advt. floridly remarks, “Aus-
tralians are an eminently practical people, and
practical ideas include a love of the Beautiful and
the Harmonious.’ Likewise they include a fond
desire to get full value for cash, which is what
Newbury gives them at these vocal, organic and
Biographic entertainments. And seeing that the
large building doesn’t hold more than 2500 people,
and the profits of a successful concert are easily
swept away by a flood of rain on the next
occasion, there is force in the management’s com-
plaint that the municipal landlord, who always
wants his full pound of flesh, is a somewhat un-
reasonable trader. At any rate, an unexpected
tenant, booking the Hall for eight consecutive
Saturdays, deserves to get the usual reduction on
a large order.
** * #
Allan and Co.’s Chamber Concerts, which
supplied a Wednesday afternoon want in the
winter of last year, will be resumed at Melb.
Athenaeum Hall on Wednesday next. Benno
Scherek takes the musical directorship as before,
and a number of high-class instrumental pieces
that are not beyond the comprehension of middle-
class minds have been put into rehearsal.
** * *
The departure of Bert Gilbert and Ada
Reeve from Melbourne last Friday was reminiscent
of the good old boom days, when Nellie Farren
and Fred Leslie had Melbourne at their feet.
The sprightly Ada frisked up to Spencer-street
station in a hansom decked with flowers and drawn
by a prad in a collar of roses and ribbons, while
Gilbert and a crowd of gjlded youths took their
places on the platform. Aforesaid gilded youths,
led by a pink-faced dude in a belltopper, yelled
Gilbert’s songs, all out of tune, and at intervals
gave weird, concerted shrieks supposed to be ex-
pressive of some unstated emotion ; while at the
fence a huge crowd of Melbourne’s gamins gathered
to call out “How dah you, you blackguard!”
until the worried Gilbert soothed them with
largesse, and his clever wife went round and shook
hands with the first three rows.
At Poverty Point.
Alba D.: “ Haddon Chambers had a fixed idea of pathos
—a lonely, moral wreck tramping north, to bury his
hated self in everlasting snows. ‘To the north’ —the
words were on H. C.’s lips 20 times a day. The unborn
' Idler ’ tormented him for years. I once bet H. C. half-a-
crown that the line ‘To the North Pole ’ would set a
house shrieking, no matter how heavily the pathos had
been laid on previously. But Haddon refused to have his
chamber of faith burgled. His mind-picture—the afore-
said wreck, weeping on a floe—is passable enough. It’s
the beastly langwidge that murders the bit of fat : the
word * pole,’ however gently breathed, conjures up a long
axe or a barber’s-shop. Saw ‘ Idler ’ produced at Birming-
ham, three years ago. Waited for last line with mixed
feelings.
Serv.: ‘ Where, might I ask, shall I address your luggage,
sir ? ’
Mark Cross : ‘ To the North Pole.’
I glanced around at the white-faced mechanics and button-
makers, and saw tears in every voice. Went out, feeling
that I owed Haddon half-a-caser. Last night, at Sydney
Cri., Cartwright gave the line in a quiet, subdued voice,
and the gods around me were lifted from deep despair to
innocent hilarity. Query :Is the lean Australian god
less susceptible to futile pathetics than the stodgy Bull
god?”
The policy of sending the reconstructed Comic Opera
co. to Westralia for a course of discipline before opening
up the new opera goods in Sydney and Melbourne marks
the recent expansion of Australia’s theatrical market.
Three years ago Perth was not reckoned among the Firm’s
show towns.
T.D. : “ The George Robey who has booked Ethel Hay-
don for matrimony has for years figured as a funny man

Historicus : “ ‘ A Royal Divorce ’ makes me „..mk of the
case of General Leclerc, first husband of Pauline Bona-
parte, afterwards .Princess Borghese, sculped by Canova
in the Altogezzare. Sick of Pauline’s whims and frivols,
Nap. ordered her off to San Domingo, with Leclerc as
governor. She worked on Leelerc’s feelings by making
him represent to Napoleon that his sister would be left
unprotected—a young thing, just out of a convent. ‘ Oh,’
said Nap., ‘l’ll provide Mdlle. Leclerc with a good hus-
band.’ ‘Who, sire?’ ‘Never mind.’ Davoust stepped
in, full of his project to marry a rich widow, and said he
came to ask the Emperor’s consent to his espousals with
. The Emperor cut him short, saying, ‘Oh yes, I
know, Mdlle. Leclerc.’ And Davoust had to marry Mdlle.
Leclerc, who proved the best wife amid all the spouses of
the Marshals.”
Madame Wiedermann (Mrs. Carl Pinschof), whom Mar-
chesi mentions so cordially in her new book of reminis-
cences, has been suffering badly from facial erysipelas,
but is out of danger now. Marshal Hall declares that
Madame Wiedermann is the only singer of first-class
quality (as distinguished from persons with voices) we
have in Australia ; and, although she only sings at classi-
cal concerts (when she invariably refuses to take a fee),
musical people felt when they heard of her illness that
her loss would be big and heavy. It is expected that she
will be well enough to sing the “ Alcestis ” music when
that weird and hoary tragedy is done by th» “Crinity
College folks next month.
Miss Fanny Wentworth soon got tired of Maoriland, and
decided to disband her little co. at Wellington, although
she was doing booinsome business there. The Dunedin
season had proved frosty, and Christchurch was tackled
during Lent, when the very Anglican population devotes
itself to prayer and repentance. Napoleon Boffard, the
cheerful tenor member of the co., arranged to leave for
Auckland on his own, and give a concert with local
talent. Nap. evidently knows a thing or two. Touching
Miss Wentworth’s amusing caricature of the French lady
in “ I dreamt that I dwelt,” he writes, “ Never mind, I
got my revenge of the French lady—l have the sketch —
Miss Fanny Wentworth singing at sacred concert." At
Christchurch, it seems, she gave them Gounod’s “There
is a green hill ” on Good Friday, and “ the audience was
waiting for something funny in the end, but was dis-
appointed.” Meanwhile Gounod’s plump compatriot
stood at the wing and silently roared in his native
language.
The grit of the Florences. Florence Mack in Sydney,
like Florence Schmidt in Brisbane, is briskly stepping the
staircase of song—and without managerial arm. The
Sydney Florence concerts on 20th May at the Cen-
tenary Hall.
At a Sydney theatre the other night, during the big
storm, a window-blind was blown with a crash on the
stage just as a serio-comic lady was getting on to her
top-note. “My oath!” came a voice from the back,
“ she’s brought down the house.”
George Robey—said to be affianced to Ethel Haydon—
is a rather painfully funny man in print. “ The name of
my favorite song is ‘ Riddle-me-ree,’” he says, “and it
suits my style down to the ground, which accounts prob-
ably for my habit of going down to the ground to start-
each verse. . . . Off the stage I am considered like Mr.
Joseph Chamberlain. I don’t mind being considered like
him, but to be buttonholed in the streets by Royalties
and Cabinet Ministers when I am hurrying on my way
from hall to hall is not pleasant. I’m continually having
to explain my lateness thus to irate managers. ‘ Very
sorry, sir, but Balfour would insist upon worrying me
with golf yarns,’ or ‘lt was Gladstone this time. He
wanted to know the price of screw's, as he thought there
W'as a screw loose somewhere in the Government’s treat-
ment of the American question.”’ This would suggest
that Robey is a labored mummer of the Lonnen order,
but it does him an injustice, according to Bulletin infor-
mation received. On the stage he is a bright success,
and off the stage he is a gentlemanly barrister, who took
to the ’alls in preference to waiting and struggling on for
a show in the law’ courts. If a man aspires to make
money by performing in a wig, why not turn character
singer at once ?

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
In the past four months the S.A. Government
has sold in Adelaide £222,300 worth of 3 per cent, stock.
At this rate S. A. should certainly be able to get all the
loan money it wants locally, though that dismal desert
has no earthly right to want any loan money. S.A.
already owes about £6B per inhabitant, and it is a country
—bar the N.T.—comparatively poor in minerals, and
nearly destitute of vegetation a great part of the time,
and with less visible prospects to borrow on than any
other State near its size in Australasia. Yet there are a
lot of rich men in S.A. The Lord only knows where they
come from.
« * • *
Victoria’s revenue for April was <£652,766, an
increase of £48,903 as compared with April, 1897. For
the ten months of the financial year the receipts are
£204,515 more than they were for the corresponding ten
months of 1897. Turner estimated an increase of £166,000
for the whole year, and expected that, after paying off
£250,000 of the old deficit, he would have a shortage of
£84,000. If things only keep going at the present rate,
however, the weary financial genius with the spectacles
has hopes that he may pay off the aforesaid quarter-
million of old debt, and still come out square.
* * » •
Westralia’s revenue for April was <£209,859,
which is £28,101 less than the receipts for April, 1897,
and £31,920 less than last month’s revenue. It now
begins to look as if the falling-off for the whole year, in-
stead of being about £90,000 as estimated last month, will
be well over £150,000. Westralia last year had a revenue
of £2O 12s. per inhabitant, while Queensland, with the
next biggest revenue, had only £7 13s. Id. Under these
conditions the fall was inevitable ; also it was bound to be
a terrific fall when it came. The woe is that the Forrest
Ministry, like every other boom Ministry, insisted on
believing that it was its own brilliant financing and the
natural greatness of the country which rushed the re-
venue up to three times the average of the other pro-
vinces. And, instead of making the best of its blessing
while it was there, yet being always ready for the inevit-
able slump, it has allowed itself to be taken unawares.
• * * *
Queensland’s April revenue was £332,306, being
an increase of £39,000 as compared with April last year.
The ten months’ revenue is £3,125,871 —£73,000 better
than the corresponding ten months to April 1897 ; and the
revenue is at present £532,000 ahead of expenditure.
» * * *
Tasmanian Treasurer Fysh reckons on paying
off £BO,OOO of his old deficit this year. If N.S.W. attacked
its ancient shortage on the same large scale it would pay
off £900,000 this year, but the Cornstalk province is con-
tent if it can wipe off £150,000 a year, at which rate the old
shortage will still be alive in 1913. And so far the regular
process has been to pay off £150,000 of old deficit and add
£160,000 or so of new shortage. Anyhow, the accumulated
N.S.W. deficit, at June ’97, was £2,363,139 (Coghlan’s
Statistics, page 765) —the biggest figure up to date, and
£443,000 more than the deficit at June, I££s.
* * • *
The A.M.P. Society’s annual report is the most
complete document of its kind published by any Austral-
asian office, and the figures for 1897 show that the famous
office is still prospering. The renewal premiums are
£1,220,405, new premiums £105,789, and total income
£2,080,565. All these figures are bigger than they have
ever been before ; despite all the storm and stress of bad
times, the A.M.P. keeps on growing. The funds are now
£14,479,578—a splendid increase of £751,038 during the
year. The expenses are only £8 13s. per cent, on total
income, which is a lower rate than that of any other Aus-
tralian office. Also, the balance-sheet gives a vast amount
more detail than the ordinary insurance society’s state-
ment is wont to do. It shows, among other things, that
the amount of foreclosed property on hand has diminished
by £43,000 during the year. Arrears of interest have in-
creased, but the total (£40,159) is still moderate. The
holding of State securities (£1,141,005) has increased by
£300,000 during 1897, and all things considered this is a
good feature of the case. Too much investment on mort-
gage is doubtful policy at a time when the bottom has
fallen so badly out of the property market, and when the
replacement of that necessary article is so uncertain
The A.M.P. has a surplus of <£445,235 to divide
among its members, and it reports that even this large
surplus will probably become still larger in coming years.
The estimates of future interest have now been reduced
to 3£ per cent., and the gradual process of reduction has
necessitated a great increase in the reserves. Now that
it is completed the A.M.P. expects to break out into even
bigger bonuses than heretofore. The Bulletin reckons
that it would be a vast amount wiser if it let the bonus
stand at its present figure for a few years, and built up a
more substantial reserve to cover possible depreciation.
During the past five years the A.M.P. has written off
£260,519 to cover depreciation, yet, large as this amount
is, it doesn’t look much considering the 14J millions of
property, and further considering what kind of years the
last five have been. If it is sufficient, the A.M.P. has been
remarkably fortunate. Also, there remains a reserve of
£288,488 to cover further possible depreciation, which
certainly looks large, yet it is only a very small 2 per cent,
on the total funds. Now that the A.M.P. has placed its
interest estimates on a 3£ per cent, basis it will have larger
surpluses to dispose of, and it could set itself to build up
a reserve of £1,000,000 without reducing the bonuses in
the least. Such a reserve ought to make it about the
strongest life assurance concern in the world, and the
advt. alone would be worth it.
Another thing — the A.M.P. ought to have a
State audit and a regular State valuation. Even if this
is not enforced by law the A.M.P. should apply for it as a
special concession. It is unlike all other assurance insti-
tutions, inasmuch as it holds as much public money as all
other Australasian offices put together. There is no
efficient control of any life office by policy-holders, while
in the case of a stupendous concern like the A.M.P. there
is practically no control worth mentioning. Only one-
fourth of its business is in N.S.W. Only a part of that
fourth is in Sydney. And only a small section of the Syd-
ney policy-holders have any personal knowledge of the
Sydney business men whom they annually elect as direc-
tors and trust with the control of 14£ millions of assets,
and whose reports they accept as a guarantee that the 14 j
millions are still there. Every assurance office runs itself
to a great extent, and the larger it grows and the further
its operations spread, the more it runs itself. The
A.M.P. Society wants a £1,000,000 reserve and a regular
State audit and valuation, simply because it has grown so
vast, and has so much public confidence ana public
money, that any mishap would be a national calamity.
Any other office might go wrong and be merely a casual
streak of ill-luck.
a
The Australian Mutual Fire Insurance Co., th a
most prosperous concern in its line in Australia, shows s
profit for the year ended 31st March of £17,647, which i
about the same as the figures for the two previous years.
Shareholders receive 8 per cent, dividend (£2000) and 20
Eer cent, bonus on premiums (£7439) is divided equally
etween shareholders and policy-holders. The share-
holders’ half makes a total return of about 23 per cent,
per annum on capital. Also, £4500 is added to reserve,
making it £98,500; and £3OOO to contingency fund,
making it £9OOO. The Australian Mutual Fire has now
£107,500 of reserves and £1179 profit and loss balance,
and its receipts from interest and rents alone last year
amounted to £62l9—nearly 25 per cent, on capital. It is
a noteworthy fact that the Australian Mutual does busi-
ness in N.S.W. only. Every time the State Fire Insurance
idea crops up a number of interested experts arise to re-
mark that such a thing is impossible, because a State
office would have to confine its business to its own pro-
vince, and the only way to make fire insurance reason-
ably safe is to spread the risks over a much larger area.
Yet the most consistently prosperous fire insurance co. in
Australia does confine its business to one province—just
as the State institution would have to do. Possibly the
explanation of the discrepancy is that an expert is mostly
an individual trained to be plausibly inaccurate on his
own side of the case —the side with the butter on it.
• * * »
The Bank of Australasia, for the half-year
ended 11th October, 1897, shows a profit of £40,278, and
the usual 5 per cent, dividend eats up £40,000 of this,
leaving £278 to add to the funds. Some recent profit re-
turns of the Australasia are as follows :
October, 1892 .. £80,346 October, 1995 .. £41,159
April, 1893 .. 60,742 April, 1896 .. 40,568
October, 1893 .. 60,607 October, 1896 .. 40,400
April, 1894 .. 48,571 April, 1897 .. 45,376
October, 1894 .. 45,735 October, 1897 .. 40,278
April, 1895 .. 40,854
This half-year’s returns are the worst since 1851, and equal
about £3 7s. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’
funds. This is low enough, certainly, though plenty of
banks are doing worse even than the ’Asia at present.
The main feature of the report is that cash on hand
amounts to £3,941,500 —equal to over 25 per cent, of the
entire liabilities to the public ; while cash and liquid
securities together amount to £5,240,169, equivalent to
34 per cent, of liabilities. This sort of thing means a lot
of security to depositors, though these bloated cash re-
serves which are earning nothing in the cellars keep the
profits low and the dividends very scanty.
The ’Asia is now 64 years old. It started with
£200,000 capital, and its dividends have ranged from
to 20 per cent. In its biggest boom year, 1885, the profits
were £274,800; now it begins to look as if they might
come down to the odd £74,800 with the 2 left out. It
is difficult to realise, in this year when the Cornstalk
population is so much addicted to looking for a midnight
crust in the ash-bin, how big and glorious that great bor-
rowing boom of last decade really was, but a look back at
the huge dividends of banks that are now either very
humble or wholly crumpled up conveys some idea of the
situation.

From boom-time to sewerage-time. In the boom,
a Melb. speculator bought a suburban block, and ran-up
some half-dozen similar wooden villas, which he insured
for £l5O each. To escape the sewerage complications, he
is now offering them for sale (for removal) at £74 each.
T’other day, one of them was burnt down. The insurance
people want to buy one of his adjoining villas for £74, and
place it, phoenix-like, on the ashes ; but he is sticking out
for his full £l5O insurance-money.
« * * •
Buying big estates and selling same in fair-sized
farms is profitable in Victoria. At the recent Ballan sale
prices ranged from £lO to £25 per acre, and, as improve-
ments were slight, unearned increment must have been
thick. M.L. system of Govt, taking over estates for sub-
division cannot come too soon.
* * * *
One of these days (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent), a swindled client will discover the law on the sub-
ject, and claim from the clerk of petty-sessions the money
which he is unable to get from his lawyer. Clerks of
court are legally bound to pay over moneys which have
been lodged with them to the successful party in a suit,
and not to his solicitor, or anybody else.

Broken Hill Proprietary is about to duplicate
its concentrating plant; the mill, which will be on the
same general principles as that now in operation, will, of
course, be erected on the mine.
* * * *
Broken Hill British concentrating mill, which
was lately overhauled by an imported expert, is giving at
least as much trouble as ever, and has been running only
occasionally during the last few weeks while still another
overhaul has been carried on.

Printed that three shifts before a recent big col-
lapse in a Broken Hill mine (and this, too, reckoned one
of the most carefully managed in the district) a couple of
working miners protested to a shift boss against working
in the place, which they regarded as unsafe, and, according
to their account, were told that if they did not choose to
work there no other place would be found for them.

Broken Hill Block 14 writes down its nominal
capital from £500,000 to £125,000. The actually sub-
scribed capital was £68,000. Of the 100,000 shares, all but
4000 (which were sold at £6 par) were handed over to the
Broken Hill Proprietary Co. in payment for the_ block ;
these 96,000 were issued as paid up to £4 10s., and 10s. has
since been called.
* * * *
So many complaints—probably ungroimded, as
a rule—are made that contractors in the B.H. mines suffer
through incorrect measurements being taken that the
proposal has been made for the appointment of a check
inspector. Mine officials, pooh-poohing the complaints,
say they don’t mind the measurements being checked,
and invite all parties to be present at measurings in which
they are interested ; but, as for a check-inspector, who
shall pay ? The miners’ union, at any rate, shows no dis-
position that way.

What the Barrier trade means to S.A. is pretty
well illustrated by the fact that in ’97 the railways car-
ried 465,000 tons of stuff to and from Broken Hill.

Cu9S : “ ‘ What has become of the Beacon gold-
mine?’ The Beacon is still serenely hammering away 24
hours a day, six days in the week, at the same old stand six
miles S.E. of Coramba, in the heart of the meanest scrub
that ever drove a prospector dotty. So far as not * hear-
ing overmuch about the Beacon,’ please take that as a
normal condition of affairs: Manager Orabb does not
work the Beacon by playing the bassoon. Further, in-
stead of the Beacon having paid ‘one or two small divs.’
it has paid three, equal to 7£ per cent, on the £200,000 at
which the property is capitalised. Is there another Bull-
owned mine in N.S.W. that has done so well in the first
12 months’work? The Beacon manager had to clear a
battery-site in the midst of a dense scrub six miles from
a decent road and to get in the machinery supplies, &c.,
and cut three and a-half miles of track ‘on his own.’
Yet, in lOmonths from the day he arrived on the ground
he had the record of 4905 tons of stone put through,
yielding 76460 z. gold. Further, he has reduced the cost
of treatment from 50s. sd. per ton to 30s. 6d. The Beacon
is quite well, thank you.
“ Denning’s claim, near Coramba, N.S.W.,
some time since put through the Beacon battery—three
miles distant—46 tons stone for 22dwts. per ton. They
are now carting in a further lot of over 100 tons, and
should this go as well as the last, of which there is little
doubt, then they seem in for a good thing, as the reef is
big—from 2£ft. to 4ft., like the ‘ Reward ’ reef in the Beacon.
The claim is situated on the summit of one of the highest
ridges in the district.
“ The Coramba King is taking another vaca-
tion. The Beacon co. has bought the battery at Nana
Greek, and will remove it to its Mt. Brown property on
the Coramba-Coff’s Harbor-road, five miles south of
Coramba. Nana Creek, which, some 16 or 18 years ago,
was a busy, thriving mining town, is now absolutely
deserted, the warden’s clerk, who has lived there for the
last 18 years, having been transferred to Coramba. The
extravagant Govt, pay this official £4O a year. Fancy
living a hermit’s existence in the heart of a wilderness,
ten miles from home and 40 miles from nowhere, for £4O
a year ! Why aQ. Divisional Board clerk is a king com-
paratively. Yet the present clerk stood it for 18 years,
and then ‘ bucked ’ when he was ordered to shift. Said if
the public wanted to do business with him let them come
to him in his sylvan retreat. Why in h —l should he
move in to the public ? ”
* * • *
Why N.S.W. Mines Minister Smith should
propose to confine his national mine-accident fund to
coal-mmers is not clear. It cannot be that coal-mining is
riskier than metal-mining, because the figures of Minister
Smith’s department show that it isn’t. These are the
number of fatal accidents per thousand workmen in the
last three years (the Mines report for ’97 is, of course, not
out yet—only four months of the current year having
gone)—
Silver. Gold. Coal.
1894 .. ... 2-14 0-80 0767
1895 .. .. 4-29 0-62 1108
1896 .. .. 216 116 2-541
The fact probably is that in coal-mines disasters come
comparatively seldom, but are apt to be big, and so to
give an exaggerated idea of the miners’ dangers, while in
silver-mines the unfortunate fellows go off unnoticed one
by one.
* • * *
Hillgrove Proprietary Mines —an English co.
that is spending a heap of money in the hope of picking
up the Baker’s Creek ore chute, has driven a tunnel
over 700 ft. “ Hillgrove is building a good deal of its
hopes of future greatness on the result of the great enter-
prise of this company.” The same co. has the “ Paddy
Lackey Deep Levels” scheme. Enterprising, no doubt,
but time wifi show as to its judgment.
• • * »
The Mines Dept, of N.S.W. might, with some
profit perhaps, get expert opinion as to the safety of
the present system of under-ground open-cutting or
quarrying at the B.H. mines. The number of accidents
which happened, and several unreported yet remarkable
escapes from still worse accidents, support the view of
many experienced workmen that the method is extremely
unsafe ; and it ought not to be sufficient answer to this
that it is cheap. The fault—at least the most striking
fault—is that the excavations are much too lofty for the
roofs to be “ read” or sounded as it is imperative for the
safety of the workmen that they should be. Instead of
being from 20 to 25 feet high they should be no more than
10 feet; then not only could places weakened by firing in
the working face be detected, but they could be effectively
staved.
• * ■ *
For unrewarded pluck in adversity and without
reward the writer wants to pass the cake to the Hongkong
men who are running mines at M‘Donald, N.S.W. Some
seven years back they paid more cash and shares for the
old Balmoral mine than all the mines in the camp were
worth ; put up a new 20-head mill, and then found that
they had little or nothing to crush. After going down for
over £20,000, they bought and opened up other mines on
the field, got on to a run of stone in the Eureka, the-first
crushing from which went 29dwt. This put them in great
shape. Another Hongkong co. has started operation's, and
the 20-head mill is being moved to the payable mine—the
crushings from which are gradually coming down, unfor-
tunately. Altogether, over £lOO,OOO of Hongkong money
has been spent in Mt. M‘Donald.

Horatioßottomley, the London mining-magnate,
who is always “ explaining ” something, was industriously
booming Northern Territories at latest. The shares, by
the way, have slumped considerably, owing to Lowe, one
of the directors, selling every share at top-prices, and,
when the mail left, Bottomley was “explaining” Lowe’s
action, amid loud cheers.
* • « *
Within two or three weeks of the awful Bottom-
ley slump, which has forced the Market Trust, with its
recently-subscribed capital of 21 millions, into reconstruc-
tion and struck absolute terror into the Northern
“Terrors,” “Moses Moss,” of London Truth, said:—
“The W.A. Market Trust has again shown great strength,
and Northern Territories have now gone ahead to £4 15s.
on a cable received from Port Darwin by the Financial
News, according to which the properties are phenomen-
ally rich—especially the Eureka and Howley. It is
stated that, in the former, there is sufficient high-grade
ore in sight to keep 40 head of stamps at work for several
years.” He also coolly talks of 50,000 tons of tailings
running from 1 to 3oz. available from Chinese old work-
ings. Great Heavens ! John leaving 3oz. tailings ! And
he puts down the general value of the lode in one level at
6oz. per ton. Knowing this group some Australians in
London started to sell them “short” in thousands.
Bottomley stood to his guns as long as the money lasted,
finally found himself in trouble, and now wants another
quarter-million to help him out of the mud and says he
has not lost confidence. No one could accuse him of that,
but there must be many people who would prefer
Bottomley losing his own confidence instead of their cash.
The same great authority barracks for the amalgamation
of the mostly-worthless mines floated by the London and
Globe, under the new scheme to carry them along,
remarking that if they don’t agree to the scheme “ more
money will have to be put up as if enough had not been
already spent to prove their real worth.
• • • •
“ The lines of Mr. Albert Calvert, the eminent
explorer, litterateur, financier, company-monger, and
sportsman, have not of late been cast in pleasant places.”
But is there any place in the world outside of London that
would have so long tolerated such a very hollow pumpkin.
Calvert’s racing-partner, while trying to recover from him
£14,000, money due, deposed that Calvert tried to fix him
up with shares in the Imperial Western Australian Cor-
poration, which, on inquiry, he found began and ended
with Calvert—there was nothing in it but Calvert, and,
having had too much of that already, he wanted to get
out of it. Whatever the verdict, “it is thought Calvert’s
name will be of no value in future for prospectus-pur-
poses.” Australia sized up Calvert when he first appeared
on the horizon.

Bayley’s United, Coolgardie, has at last had a
crushing—2l6 tons for 48oz. But it is explained that to
date it has treated 4001 tons of tailings by cyanide for
over 4000 oz. gold. That must have made a big hole in the
heap, and this crushing indicates what will become of the
mine when the heap is finished.

* * • •
Kalgoorlie wire:—“ The market is now much
firmer, and investors are eager to get in, feeling that the
war will not have any effect on future working.” Strange
that the war-scare affected no other mining camp in Aus-
tralia. Kalgoorlie mines are controlled by an army of
London gamblers, and apart from yields or developments
“ bears ” and “ bulls ” will swing the pendulum. The first
get the benefit of any scare, war or otherwise, and the
latter get it as soon as it is over.
* » * »
Of the 82,3440 z. of gold obtained in Kalgoorlie
district during the first quarter of the year, 16,3890 z. was
recovered by cyaniding. The L.V. Consols, Australia,
and Brown Hill, which are using the process, are in
hourly dread of a demand for a percentage by the holders
of the patent rights; and as the Golden Horseshoe,
Boulder Main Reef, Ivanhoe, Bank of England and others
contemplate using the process, an agitation has begun (in
influential quarters, of course) in favor of the Govt, com-
promising with the co., as was done in M.L.
* * *. *
“ The Golden Casket has belied its name,” said a
recent Bulletin par. Mines with such names nearly
always do. They are generally the “second errp,” pro-
duced for company-floating on the strength of the first
crop’s success. Of the Kalgoorlie mines proper there is
only one “ Golden ” anything. It is the Golden Horse-
shoe, and it has gone out of the gold-producing list. The
Towers (Q.) has one successful “ Golden” only—the Golden
Gate. Bendigo hasn’t one, nor has Ballarat. Broken Hill
had Silver Hills, and Silver Crescents, and Silver Dales,
and Silver Crowns, and Extendeds of all these things ; and
not one of them paid a dividend —few of them ever gave
any ore to market. When the gold or the silver is stuffed
into the name the chances are that it’s not in the mine,
and so it is sounder policy to “ pass.”

Of the upwards of 200 gold-producers of Westra-
lia 143 are wholly or partly owned in England, and 71 per
cent, of the total gold-production came from these. Of
the 18 div. payers now in that province 13 are Bull cos.,
and they take 96 per cent, of the total divs.—amounting
to just under half-a-million. These and many other
figures are furnished by J. C. Auldjo, who seems to be
quite unable to get hold of the call-paying end of the
game.

* * • *
For the year ’97 Queensland sugar statistics run
thus : 66,211 acres of cane were crushed for 97,824 tons of
sugar and 2,364,020 gals, molasses. Southern Q. contri-
buted 34,146 tons (of which Bundaberg’s output was
16,999 tons) ; Central Queensland produced only 805 tons ;
Northern Q. 62,873 tons. Mackay alone gave 22,818 tons
towards the total output—for once beating Bundaberg,
temporarily depressed by a bad season.
'* • * *
Chillagoe Proprietary shares have reached .£1650
for a 480th—£200 paid ; and the Chillagoe boom has
started in dead earnest in Melbourne. One Chillagoe
share was even bought in Sydney (of all places !) last week,
and the average Sydney mining-man is just sufficiently
awake to rub bis eyes and ask : Where is Chillagoe ? The
pegging-out boom is on—many miles have been pegged ;
ana there are many boomsters on the road to Chillagoe
capable of “staking” all the land between Chillagoe and
Cloncurry on the one side, and the coast on the other.
They have come from Tasmania, where the copper-boom
has burst; from WestraUa, where it is easier to raise
money from the dead than on a mine ; and from Mel-
bourne, where boomsters of the pegging-out and “ cheap-
option ” sorts are always kept on tap, to be sent to the
uttermost parts of the earth on an emergency. Of course,
they are all the representatives of “ powerful syndicates,”
and have millions of capital behind them. That is why
they don’t walk to the field. Queensland provincial press-
men within hundreds of miles of Chillagoe can now look
forward to a large supply of cheap whisky with con-
fidence. Other Melb. syndicates have everything ready
but the money, and are advertising investors to dump
down £5 quickly, as a capable man is ready to start.
That awful mining authority, Sydney Sunday Times,
directs attention to the new thing, about which it is—just
you bet! —going to have special information shortly—
obviously quite innocent of the fact that the camp is
nearly 20 years old, and it is over a year since the people
who have now got all the mines for miles round started
operations. “ Beware of Chillagoe cats ”is the writer’s
tip, and it is given with a feeling that, like his criticisms
of Westralian mining swindles, it will only be respected
by that asinine animal, the public, when it is too late.

M.L. mines: Waihi — 6690 tons, for bullion to
the value of £22,852 —the largest output for any month.
A consistent producer ; worse luck, there are very few in
the country. Waitekauri —1680 tons, for £4851 value.
Most of the mines taken over by Bull in last boom are
proving beyond doubt that they were born in lying and
fostered in iniquity, and that the gold is not in them.
* * * *
Maldon (Vic.) remembers once more the glories
of the South German, which paid £185,400 in divs. on a
called-up capital of £11,400, and threatens a revival. Last
quarter the gold-returns for the district amounted to
£88430z., or 35620 z. more than for the last quarter of ’97,
and this and the operations of the Maldon Goldfields co.
(English) have sent the little town on a tear. Of course a
Stock Exchange has been opened. Up to date the English
co. has done nothing more than forward a five-ton parcel
for treatment; but as it gave 4oz. to the ton the district
already distinctly hears many stamps clattering.
By the way, what a marvellous prejudice the
average mining man has against machinery that doesn’t
make a clatter. The Lake View Consols filter-presses are
locally scorned by a big crowd on account of their silence,
just as the leaching plant at B.H. was.
* * * *
Shearing in the earliest Western District (N.S.
Wales) shed —Nocoleche—has commenced, under the
contract system, naturally detested by the unions. A
contracting firm claims to have made big engagements
with Bourke, Brewarrina, and Walgett machine-using
. sheds for the season.

SOCIETY
The Hauhau religion, which seems to be
the main animating cause of the present Maori
outbreak at Hokianga (M.L.), is a queer mixture
of Christianity, Maori mythology, and European
militarism. It was promulgated during the
laranaki war of 1863 by a prophet named Te Ua,
who instructed his compatriots that they were the
lost ten tribes of Israel, and that by united action
they could drive the pakeha into the sea. They
were to worship the Christian God, also Riki, the
Maori God of War, who had chosen as his military
representative on earth the spirit of Joshua, the
?°pof Nun. The ceremonial of this remarkable
faith consisted chiefly in dancing wildly round a
pole stuck in the ground and bearing at the top a
newly-severed human head, while the votaries
chanted something like this: “God the Father!
Hau ! God the Son ! Hau ! Hau ! ! Attention !
Instruct us ! Attention, Jehovah ! Avenge us,
Hau ! Jehovah, stand at ease ! Hau ! Fall out!
Hau hau, pai mariri hau ! Big rivers, long rivers,
big mountains, attention ! Hau-hau ! ” The gro-
tesqueness of the function was increased by the
fact that the military commands in the prayer
■re rendered in English, with an imitation of the
AT N.S.W . NATIONAL ART GALLERY.
FIRST CRITIC : “A strong bit of impressionism!”
SECOND CRITIC: “ Yes; evidently of the rude and lusty school!”
-Piccadilly bleat of the peg-topped warriors of the
Crimean era. Te Ua’s religion probably would
not have made much headway had not he pro-
phesied the wreck of the Lord Worsley, which
actually occurred at White Cliffs, Taranaki, and,
of course, tremendously impressed the Maoris and
greatly increased their patriotism. It was Kere-
opa, a disciple of Te Ua’s, who murdered mission-
ary Volkner at the East Coast. Volkner was first
hanged, then decapitated, and finally the Maoris
gravely drank his blood out of his own com-
munion-cup !
♦ * * *
It is not generally known that up at
Argent’s Hill, Nambucca district (N.S.W.), there
is a large and flourishing community of Mormons.
Some of their rites, especially that of baptism, in-
duce considerable curiosity among their “Gen-
tile ” friends, and large crowds gather to witness
the “ immersions.’’ So far as is known, polygamy
is not practised, but the shameful intermarriages
that are taking place will yet lead to State inter-
ference—the sooner the better.
• * * *
Mrs. Rosser, who suicided with her hus-
band, the French cyclist, in Melb., the other day,
prepared herself for the great event as carefully as
if she had been dressing for a ball. She put on
her most effective dress, elaborately curled and
dressed her hair, arranged herself upon the bed in
graceful pose, and died with her face set in a
pleasant smile. The writor knows of another
similar instance, but the woman in the latter
case hanged herself in evening dress, with all her
jewellery on, and a touch of carmine on her lips.
The average man’s vanity is not small, but it is a
poor passion beside that of the average woman,
who would always rather look well than be well.
* * * *
Nature and Art again. A girl hanged her-
self at Maribyrnong (Vic.), the other day, and her
mother at the inquest said she knew of no motive
aside from the fact that the girl had been much
depressed by reason of her inability to learn a part
in a teetotal dialogue that had been set her at
Sunday-school. Constance Cotterell, authoress of
“An Impossible Person,” was roared at by the
critics when she made her heroine attempt to
drown herself because she could not get off by
heart some slabs of Shakespeare that she was re-
quired to learn. Perhaps there is a good deal of
reason in these rash acts. Live and learn is not
always an easy thing.
* * * *
Those who profess to “know all about
these things ’’ assert that the real reason so many
domestic creditors of a certain high official have
been left lamenting for a season is that the money
intended to liquidate the debts was entrusted to
another official, who was as delighted to meet with
the “stuff” as with a long-lost relative, and
equally loth to part with same.
* * * *
In Melb. Criminal Court, last week, the
astonished loafers in the gallery sniffed the odor of
Australia’s good old convict days when Judge
Holroyd passed the death-sentence on a man con-
victed of setting fire to a house on March 23rd,
“certain persons being then therein.” Of all
civilised countries, this is the richest in capital
offences. The Federal flag, when it comes, cer-
tainly ought to present a picture of Nosey Bob
gazing upward through his hempen noose at the
Southern Cross.

* * * *
The recent abundant crop of terrible mari-
time disasters will not have been an unmixed evil
if it wakes up the stay-at-home, feather-bed
majorityof the Australian community totheurgent
necessity for a complete overhaul of the local re-
gulations affecting the seaworthiness of shipping.
I j ie attempt to have the matter drastically dealt
with shall not, if The Bulletin can help it, peter
gently out like the half-hearted efforts of tho
past. The nail must be driven home this time,
and the province relieved from the reproach of
being oriminally indifferent to the safety of tho
sea-worker.
* * * *
Certain recent disasters suggest the re-
markable contrast between the control (?) of sea-
traffic and that over traffic ashore. Shipping
bosses repeatedly order, or, at least, allow, their
employes to brave risks which, if they were under-
taken in occupations ashore and life was lost,
would place the men responsible in gaol for a year
or two. Writer remembers one case in which the
captain of a coastal steamer flinched before
the tremendous seas raging on a river bar,
but received positive instructions to cross. A
t xssenger remarked that a tussle was likely. “ A

baby’s going to have a fit.”
JOHN : "By Jove Iso he is. Where’s my camera?”
tussle !’’ ejaculated the skipper, grimly. “ There
ain’t going to be a tussle, sir. There’s going to be
a wreck.” Next instant —scrunch —bump—bump
—and the stewards were handing out life-belts all
round. Had the skipper refused to cross—well,
there are plenty of master-m iriners coasting about
as second mates.

In acknowledging receipt (per Thos. Brady,
Cooktown) of J 213 4s. 9d., subscribed by the miners
of Woodlark goldfield, New Guinea, The Bulletin
regrets that, despite the irrefutable facts in his
favor, Suffield is still in prison ; and the ghoulish
N.S.W. Dept, of Justice persistently refuses to
permit inquiry into his case. Pointed attention
will, it is hoped, be drawn to the matter in next
session of N.S. W. Parliament.

Kati-Kati : At ’Ome people talk about
their rich relatives in Australia. In Australia
they bray about their rich relations at ’Ome. Of
the poor relations nothing is said ; they do the
talking. Curious, this pride in being more un-
successful than one’s relatives!

Pronounced “ Johnnie ” sitting at
table in Vic. mining-township hotel, with
bottle of fizz under his wing. Burly Out-
Backer, to ditto, with nod towards gold-top :
“Parse the tomater-sauce, will ye ! ’’—and
in an instant the cham. is grabbed and the
bush ogre is pouring it liberally over the
contents of his plate. The horror of the be-
reaved was only less ludicrous to behold
than the blank astonishment of the bereaver
at the way the “sarce ” played-up amongst
his “ vittles.”
Very diminutive bounder (aggra-
vated Hebrew stamp), in gorgeous array,
seated solus at the centre table of Mel-
bourne’s most fashionable cafe, ostenta-
tiously sipping gold-top (he had a big bottle
all to himself), and smirking round for ad-
miration. As the place began to fill up,
however, he lost his nerve ; his hand began
to shake as he lifted the glass to his mouth ;
he broke out into a cold perspiration as the
girls all round him giggled while comparing
the hoggish mannikin with his big bottle :
and at last, unable longer to endure the
mischievous amusement with which his
gradual collapse was being observed on every
side, he jumped up, upsetting his chair, and fledl
into the wilderness—undone by too much glory L

* * * *
An Australian temperance-saint defaulter,
whose painful disappearance with the cash is
being gradually forgotten, among his other good
works once presented a handsome ornamental
iron railing to a certain church. The butter of
eulogy was spread very thick over the Cold Tea
brother on account of this gift, and it was not till
he had gone, and had taken a good deal of the
church funds along with him, that the builder
turned up to state that he must either have his
mom yat once or get back the railing. The comic
saint had told him to debit it to the church, and
the gift was a sinful hoax.

VISIONS.
An Epilogue to “Ghosts.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear, though the daylight’s scarcely seen,
I am l deadly dry so soon,
And thou art naught but a dead marine
A-flash in the misty moon ;
A-flash in the misty moon, I say,
And dry as the husks of Sin ;
’Tis thine to mock at my thirsty clay,
And usher my goblins in.
For there in the dawn are spectres twain
That lurk in the ghoulish grey,
The ghosts of two by a landlord slain
When he telleth a boozer “ Nay ! ”
A flask so fair doth a hero bear
As a Viking bore his queen,
’Tis only the bottle that once you were,
And the drunk I might have been.
Q.

• * * *
Of the 640 candidates who applied for the
40 billets in Vic. mounted police, the number was
judiciously weeded down to 445. 236 of these
were promptly bumped because of varicose veins
and other physical defects, 33 were not tall
enough, 12 were too old, 6 too young, and 31 too
heavy. 127 were still left, and from them 69 were
picked for further examination. 10 of these failed
to pass the medical test. 18 didn’t ride well enough,
and 2 had faulty sight. This left 39, who were
appointed, and the department is beginning to
despair about finding the 40th man.
* * * •
Among the candidates for the Vic. mounted
police examination was a strapping young fellow,
“highly recommended by the local member,” and
whose physique at once made him a likely man.
Medical examination showed him to be entirely
satisfactory, and the usual enquiries were started
as to his character. The result was that the
would-be “ cop ” was shown to have a list of con-
victions for minor and fineable offences of which
any Collingwood larrikin might be proud, his
brightest deed of riotous behavior having been
expiated by three months’ hard. When the local
member was remonstrated with over his recom-
mendation, he indignantly protested : “ Why,
that’s just the sort of man you want in the police-
force—he’ll know how to look after the others.”
* * * *
P. . “ 640 applicants for 40 possible and
unannounced vacancies in the mounted-police
force of Victoria. Rather ! Don’t think it’s only
the Mary Anns who worship ’em. Why, here in
a provincial daily—one of the first half-dozen in
N.S.W.—is a half-column report of a ‘ send-off ’ to
Mounted-Constable So-and-so. There was ‘ a
large gathering of friends and representatives of
various bodies,’ including a couple of M’sP.; and
part of the business was ‘ a presentation from a
few of his friends as a token of respect,’ though if
there isn’t a rule against the making of presenta-
tions to constables there ought to be.”

At the recent Tas. militia manoeuvres the
local press reports that
Ten rounds of common shot and five of shrapnel were
served to the southern Artillery, the northerners being
onlookers, having failed to bring their ammunition. With
the common shot the targets were pierced in three places
[seven misses in ten]. At the close of the practice the
Commandant expressed himself very highly gratified. . .
The infantry forces were engaged in field firing. Firing
was carried out by sections, who fired volleys in suc-
cession from the right of battalions until the ammunition
was expended. Considering that a very large percentage
engaged in the practice were absolutely new to it, the
Commandant expressed his pleasure. The camp is one of
the cleanest ever held. The muster is now over 600 (! !).
During the officers’ mess a flashlight photograph was
taken of the gentlemen at dinner. ... In con-
nection with the report that the northern artillery
were onlookers at Saturday afternoon’s proceedings, it
might be mentioned that they, like the southerners, only
have fifteen rounds supplied to them each year, and they

The Bulletin hereby gives due notice, ;is
required by the Act, that if there are 80,000 votes
for Federation in N.S.W. on June 3 it intends to
smash Zech’s window. Zech’s is about the biggest
plate-glass window in George-street, Sydney, and
considering the smash that a brick would make
going through the window, and the wreckage the
same brick would make among the glass and
china inside, and the further smash that the ruins
of the window would make among the glass and
china, this paper has selected Zech’s window as
the place where the biggest possible smash can
be done with the smallest amount of brick. It
has long been saving up Zech’s window against
some great occasion when it would have to cele-
brate or burst, and if the necessary 80,000 votes
are forthcoming on June 3 the said window goes.
And if Federation is carried in all other provinces
as well The Bulletin intends to wreck Nichol-
son’s piano emporium into the bargain.
True to their predatory instincts—as a
newspaper leader-writer would observe—some of
the women passengers who put themselves into
life-belts when the s.s. Edina looked like sinking
recently on the Vic. coast, refused to relinquish
their fittings when the danger had passed. Ul-
timately they carried the belts home with them
as interesting mementoes of the “shipwreck,”
and became indignant, as a rule, at the “ mean-
ness ” of Howard Smith & Co. in requesting a
restoration of the goods. The glorious unreason-
ableness of lovely woman appears to great ad-
vantage in any little case of this sort. Says she—
a sailor found me a belt to put on, and if I had
been drowned at the bottom of the sea how could
they have expected me to give the thing back
again? Also, she insists upon making special
rules for special occasions, and if in the course of
a panic rush at a theatre she were to get accident-
ally possessed of somebody else’s diamond brooch,
or somebody else’s husband, no arguments would
persuade her that she hadn’t a fair right to keep
it or him under the cireumstances.

Colonel Fire-low Price remarked in his
swashbucklering way at a recent Melb. banquet:
“ I see before me representatives of every leading
industry of the pro vince ; the class of men who have
helped to make Victoria, and will continue the
making in spite of the frothy utterances of im-
ported agitators.” Cheers from the makers of the
province. Unfortunately, several other “makers”
were unavoidably absent—in gaol—or they also
would have cheered.

Dear Bulletin : Your Melb. polony yarn
isn’t a patch on what occurs every day in Sydney
and suburbs. To-day our butcher sent us some
excellent steak and some kidneys, for asteak-and-
kidney pie. My wife brought me one of the
kidneys with the question “Is there anything
wrong with this kidney ? ” I examined it and
found the kidney substance almost wholly re-
placed by hydatid cysts. If the servant had been
cooking it would probably have all gone in. Did
you say steak and hydatid pie ? No, thanks.—
Medico.
When readers see in the daily press a
column or couple of columns of clear-typed
anonymous matter, affecting to describe,
but, in reality, fulsomely eulogising
the management of a Govern-
ment asylum or depart-
ment, or some such insti-
tution, they should warily
test the statements before
giving faith to them. Num-
bers of Australian institu-
tions now regularly concoct
and forward such articles,
and have the front to ask
the editor to publish them
as if written independently
in his office. In several
cases lately, a large Aus-
tralian daily has complied
with the cheeky request,
with the result that the in-
mates of the institutions,
other than the officers, tak-
ing the articles to be the
work of newspaper-men,
will curse reporters while
they live.

When a Vic. farmer
Out-back wants to get a
small, “ neglected ” boy
from the State to drive
home his cows gratis, he
has first to apply to the
secretary for the district,
who is usually the minis-
ter’s wife. Next he has to
get a gospel-shop-keeper to
sign a document stating
that he knows and esteems
the applicant and his
cousins and hisaunts,
and, as there is
generally only one
such man in the
place, he applies to
the secretary’s hus-
band. This done, the
application is con-
sidered by the com-
mittee —which consists
of the minister and his
wife and the secretary and
her husband. Hence it is
very important that the applicant should be
a regular attendant at the weekly howl, else
his chance of getting a small, scared boy who
daren’t talk back to run after the cows gratis
amounts to about half of nothing.

A lady speaker at a late meeting of Melb.
W.C.T.U. stated, in tones of alarming horror, that
she knew of a youth being turned from religious
resolves through seeing a Sunday-school teacher
put drink to his lips. Awful !
Dear Bulletin.— A woM or two more
about that “ unnatural offence ” case and the Mel-
bourne sup-press. “ Scribe,” as a reporter, should
know better than to call a mere record of a sen-
tence a publication of a case. Re the extraordinary
delicacy of Melbourne dailies, “ Scribe ” is quite
too ingenuous. How about the Oscar Wilde sen-
sation? Did the sensitive editor cut out the
demoralising details, or did he crowd ’em all in,
and beat up “further particulars?” Melbourne
was not to be benefited in any way by the sensa-
tional accounts of the purple viciousness of the
author of “ Dorian Grey,” but an account of the
baboonish crimes of a local man in the position
Kennedy filled, understandable to adult citizens,
was demanded of the public press as a public
duty. When “ Scribe ” admitted that the dailies
had published reports of such cases he demolished
the one poor excuse he had for rushing into print
on this matter. “Scribe” should have signed
himself “ Circum-Scribe.” —S.S.

the truth.
* * * *
The foolishness of Acting-Governor Mad-
den, in getting himself photographed (for the
Australasian) as Commander-in-Chief-Justice of
the Victorian military and legal forces, is almost
incredible. Time that some scientist or other
turned his mind to the question of Australian
climatic and educational influences on Irish
character. In White Knight Fitzgibbon, and
Madden of the waxed mo., Melbourne contains
two Irishmen who, having all the national audacity
and irrational talking disposition, are utterly lack-
ing in their ancestors’ sense of the ridiculous. If
Madden were a Dublin man, he would laugh at
himself as the only successful lawyer on earth who
ought to have graced the floor of a draper’s shop.
Being an Australian of the brainy-athletic order,
he reckons himself a brilliant spectacle in any
capacity, and will probably be masquerading at
some function in a suit of tin armor before his pre-
sent term of Acting-Governorship has expired.
He can put his hand on his heart and say, with
Fitzgibbon, “ There is no fun in me.” But why
is this ? What is the explanation of bright Irish
“ cheek ” developing into this humorless self-con-
fidence ?

Recently the Governor of a certain Aus-
tralian gaol and his horse were both sick, and the
gaol doctor prescribed for both. Somehow the
bottles got mixed, and the horse took his boss’s
mixture and vice versa. Horse now dead ; and the
Gov. is getting well tolerably fast.

UNDER HOT SKIES.
(A North Queensland Shetch.)
Poor little thing ! A tremulous unfolding,
A fleeting dash of color, the delight
Of wakening, the wonder of beholding
Those thousand subtle mysteries the night
Of dreamless innocence had harshly hidden.
Then, all at once, a gasp of ecstasy,
A feast to which all fairy queens are bidden,
And earth and heaven coruscate in thee.
And—after that—the long, long years of fading,
When those who loved and worshipped thee in spring,
Drop slowly off, and sullenly evading
Thy hungry look, pass by, poor little thing !
VON KOTZE.
Alas ! it was sad !
When the God-fearing lad
His Sunday-school teacher saw lip the long-sleever,
From grace he fell straight,
Took to staying out late,
And drinking like any debauched unbeliever.
But, strange to relate,
This is never the fate
Of youths who see teachers, with sanctified vigor,
Lip she-pupils gay :
More pious grow they,
And cling to Religion like Death to a nigger.
