A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 10, 1898.
My Deak Myee—
The Hon. Mrs. Ferguson, a lady who has always
sacrificed herself cheerfully in charitable causes,
set off for Europe last Monday with her Majah
and her baby, and Margaret-street was blocked to
the wharf with cabs and carriages. To its mates,
the crowd owned up that it was defrauded by the
non-appearance of the vice-regal parents, whom
its delicate instincts had pictured weeping publicly
on the shoulders of the departing. Flowers filled
the cabins, and those who could not decently
attach themselves to the principal parties hung
round the nurses and the baby. Mrs. Ferguson
was neatly gowned in a navy tailor-costume, and
set an example to—well, certain decked-out brides
of “small” but moneyed people whom wo have
lately seen set out on their travels. In the
background, violent scenes went on between ladies
with families clinging round their skirts, who
couldn’t climb boxes, and so were hazy on the
vital question of the hour —did Mrs. Ferguson
cry ? These ladies fought the matter out manfully
in spite of the haze, and were as keen as over on
the subject when the Moama was out through the
sea gates, a speck on the horizon. For their own
Seace of mind, they inclined towards the tears.
Nothing hurts a crowd so sorely as a dry depar-
ture.
All the charities are awake this month and hop-
ping. The Tempe ball is under discussion, and
the Factory Girls’ ball, which comes off on May
19th, is causing many nicely-cut noses to turn up
in a way that suits them better than they guess.
The reason of this nose-lifting is that some two-
hundred shop-girls are to be allowed into the hop
at half price, and Miss Potts Point may be vis-a-
vis to the maiden who sat up the night before to
complete the hand-tucking of Miss Potts Point’s
chiffon sleeves. Society-girls are so red with
WTath and injured feelings that it wouldn’t be
kind to advise them to go soft, and remember that
their mammas have climbed into society by the
ladders of these same charity committees. Strange
to say, the men who prop up the pillars of ball-
rooms are taking a lively interest in this particu-
lar dance.
Prince Alfred Hospital Ball, last week, was a
huge success as far as the galleries were concerned.
The two-shilling on-lookers sat in their seats, and
gazed away comfortably at all there was to be
seen, and reckoned the ball was a brightly-gilt
one. But the party proper stood on one leg for an
hour and a-quarter, and watched the cotillion
appropriate all the gaud, and all the chairs, and
finally went home to catch the last train with-
out a pinch of supper in its pocket. The big
circle of chairs had entrapped the guileless
Government House party, and the supper doors
remained sealed until the last trump of the
cotillion broke up the circle at half-past eleven.
Mrs. Willie Mitchell, in her historic red and
ravens, was the smartest feature of the cotillion.
She set everyone going, and would have been a
tip-top leader if she had stopped her followers
half-an hour earlier. However, natural history
points to the fact that the followers are harder to
stop than to set going, and the ball that was not
the cotillion had better accept the inevitable. Her
invitation to the dance was for young men only,
Captain Dangar being set to entice the ladies. A
few thin and tired figures made even the ad-
miring galleries yawn, but two performances were
highly successful. One placed 30 men behind a
curtain, and 20 maidens in front of it, and the
supreme moment happened when a man had
battled madly through the curtain and punched
another man in the eye, to find himself in
the arm 3 of a 20-stoner who was a cook
in her 10-stone days. Another jolly figure,
savouring rather of horse-play, showed Mrs.
Willie whipping up a team of prancing men,
while Captain Dangar drove a dozen women
round the room. A philosophical reflection had
a feed here on the fact that the lady drove her 24
men along much more easily than the man his
dozen women. These games don’t shine as
luminously on paper as a description of kiss-in-
the-ring, and twos-and-threes; but Sunday-schools
don’t go to the same picnics as cotillions, although
you might imagine they were related if you didn’t
know better.
Towards the end of the game the Town Hall
was turned into a lively toy-shop, and mothers
rammed squeaky dolls into their pockets with
placid smiles, while family men carefully acquired
jumping-jacks and shooters for Tommy at home.
Unattached men and girls were not so happy with
their toys, and I saw a tawering regimental vainly
beseech a friend to rid him of the curly-headed
waxen beauty who cried “mama” and “papa”
whenever he touched her. One of the cruellest pre-
sentations thrust a powder-puff on a man whose
skin is a shade too dark for ordinary wear, and the
gallery got a crick in its neck when the Governor,
with a bland smile, refused Mrs. Willie’s offer of
a toy.
Both the cotillion and the ball were well-dressed
affairs, although the ball didn’t show off to as
much advantage. Mrs. Harry Levy was fixed up
in patches of heliotrope brocade, white chiffon,
blue velvet, diamonds, and other items. Mrs.
Thring garbed herself in green brocade and pop-
pies ; and Mrs. Macarthur, the other secretary,
struck blue notes over white satin. A white silk
frock was smartly trimmed with the black shoul-
der birds introduced into N.S. Wales by Mrs.
Mitchell. Two Hebraic sisters would have worn
jaunty muslin frocks more effectively if they had
reproduced each other less faithfully. The day is
done when the mother of a family bought a bale
of cloth, and attired herself and her daughters
after one image. In fact, it is not even toney to
dress twins alike, and the white frock and blue
ribbon for the fair baby and pink ribbon for the
dark has been superseded by a complete contrast
of pelisses.
Do men appreciate Hospital Saturday? is a
problem that the lady-scribe who sets Sydney
social problems for Sunday afternoons will pro-
bably be asking soon. The question was answered
in a way last Saturday by many men. “There
are no pretty women and no nice young ones on
this year,” and they buttoned up their coats, and
went home sadder and richer men. The great beg
of the year fell a little flat this time, although
the amount collected was not so far short of last
year as expected. The rain may have been at the
bottom of the mischief, for young and pretty
women won’t run inordinate risks of colds and red
noses, but it was a noticeable fact that the col-
lectors were chiefly of the type that is branded
“Good women.” They stuck to their guns like
Britons through the rain, and assurance was the
only quality they needed. It is one thing for
a man to have a box pushed meekly under his
nose, and another to be killed by a smile and then
revived by a peremptory demand for coin. Of the
two methods, even the millionaire prefers the lat-
ter, and the Domain loafer has never had
more than one opinion on the matter. The
top score was achieved by the ladies who haunted
Anthony Hordern's corner, alongside of Paddy’s
Market, where you do not find your heaviiy-
gilded capitalists, but where Mother’s swollen legs,
and Tommy’s tonsils, and little Sis’s bronchitis,
and poor Dad’s smash-up under a bale of wool,
have made the hospital a very real institution.
A woman’s point of view of this subject must
be less definite than a man’s, for the collectors let
women severely alone for the most part. But a
murmur’s in the wind, and next year’s Saturday
may set a contingent of johnnies to beg coppers
from their lady friends. The sex influence tells
even on coppers, and where’s the sense in setting
women to beg from men, and letting other women
go home unmarauded for the want of a man to
ask them ?
False-hair centred itself in chignons of abnor-
mal growth when Ouida wrote “Moths,” but
luckily the dramatist does not insist on anything
of this kind. In fact, the Cartwright co. dressed
the play on Saturday night a few months in ad-
vance of Sydney fashions. Miss Darragh, as
Fuchsia Leach, gleans the richest harvest of
frocks, and rings the changes on a green glace
silk and a white grenadine, both being made with
the big insidious flounce from the hips that stamps
all the nice gowns now. A yellow chiffon, trimmed
with little ruchings of itself, was carried elegantly.
Miss Beatrice Lamb keeps herself unspotted from
the world in white all through the play, but does
not don the most effective make-up for such color-
less confections. Yere is pale and beautiful, as
are all Ouida’s heroines, but there is pallor and
pallor—the pallor of life and the pallor of death,
for instance. The one archaic frock of the play is
worn by Gwen Dallas as Nadine. A fawn satin
bodice, and a multi colored skirt, whose pleats are
not quite old enough to be aesthetic, and far too
old to be in fashion, had reached rather an un-
comfortable stage of life for a frock.
The re-appearance of Myra Kemble, after a long
absence from the footlights, roused the house for
several minutes, and revealed the fact that the
popularity of this lady is as strong as ever. She
was innocently wicked, but her Lady Dolly was
too frugally attired for a wicked Ouida-woman,
whose simplest white muslin frocks never cost her
less than ilsoo apiece. Miss Stewart was a much
more lavish worldling as the Duchesse de Sonnaz
and looked appropriately chic and naughty in her
white hat and brown velvet pelisse, heavily
adorned with ermine. The male Moths don’t add
much to the picturesque side of the play, and, in
spite of Ouida’s famous tirade against the modern
man’s attire, they sport regulation coats, and only
one is credited with corsets.
Bland Holt and his “Life of Pleasure ” are
knocking out some happy tableaux at the Theatre
Royal. The house-boat scene appeals loudly—
Chinese lanterns, crimson carpets, window-cur-
tains, gay crowds in the cabin and on the vpper-
deok, and four professional banjos tum-tumming
cheerful tunes to the night air. What is a banjo
without a houseboat? The reversal of this truth
struck William Black long ago. He revels in
these water-houses, but never sends a party out
without a banjo, and that may be why his men and
maidens leave the heavy sentimental aione. This
instrument doesn’t strike the very bottom of the
heart-strings, even in the moonlight on the
Thames, and is nicely adapted for picnics.
Miss. Frances Ross, in a top hat and riding-
habit, is one of Du Maurier’s sketches to the life.
The long neck that pleased this artist’s eye, and
the meekly-aristocratic features, are all there.
Elizabeth Watson, whose life of pleasure is the
reason of the play, does not draw too heavily on
her dressmaker. Her early gowns are nice in a
gentle little home-made way, but home-made,
when applied to frocks, doesn’t mean made in
London, or even in Germany, though the latter’s
nearer. Like a sensible woman, she puts on her
best dress when she is going to throw a glass of
wine in her false lover’s face. This is a green
velvet princess robe, revealing green-silk under-
skirts, and the whole brightened with gold trim-
mings. Some merry-looking cloaks flaunt them-
selves round the Empire Music Hall scene, but
the best-dressed woman in the play is a walking-
lydy who never opens her lips. ‘And here a false
note strikes loudly, for a smart dress will make
the most silent woman in the world fluent.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Maddens make the mounted orderlies
attached to Govt. House work hard. They make
the most of their temporary glory, and scarcely go
anywhere without an orderly clattering in advance.
One can hardly go out at night without seeing the
carriage dashing through the city with the orderly
on in front and a paddock of boiled shirt front
gleaming from the inside of the brougham. It is
related that on an occasion during Gov. Madden’s
first performance of the part, he visited the theatre
in state and the crowd blocked the footpath when
His Waxellency wished to enter. “Orderly,”
screamed the Private Secretary, “cleah the path ! ”
“Can’t, sir,” replied the mounted trooper, touch-
ing his hat, “ it ain’t my fungshun to take my ’oss
on the footpath, and the people wouldn’t like it.”
So the sec. and his boss had to push through the
crowd, and the orderly was afterwards officially
informed that he had acted quite properly.
“On the night of drizzling May 6th, gleams of
dripping umbrella enlivened the gloom, and whiffs
of wet mackintosh flavored the air round about
the ’Varsity and College Crescent. One attraction
was a united debate between Princess Ida girls,
law students, and ’Varsity Union members ; while,
over the way, Queen’s College was billed for its
yearly concert, with the Acting-Governor and
party in the arm-chairs. At the latter function.
Mamma Madden and daughter Ruby, with their
fair hair top-knotted similarly, wore black frocks,
differentiated by a red cloak for the elder lady
and blue shoulder-straps for the younger.
“ Which reminds me, somehow, of bitternesses
engendered in progressive euchre’s early days by
misunderstandings re those terms ‘senior’ and
‘ junior.’ A man—or, worse still, a woman—would
come along and demand, in a stand-and-deliver
tone, * Are you senior or junior lady ? ’ The ques-
tioner meant, of course, ‘ Are you newly-arrived
at this table or not ? ’ But until you understood
these technicalities you probably snapped back,
‘ Shall I send for the family bible ? ‘ or ‘ I’m will-
ing t® put it to the vote if she’s not frightened ! ’
“ Straying back to the concert in the college-
hall, warmed by a hearty blaze in an open fire-
place. Between the acts people fell to studying
the wall-decorations. They were mostly Maori
or island curios., though a commanding portrait
of a dark gentleman, with long black beard,
longer black coat, and lean forefinger pointing
down to an open bible, caught my eye. Wesleyan
parson, maybe, because ‘Queen’s,’ you know,
belongs to that ilk. Anyway, no collar or cuff
showed from where I sat, and for all one knew
his rev. might have had conscientious scruples re
linen underwear, limiting himself to a decollete
short-sleeved woollen weskit.
“ But at the eleventh hour the artist must have
spotted the ‘ hidden want ’ in his picture, and
cleverly exonerated his sitter from unjust suspi-
cions by unfastening just one button of the pic-
tured coat, somewhere about the middle chest,
so as to reveal an inch of white.
“Then there was the college crest, at sixes and
sevens with its accompanying motto, like most
heraldic mixtures. What is the connection, does
anyone know, between ‘griffins rampant’ (on the
fight, presumably) and the legend ‘ We build for
eternity ? ’
“As for the performers, Fanny Lyndhurst
warbled feelingly, while Minnie Waugh and
Grace Kemp (Mrs. Fred Evans) were pleasant to
look upon, the latter wearing white brocade and
carbon buttons. Gladstone Simons’ recitations
were worth listening to ; and the hospitable col-
lege boys themselves encored everything, hustled
and bustled Barker’s big harp up and down the
platform-steps, handed ladies on and off, sang the
’Varsity anthem, nervously spilt hot coffee over
girl-guests in their studies later on, and altogether
made things go with a fizz.
“ A course of Autumn Flower Shows leads me
to the gratifying conclusion that although God
clothes iilies, chrysanthemum growers attend to
their own floral tailoring with flaunting results.
Fashion invariably bloats its objects, and chrysan-
themums have been a craze for years ; conse-
quently the gay Jap. blooms of a decade since
have swollen into glaring mammoth blobs of color
like gaudy cauliflowers. “Who drives fat
oxen should himself be fat.” The heavy, florid,
overfed affluent blooms which stare haughtily at
you from the show-tables and in the gardens and
drawingrooms of the rich, are the exact type of
the folk who grow them. Fat flowers for fat men.
But dahlias this year are the fashionable snap.
Chrysans. are out. The up-to-date dahlia has,
consequently, become more bullet-headed, more
loudly tinted, and .more stiff. It always was a
haughty vegetable, anyhow.
“Re the Kalgoorlie row, please note as a touch-
ing instance of family love that Govt. Resident
Brown, of Victoria Plains, W.A.—who wired
‘ My God, I’m proud of you 1 ’ to John Forrest in
his trouble—is a near marriage-connection of the
bullocky Premier’s. Mr. Brown’s sister is the
widow of Lady Forrest’s brother, and altogether
relationships are so complicated that if I wanted
to be popular with Perth blue-blood, I’d as soon
fly as jab any of the Browns, Hammersleys,
Lefroys, Lofties, or Forrests in the ribs with my
brolly.
“ Funny to note the air of puzzledom that stole
over a good majority of Marshall Hall’s audience,
at his Town Hall concert, when he abruptly
announced that Madame Wiederinann was too ill
to sing, didn’t say what was to follow, and dashed
his orchestra into something weird and mysterious.
Nearly everyone thought that the Prof, wouldn’t
lose a chance of airing his own composition—
hence, perhaps, some of the thunderous applause
that followed the orchestra’s splendid rendering of
Hamish M'Cann's ‘ Ship of the Fiend.’ One indi-
vidual, very much in the inner circle of Conserva-
torium things, who had been at all the Alcestis
rehearsals, gained a brief celebrity amongst half-a-
dozen hearers by pointing out the soulful meaning
of particular passages, and averring that the crash-
chord at the end meant Alcestis’ last cry. Where
is that critical knowledge of music concerning
which the culchawed few have so very much to
say?
“ Last Friday was Poverty Pointers’ day-out in
Coll ins-street. First and foremost sailed down
the Block Elliott Page, in wintry garb of rifle-
green cloth befurred, with bunches of violets stuck
about her, escorted by Marcus Napoleon Knight.
Next sensation was a beflowered hansom, the
horse with a garland of chrysans. round his lean
neck, and inside more flowers—it was like a short
funeral for flowers. More posies; also Bert Gil-
bert, who had drunk his last glass as Uncle the
night before, and Ada Peeve, all smiles. These
two stars haven’t gone with the opera team to
Westralia.
“ Great excitement the last night of ‘ The
Babes.’ The performers, big and little, played-
up like two-year-olds. Dollies, in the toy-ballet,
came down like ninepins. A ‘ robin ’ turned over
on her ankle, and looked like squashing the sleep-
ing orphans. Little Gulliver, snubbed by Maid
Marion for his * sauce ’ re her bloomers, jumped
up and tried to snatch a kiss. Pa Gilbert put a
stop to his daughter’s pertness by picking her up
and carrying her off like a baby.
“ The audience felt sad but tried to bear up.
When Ada Reeve knocked up against a man in
her brown-velvet ‘ twicers,’ and offered her king-
dom for a petticoat, the wished-for garment, such
a nice, useful pink-flannel one, descended from
Heaven, or, at all events, from the gods. Also
the frisky favorite was loaded with floral boats,
lyres, and buttonholes galore. Hubby got pipe,
walking-cane, and, like the rufflans, a bottle or
two. A big blue-satin bon-bon, ‘ pulled ’by the
baron and his daughter, held chains and chains of
flowers, which Miss Reeve hung round her neck.
“ There were such lots of weird, wild laughers
in the audience that night. A stout woman in
the d.c. ‘ took on ’ terribly at the wet-nurse joke.
During her paroxysms, her neighbors heard bodice-
seams go off like crackers, and by the end of the
evening all her frisettes, and most of her hair-
pins, had slipped down her back. Miss Elliot-
Page, all ruched with red chiffon, looked on from
a box with Julius Knight and Baritone Pauli. A
bride and bridegroom, just back from the honey-
moon, came along with the party, the heroine of
the group all in black from the crest of funereal
feathers in her golden hair downwards.
“ After the performance forlorn young men
stood about outside disputing the rival charms of
the ‘jelly,’ ‘champagne’ and ‘salad’ girls, and
vowed that if it meant one o’clock, they’d see Ada
Reeve come out. One reason of that bright star’s
popularity is the graciousness of her gift-reception
tactics. She never overlooks the shabbiest flower,
and t’other night when a solitary Virginian-
creeper leaf fluttered timidly from aloft, she wel-
comed it as warmly as the rest. She likes
chronicling her booty, too—the diamond bangle,
the canary-bird, and what not. Hubby’s bag, so
far, includes a gun and a drinking-flask.
“ At the Royal, ‘ The Work Girl ’ (Eugenie*
Duggan) was sweated according to promise on
Saturday night, and pocketed her starvation wage
in the presence of a sympathetic multitude. Or
square-headed spectator enquired from his seat A
the audience how long Eugenie had worked for
her 3s. 6d., and, getting no answer, withheld his
hoot from her employers till the facts should be
placed before him. Later on, the worritced work-
girl, vexed by a false lover and a child of shame,
jumps into the Thames and is rescued by her long-
iost father. The latter carries her unconscious to
her garret, whence she soon emerges neat as a new
pin, with hair freshly crimped and dainty col-
larette and cufflets, to tell her shocked parent her
tale of woe. Ida Gresham is effective in her
greeny silk evening frock with lots of bare
shoulder framed in an all-round ruching of
violets. Somehow the naughty girls of melo-
drama are passing fond of the 1830 bodice. And
small blame to them, for their anatomy, unlike
their stage morals, is generally beyond reproach.
“Seems to me that lover Montgomery, of ‘ The
Work-Girl,’ models his deportment on that of
Aide Richard Neville. Even when his jealous
wife and irate pa-in -law switch his old love round
the drawing-room, he hangs his arms meekly at his
sides after the pattern of his supposed exemplar.
But if Lord Richard’s young woman were being
spanked for nothing, he’d probably change his
tactics. Circumstances alter cases.

A new rule decided upon by N.S.W.
Cabinet at the instigation of Premier Reid, to
apply in all future cases, is that a Supreme Court
Judge may obtain leave of absence on full pay for
six months, but any extension of that period must
be at ordinary pension-rates. Hitherto a Supreme
Court Judge obtained six months’ leave at jB2GOO
and then applied for another three or six months
and usually got it on the same terms. In future
the extended term will be at the rate of £lB2O.
The late Francis Longmore was a N.S.W.
man in the first instance. From 1839 (when, as a
lad of 13, he came over from Ireland) until 1852
young Longmore tilled the soil at Wollongong.
The gold-fever drove him to Ballarat.
The three N.S.W. District Court Judges
now on pension served—Wilkinson, 33 years;
M'Farland, 30 years; and Dowling, 38 years.
The pension is £750 a year, and in the cases
stated has been running for five, seven, and eight
years.
The Mrs. Weatherley who died at Yass,
N.S.W., the other day, at the age of 82, was a
sufferer in the Gundagai flood of half-a-century
ago. A boatman rescued her just as her house
was floating away. Over 70 buildings were car-
ried away, and 89 persons in Gundagai alone were
drowned on that occasion.

A proposal that Ben Tillett should be in-
vited to speak at Healesville (Vic.), roused a local
councillor to move an amendment that the dogs
should be sooled on Ben if he were seen anywhere
thereabouts. When a man knows his own
deficiencies in logic it is probably the wisest thing
he can do to keep a dog to argue for him.
An old, unscrubbed man who died in Melb.
last week with bank deposit receipts for £lOOO
sewn into his loathsome rags, claimed to be the
lawful heir to a certain Hazelbrae property in
Scotland. Satisfactory to know, however, that he
couldn’t prove his claim. The property has re-
mained in the hands of people who, presumably,
wash themselves.
Chief Justice Madden has—thank the Lord !
—decided to “ weigh his words more carefully
in future,” and refrain from drawing comparisons
between Shakespeare and Mark T vain. If
Madden were to make a practice of weighing the
same lot of words at regular intervals he would
be surprised to note their steady loss in weight
through evaporation.
Mrs. Phil May (writes London correspon-
dent of Sydney D.T.) ought to be a happy woman.
She has been done into a bronze bust by Bertram
Mackennal, for exhibition in this year’s Royal
Academy, and her portrait is included in the new
series of “ Types of English Beauty,” which “ can
be obtained at all respectable stationers,” etc., in
London and the provinces.
The demised Longmore, Vic. ex-M.P.,
had during his first term a Parliamentary hench-
man, one Cope, a clean-shaven, comedian-looking,
facetious local preacher, who, in a yellow ban-
danna neckcloth, sat hunched up in the corner,
emitting Hancockian interjections. One night
Longmore said—“ I ask, Mr. Speaker, what class
of immigrants the Government would introduce?”
Cope : “ Porpoises ! ”
Q. Governor Lamington may yet fill a big
place in British politics. He is in the English
inner circle, is a personal friend of Curzon, who
was his “ best man,” and is a better speaker than
most of the lords who occupy front positions. His
style recalls that of Hartington ; but he probably
has more brains than Hartington, has a finer wit,
and is more human. Indeed, he is very human ;
he would be a gigantic success in Q. if find
the missing words.

“I suppose you'd be sorry if I were to die?"
“Of course I would. You know I look a perfect guy in a

S.A.’s lost Rechabite, who has now been
missing along with the cash for many months, is
still heard of at intervals. He has been “seen ”
at Melbourne and at Sydney by people who didn’t
report the matter till he was gone, and a Melb.
hair-dresser who shaved off his respectable beard
also knew him, but kept dark till some time after-
wards. He is now supposed to be overlanding to
Brisbane, but nobody seems to tear after him at
any pace worth mentioning. The unanimity with
which people who spot the much-wanted Rechabite
walking peacefully up and down big cities,
decide to say nothing about the matter till he is
gone, is truly remarkable. Does anybody really
want that walking Rechabite ?
Charles Clements, recently hanged for
wife-murder at Dunedin (M.L.), died like a com-
fortable fat man without any sign of emotion.
When arrested he was a gaunt, emaciated prisoner,
but he fattened wonderfully in gaol, and, after his
sentence in particular, he laid on flesh at an alarm-
ing rate. He finished up with a large breakfast,
and the only remark of any importance (apart
from a casual oath) which he made on the scaffold
was, “Don’t let me drop all at once.” But there
is no way of hanging anyone by instalments, so
they dropped the fat man simultaneously, and,
after he had bounced once or twice, death, in the
beautiful language of the daily paper, “was in-
stantaneous.” t
John Haynes, M.L.A. :
About Charley Collins, M.L.A., recently dead. Please
don’t let it be inferred—as it might be from your recent
par. —that I was preparing Mr. Collins’ farewell address
to the electors when the news of his death arrived.
Collins wanted no such assistance, being a hard-headed,
able man with broad political views. He could give
points in politics to our very best men, and was no drone,
no slavish follower of the biggest side ; no billet-hunter
or log-roller. I always found him a plucky voter, and
absolutely independent. As a Jew he was a Christian,
and as a Christian he was no “ Jew,” for he was always
staunch with the people. What Collins asked me to do
for him was merely to revise his “ screed ” for suggestions
or corrections. As for myself, Charles will know I do
him justice here, and perhaps in return he will some day
put in a word for me with Father Abraham.
The late Francis Longmore (Vic.) while
one of the kindest of men, held on to some hatreds
as a limpet clings to a rock. One of his theories
was that Fatman was sure to fry in the next
world. “I’m disinclined,” he said on one occa-
sion, “to believe in the dogma of an eternal
punishment. It seems paradoxical, if we admit
the justice and the mercy also of the Creator.
And yet, when I contemplate that ogre of our
civilisation, the land-monopolist, and recognise
that society owes so much of its woes and misery
to his baneful influence, I cannot well see how we
can banish the theory of hell from our faith. For,
surely no other punishment equivalent to the
enormity of the land grabbers’ offence is con-
ceivable, than that of an eternal wasting m the
flames of hell.” A good old man, of simple
faith.
Since Ben Tillett’s advent to Melbourne
many of the Labor-leaders and Trades Hall people
have much changed their style of elocuting. In-
stead of each speaker going on his “ own ” and
evincing a distinct individuality, they all now—
with but scant success—model pronunciation, ex-
pression and gesture upon the little man. Stephen
Barker, J.P.—he never signs without the final
letters—president of the Trades Hall, is about
Ben’s most slavish imitator, and has adopted all
Tillett’s faults without acquiring any of his
merits. Ben pronounces “knowledge” “no-
ledge ” ; so does Barker. Ben chews up his open-
ing sentences as if he were just finishing a tasty
mouthful; Barker begins as if he had a large, hot
potato in his mouth. Ben wastes about lOsec.
between some of his sentences in a fidgetting “ er
—er”-ing, and Barker runs it to about 40. Ben
himself must be tired of this “ sincerest form of
flattery.”
Hanson, the recently - de-
ceased Sydney printer, was once
partner with Sam Bennett in the
old Empire. Bennett used to
lunch with Hanson at the latter’s
house. But when the two old
chums had quarrelled, Hanson,
remembering how he used to feed
Bennett every day, actually sued
Sam for 400 luncheons at a shil-
ling each. Bennett defended and
got a verdict with costs. And
Hanson, always a melancholy kind
of bird, looked from that day
forth as though he were taking
it out of his own personality
to make up for the losses he
had incurred in nourishing Sam
Bennett.

Verily the old order changeth. Sixty
years ago Hone Heke, in a war mantle of kiwi
feathers, the mer 4 of his chiefship in his hand, led
his be-plumed and tattooed warriors against the
forces of Kuine Wikatoria, and succeeded in sack-
ing the European settlement of Kororarika — the
brothel of the Pacific as it was called. To-day we
read of his grand-nephew, another Hone Heke,
attired in the high hat and broad-cloth of the
pakeha, with pakeha words meaning “member of
Parliament ” tacked to his name, a pleasant-faced,
copper-colored gentleman with flashing white
teeth and a flower in his button-hole, hasting by
train and steamer to act as pacificator in a dispute
between his truculent tribesmen and the pale-
faced children of the civilisation into which he has
been adopted. Catch old Hone Heke the First
stopping a tight or any “ divarsion ” of that sort !
Fancy the scorn of the disembodied spirit of the
grim old warrior as he contemplates the degeneracy
of his modern kinsman who, instead of being noted
as a skull-breaking, blood-drinking leader in haka
and toa, is chiefly famous for the possession of
one of the nicest tenor voices in all Maoriland !

Scotty Stirling : “ The recent bowling-out
of the Maorilander Burr, by the duty-stamp that
could not have been printed before 1882, reminds
of one Alexander, who forged an excerpt of a
charter of novodamus, whereby he was entitled to
a little property, including Nova Scotia, all the
American fisheries, the right of creating knights
and baronets of Nova Scotia, tremendous posses-
sions in Great Britain, Ireland, and the Isle of
Man, and the earldom of Stirling. Unfortunately,
he did it on the back of a French map of Nova
Scotia, dating it two years before the map could
have been published. The fraud was detected by
the interpolation of a line on the lithographic-
stone, but the Scotch verdict of ‘ Not proven ’
was recorded at the trial. N. 8.—1 wish be had
got it, because I, Scotty, am a nearer relative than
Alexander to the attainted earl, and I would cer-
tainly have made The Bulletin a baronet. Vide
Samuel Warren’s miscellanies, the ‘Romance of
Forgery.’”

LASER B€€R.
Aboriginalities.
Quarantine is a great institution. Recent
investigations proved that the tick can live on
almost every “ critter ” that flies and every snaky
calamity that crawls. Beetles and flies are found
to be swarming with the pest —and beetles and
flies respect no quarantine line. Euphemism at
present in vogue in N.Q. : “There ain’t no ticks
on Thompson’s gal.”
* * * *
Illicit distillation is about the most profit-
able bush industry ; half the pubs, would close if re-
stricted to the sale of duty-paid spirit. As shown
by the small number of prosecutions, the risk is
small with reasonable precautions. A blend of
honey and corn makes a good ferment ; both are
home products and save the nasty enquiries aroused
by large purchases of molasses. The new worm-
less still is a great improvement. The use of
charcoal prevents smoke. A good dog voices a
stranger half-a-mile off. “ Heaven and Hell ”
fittingly names the product.

Jinda : “ Whether Wragge’s assistants,
stationed at Kosciusko, will be able to remain there
through the winter is much doubted by men who
have lived on the Snowy. Writer spent ’97
fossicking and ’possum shooting within a few
miles of Kosciusko and, although the winter was
exceptionally mild—in fact Kiandra could not
hold its snow-shoe meeting that year, on account
of the snow on the champion course not being
sufficiently deep—found the snow and wind storms
altogether too rough. Was also one of the party
which made the first winter ascent, on 19th Aug.
last. A more bleak or exposed position could
hardly be imagined, and, although the height is
nothing out of the way (7,600 ft.), the inaccessi-
bility of the station, and the prevalence of high
gales, will render the observers’ stay a perisher.
Apropos of the winter ascent : A daring exploit
by one of the party has, so fax', not appeared in
print. With the exception of one, all the party
left their shoes at the foot of the last 500 ft. pinch.
McAllister, who manages a mine on the Grey
Mare Range, carried his ‘ski ’to the summit. After
the inevitable photography, the rest of the tourists
were astounded to see Mac. jump on his shoes, and
start from alongside the cairn. The snow was
frozen in patches on the side of the hill, and the
way in which those shoes jumped 10 and 12 ft. at
a stretch, was enough to make the oldest ‘ riders ’
squirm. I’ve done the quarter mile in 23 secs,
myself, but I’m prepared to say Mac. did over a
mile a minute. He reached the bottom safely, but
could not pull up for at least another half mile, out
on the plain. ‘Wouldn’t tackle it again for
*BlOO 1 ’ said Mao. when we rejoined him. ”

Dear Bulletin, Talking
of ohopping-matehes, the most
remarkable match I have seen
took place in the Kahikateanui
Block (M.L.) in ’7l, when Cana-
dian Pete, Long Joe, and Mick
the Tassy put up £3O among them
for the man who could get a 4ft.
pine down first. When they
came on the ground each man
had with him something beside
his axe. Pete had a long-handled
shovel, Long Joe had a bucket of
water, and Mick had a packet of
candles and a tin box of matches.
What on earth they wanted with
these we couldn’t guess, and we
had some doubt as to whether
any tools beyond the axe were
allowable ; but as each man had
provided himself with something
“not in the bond,” we let them
all go ahead. We took little in-
terest in the matter till we
judged they would be “ taking
down their second chip,” and
then we went in to inspect.
There was Pete, with his axe
stuck in the tree, shovelling away
for dear life at a pile of chips
which had risen nearly to his hips
and stopped him from swinging
his axe. We went on to find Long
Joe dipping his axe in the bucket
of water after each second stroke
(chip, chip, dip —chip, chip, dip)
with the regularity of a clock.
“ What was he dipping his axe
for ? ” Why, to prevent it get-
ting red-hot and burning the tree
down instead of chopping it, of
course. That w. uld have dis-
qualified him. We plunged
through the supplejacks to see
what show the Tassy had against
two such heroes. There was
Michael, chipping away with a
candle burning in his “scarf.”
What did he want the candle
for ? ” Why, to see with. His
chips were flying so fast that they
darkened the sun. Naturally,
Mick’s tree was down first. And
Maoriland is also a great country.
—Adah.

Dear Bulletin. —May not
excessive “bunkiness” have some-
thing to do with the Great Aus-
tralian Apathy ? Outside the
larger cities, three-fourths of the
population read, write, and have
their spells while lying down.
Chairs are usually of conspicuous
not-thereness, and so much has
the bunk-habit grown into our
life that even the casual “spel-
ler ” by the roadside invariably
stretches out in preference to
sitting. Bunks are not handy
things to sit in. The new-chum
tries it at first, but always succumbs to the custom
<»f the country. Also, if excessive tea-drinking is
individually injurious, what collective effect must
it have upon such a tea-swilling community as the
Australians ? —Derelict. [Never stand up if you
can sit down, never sit down if you can lie down,
never lie down if you can be dead and remain
down permanently.—Ed. B.]

On Wanganui River steamer. Potatoes
wanted. Cook goes ashore with two buckets, digs
potatoes up, fills buckets and brings to steamer.
Captain hands cook bottle; cook goes back to
where he dug potatoes, stands bottle on a mound.
As steamer goes round river bend, aged white
man is seen sitting on mound and swigging from
bottle. Left sitting.

In the old Wellington (M.L.) public-house
dayo, when it was drinks for everything, drinks
for every game of euchre or crib, &c., the “ regu-
lars ” used to get so full that they couldn’t hold
any more. But pubby was even with them if
they wanted to stay on, which was generally the
case, as they couldn’t negotiate the badly-lighted
streets when so “ tanked.” He used to issue
bronze tokens, each representing one drink ; so
that the swollen-headed “regular” of the night
before could present same in the morning—without
additional expense—for his pick-me-ups. But,
after he had got through his second or third pick-
up, he was generally booked for the rest of the
day, and by the time he was shot-out next morn-
ing he would be possessed of more tokens. Things
have altered.

They were talking plough in the bar, and
when the gorgeous commercial offered there-and-
then to take up all the local ploughing champions
they commenced “ chucking-off ” at the idea
of the immaculately - clad drummer turning
a furrow. “Well, money talks,” said the
man of samples. “ I’ll make a match against the
three of you, for a sweepstake of a fiver. You’ve
got a plough in the paddock next door, and we’ll
plough over to a lamp on the other side. Let the
landlord judge in the morning.” It looked too
good to miss, so the yokels raised the money in
the township, and, the stakes being lodged with
the publican, all hands adjourned to the paddock.
A big lantern was left against the further fence,
and the drummer peeled off his frock-coat, hung
Ids bell-topper on the fence, and waded in first.
The three local men followed—each privately con-
vinced that he had, at least, badly beaten the man

Ed. Bulletin. —It is printed that the
true barramundi exists only in the Dawson.
Allow me to express my belief that it is found in
all the Gulf (of Carpentaria) waters. I have my-
self caught the giant perch with its distinctive
orown spots on either side of the head in the
Walsh and the Mitchell Rivers. The Walsh,
which in summer is nothing but a chain of clear
sand-bottomed water-holes, is particularly rich in
fish, and at the height of an old-man drought the
steadily shrinking holes are literally alive with
fish, big and little. — Kotz.
* * * *
S.C. : “ Have noticed in Bulletin much
discussion re ‘bung-eye.’ It may be the same
disease that attacks residents of Samoa, Tonga,
Tahiti, Fiji, and other tropical islands. In Fiji
it is called Cika (pronounced Theeka). Rife in
summer ; children are the worst sufferers. Natives
say it is caused by the seed blown off the reeds ;
this starts itching and causes running or blear-eye,
which flies further inflame until both eyes are
swollen so that the sufferer becomes blind. The
only cure is to remain in a dark room for
about a week with bandaged eyes. All S. Sea
flies are poisonous. If they alight on the smallest
cut, and are allowed to remain, that cut will be-
come a serious sore. A mosquito-bite, if left alone,
won’t be felt next day ; but, if scratched and a fly
gets at it, it will take weeks to heal.

Wellington: “Have just finished a tour
in the districts where the ‘ Maori comes from ’
(between Wanganui and Tokaanu), and noted
with interest some of the poetic romance attached
to this beautiful but fast-decaying race. Heard a
honeymooning couple complaining one morning to
an accommodation-house proprietor that their
cooings had been disturbed by a shock-headed, un-
sober native bursting into the nuptial chamber
and wanting to raffle a pipe. In another place a
friend was suffering from a stiff back, and had the
affected part rubbed with whisky, some of which
was left in a pannikin by his bedside. In the
morning a friendly native came in and, smelling
the spirit, asked what it was. ‘Whisky,’ said my
friend. ‘Me try,’ and the darkie tossed off the
liniment with gusto, patting the place where his
waistcoat ought to be. In Pipiriki, where the
Maoris are celebrated for their chorus-singing, a
party of tourists made up their minds to hear a
sample, and, after arranging terms (mostly for
stimulants at the finish), a young musical chief
led the songs. The first 10 verses were fairly good
native style —usual monotonous chant—but the
11th and last was undoubtedly : ‘Come, landlord,
fill te flowin’ powl, until it does flow obar. For
to-night we merry merry pe. For to-night we
merry merry pe. To-morrow we pe sobar.’ In
fact, the Maori thinks of nothing but liquor in
connection with pale faces.”
* * • *
The locomotive in Bananaland is subject
at times to various limitations. Last year a
train on the Northern line was stopped by cater-
pillars that had gathered by millions on the rails.
The consequent greasing had so minimised fric-
tion that progress was impossible. A month ago
a train on the Southern and Western line was
blocked by i/rass. A heavy wind had piled the
loose, dry herbage over the rails to such an extent
that the train had to come to a standstill. Any-
one who has seen the grass accumulated against a
fence in the Western country during a stiff breeze
can easily understand this.

Things are deteriorating woefully in the
bush. A sw aggie was trudging along the road,
bearing his burden as cheerfully as a temperature
of lOodeg. in the shade would allow him, when a
cocky drove by in a spring-cart. “Give us your
swag,” said Pumpkins, kindly; “I’ll carry it to
town for you for four bob.”' “No, thanks, old
man.” Cocky drove on, but shortly turned back.
“ Look here, mate. Blow me, if I don’t give you
a lift for two bob I ” Offer again declined; and
Pumpkins departed crestfallen and curseful.

“ Narrogin ” (W.A.) : The settlers here
talk glibly of lakes, swamps, and l’ivers, but for
months one might ride through the district and
never see a pint of water. Nevertheless, until
some 25 years ago game of all sorts abounded, and
the lake country was the happy hunting-ground
of innumerable blacks, with whom the early settlers
had much trouble until the measles broke out and
swept the blacks off the face of the earth. When
the disease was upon him, the poor, ignorant
savage always made straight for the water to cool
his heated carcase, with inevitable fatal conse-
quences. The shores of Parkering Lake were
soon strewn with the dead bodies of the victims,
and about this time several settlers missed their
pigs. As these, however, were in the habit of
foraging for themselves in the marshes and swamps,
little note was taken of their absence; but one
day, when a drove of pigs turned up looking sleek
and fat, their owner’s suspicions w(?re aroused.
He took a ride round, and there, sure enough,
along the lake shore were the dismembered limbs
and torn carcases of the dead blacks upon which
the pigs had been feasting.

The danger of amateur corn-carving has
found another instance in the case of the Mrs.
Jackson whose abduction by her own husband
made her famous a few years back. The husband
had started, immediately after the marriage, for
Australia, and, when he returned to England,
the lady would have none of him. Finding
that, as a suitor for conjugal restitution,
the law would have none of him either, hubby
promptly kidnapped the unwilling one and de-
tained her in a house to which her relatives vainly
laid siege. Then they took to the law, and habeas
corpus scored again. Jackson had to “ disgorge ”
Mrs. J. and revert to the anomalous position of
not being able to have either his wife or a divorce.
And now Mrs. J., having tinkered with a corn,
has died in a couple of days of blood -po i son in g.

A Far Western correspondent to The
Bulletin
John Forrest's long-deferred visit to Esperance Bay and
Norseman has at last come off, but the policy of wait-a-
while has effectually snuffed out the claims of both places
to consideration on the grounds of commercial and
mining importance. Two years ngo Esperance was a
bustling port, and the Norseman a busy reefing field.
Now the Bay township consists of a desolate collection of
iron shanties backed by a circle of sand-hills, and the few
remaining inhabitants take in each other’s washing for a
living. Esperance residents deserved a better fate. They
used to hold champagne banquets on the slightest provo-
cation ; the arrival of a steamer always meant a glorious
jamboree, and in between whiles the few business places
would close with unanimity, while bosses and em-
ployes combined to paint the “ town ” scarlet by way of
relief to the deadly monotony of sand and iron. The
loss of the services of the “local correspondent” struck
Esperance hard. He was a person of vivid imagination,
who out of a couple of citizens on a day’s fishing could
evolve a harbor excursion of 500 holiday - makers.
Norseman is in a state of dismal stagnation. Even the
presence of “ IJitlander” Sampson with his monocle fails
to relieve the dull grey of depression. But for the occa-
sional excitement over some mine-manager’s discovery of
a new lie to wire to Adelaide the casual visitor might
imagine he had struck a Cuban village after passing
through the gentle hands of Weyler.
The Wunderlich Ceiling and Roofing Co.,
of Sydney, has been fortunate in securing, as head
of its designing department, Mr. Phil Goatcher,
whose beautiful decorative work is so well-known
in connection with the Art Decorative Co. —now
incorporated with the Wunderlich.*

A New History of the Discovery
of Australia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cardinal Moran, in his “History of the Church
in Australia,” has made the remarkable discovery
that Port Curtis, Q., was the identical spot De
Quiros landed at in 1606, and christened “Tierra
Austral del Espiritu Santo.” This land, for very
good reasons, has always been held to be the
largest of the New Hebrides group, but Card.
Moran places it as mentioned, and claims that
there, nearly three centuries ago, the first Mass
was celebrated in Australia.
Such an assertion by a lesser man, in a lesser
work, would have excited only passing ridicule,
but coming from such a quarter many people have
taken it so seriously that it becomes v th while
to show the utter absurdity of it. The Cardinal
begins rather artfully by stating that the degrees
of latitude, in the published narratives of those
days, were often purposely misleading. But, for
all that, he seems himself to accept them as cor-
rect when it suits him.
Cardinal Moran states that there are extant
three descriptions of this land of Espiritu Santo —
those of Torres, De Quiros, and Don Jnan Arias.
In point of fact there are a good many more;
besides many letters on the subject. Juan
de Iturbe, the accountant of De Quiros,
wrote a long account; so did the chief pilot;
so did Don Diego de Prado, and others.
In seeking for justification for his Port Curtis
theory, the Cardinal seems to lean mostly on the
narratives of De Quiros and Don Juan Arias,
whereas the geographers prefer the unvarnished
tale of Torres. In fact, there is no comparison
between Quiros and Torres, and the exaggerated
estimation in which the former is now held is in-
explicable. Torres was a sailor and a leader;
Quiros w’as a dreamy, religious enthusiast. They
left Callao with instructions to go as far south
as latitude 40deg. They went as far as 26deg.
and then, against the advice of Torres, sailed
northward. Mutiny and disaffection showed
themselves. Torres advocated the sharp measures
of those days. Quiros “ placed an order on the
main-mast that no one should swear or take the
name of God in vain, under penalty of a fine of a
patacon for the departed souls.” Needless to say,
this did not have much effect. Several of the
chief officers went on board the Almirante, the
ship commanded by Torres, disgusted with the
want of firmness shown by Quiros. Don Diego
de Prado, writing to the king from Goa,
sending maps of Torres’ voyage, says: “That
which was discovered by Pedro Fernandez Quiros,
the liar, were those rocks and small islands,
because his crew mutinied at the bay of the island
of Espiritu Santo.” He furthermore calls him “ a
prattler ” and several other derogatory names.
Juan de Iturbe, writing from Mexico, accuses
Quiros of having neglected his opportunities, and
discovered nothing more than islands which were
known before. In addition, he ridicules him for
having established the order of Espiritu Santo,
with habits of blue taffeta, and given liberty to
two negro coolies who were not his to liberate.
The Cond6 de Temos was of the opinion that
Quiros had got it into his head that he was to be
another Columbus, which was his weakness.
THE FAIR SEX.
BENEVOLENT PERSON (to young gin) : “Is that
Your little one?"
VICTORIA : “No; him belonga the other lady."
This seems to show that Quiros had not much
honor in his own time ; in fact, Torres scarcely
veils his contempt for him. He had one friend,
who “ damned him with faint praise a priest,
Fray Luis Alioga, who said that he was a good
Christian and a well-intentioned man.
Cardinal Moran, however, believes in Quiros
and in Don Juan Arias. The latter says that the
land was populous—the natives superior to the
general run of black races ; and that there were
plantations of trees and many kinds of delicious
fruits. There were lofty mountains in the back-
ground, and two large rivers fell into the bay—
one as large as the Guadalquiver, and the other
not quite so broad. The description given by
Torres will come in its turn. That of Quiros is
very glowing. It states that there were several
beautiful islands, seven of the principal ones ex-
tending 200 leagues ; and one of these in particular,
distant only 12 leagues from the harbor, was about
50 leagues in circumference.
Now for the Cardinal. “All these details fit
in admirably with Port Curtis, on the Queensland
coast, in the 24th degree of south latitude. The
trend of the land from east to west, the row of
islands in front of the harbor, the large Curtis
Island at a distance of a few miles, all correspond
to the harbor of Holy Cross, in which De Quiros
cast anchor (?) The Dawes Range and other
mountains are seen in the distance. But a yet
more striking coincidence is found in the rivers.
Two important (?) rivers flqvv into Port Curtis—
the Calliope and ” (here the Cardinal has made a
The ship HEREWARD, sent ashore by the gale at Maroubra Beach , near Sydney.
Photo., W. Johnson, William-st., Sydney.
slip, and put in “ Burnett ” instead of “ Boyne ”).
The latter has three large tributaries —the Auburn,
Boyne, and Barambah—and when, with their
united waters, it enters the sea, it is in every way
equal to the great river—the Guadalquiver. Now,
as it happens, the Calliope and Boyne are not at
all important rivers, and the united normal flow
of fresh water in them would not much more than
fill a plunge-bath. Auburn and Barambah Creeks
have dry, sandy beds. The Guadalquiver can’t be
much of a river if it can’t go one better than the
Boyne.
But we will give Torres a chance. Here is his
account of the finding of Espiritu Santo: “At
the end of that time there wore opinions that, as
it was winter, we should not go beyond 14deg.
latitude, although my opinion was always very
much to the contrary. It was decided that we
should seek the islands named by the Indians of
Taomaco; so we left this neighborhood, steering
to the west, and, after a day’s sail, we discovered
a volcano, very high and thick, more than three
leagues in circumference, very thickly wooded,
and with black inhabitants with thick beards. To
the west, and in sight of this volcano, at the dis-
tance of eight leagues, there was an island not
very high, and very pleasant to look at.” (Here
a short passage about—“ Leaving this, and going
southward towards this large island that we saw,
we discovered in it a very large bay, well-in-
habited and very fertile, with yams and many
fruits, pigs, and fowls. ”)
This was Espiritu Santo, and most people
will acknowledge the faithful resemblance
to Port Curtis. Even the pigs and fowls
are present to this day. Torres goes
• on : “ All these people are black
and naked ; they fight with ar-
rows, javelins, and large clubs.
. . . The bay is very fresh,
and has many and large
rivers; it is in 15§ lati-
tude, and will have 25
leagues circumference.”
Here the ships parted
company, De Quiros
sailing away in the
middle of the night
and • finally turning
up at Acapulco. Exit
Quiros as far as the
Bay of St. Phillip and
St. James and the
founding of the New
Jerusalem were con-
cerned. The further
details of his voyage
are not pertinent.
With Torres it is
very different. He
waited and looked
about for, he says, 50
days, then held a
council and started.
“I intended todouble
the island ” (Espiritu Santo), “but the weather did
not permit, though I ran along a large part of it,
in which I saw very high mountain ranges. It
has many ports . . . throughout there is much
water and rivers full of water.” Torres then
sailed south-west, and continues : “ By the south-
west course I did not find any signs of land ”
(according to Cardinal Moran, he ought not to
have found any signs of salt water). “ Thence I
turned to the north-north-west as far as lldeg.
and a half. Here I fell in with the beginning of
New Guinea, the coast of which trends from east
to west, a quarter norjth-west and south-east.”
It will thus be seen that for Torres to sail on
the course he did would have necessitated his
striking inland at once from Port Curtis. Although
these tactics are often adopted by modern coast-
ing steamers, they were not in vogue in the days
of the discovery ships. On his south-west and
nor’-nor’-west course Torres would have done a
good deal of overlanding before he got to sea
again. Moreover, Gladstone and the Louisiades,
where Torres struck Papua, are not far apart
in longitude. How about the volcano only
eight leagues from the first island which was in
sight of the one they christened Espiritu Santo ?
How about the arrows, the fruit, the abundance
of fresh-water rivers and high mountains ? Well,
the Cardinal wipes these discrepancies away with
a bold sweep. De Quiros states that they erected
a cross, and built a church to our Lady of Loretto.
Twenty Masses were said there, and the men
flocked to share the indulgence (they were in a
state of semi-mutiny at this time). There was
also a. procession which sanctified the land by its
presence.
“ These words,” says Cardinal Moran, “ appear
to set at rest all controversy as regards the first
celebration of Holy Mass on the Australian Con-
tinent. ”
With regard to the relics of a Spanish vessel,
said to have been found at Port Curtis, there is
nothing very strange in this, for the timbers of
more than one have been found on the Barrier
Reef further north. The ships of the Pacific ports
of New Spain could easily come to grief on the
unknown Queensland coast, if they were blown
south of their course. If Cardinal Moran has, as
he claims, settled the question by the account De
Quiros gives of the first Mass, ho has also, ipso
facto, established the hitherto unsuspected fact
that Torres was the first overlander. There is no
escape from the d ilemma.
ERNEST FAVENG.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed in,
and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

SOME VICTORIAN NOTIONS.


Eight-Hours’ Day in Melbourne roused the
Age to a leading article in which it congratulated
the workers on their affluence and comfort. Also,
it urged them to be peaceful and contented, and
turn a deaf ear with a large plug of cotton in it to
agitators who want them to adopt “untried nos-
trums ” for the further improvement of their con-
dition.” This fine, gouty, port-wined conservatism
is very comfortable for Fat Man—only if the
world had always been contented, and everybody
had carefully avoided untried nostrums, David
Syme would be a stark naked savage to this day.
And the general opinion of those who know the
large, bony, knobby David is that without his
clothes on he would be a frightful spectacle.
Mr. T. H. Kelly, who leads the financial
wing of N.S.W. Anti-Con ventionists, is reported
to be the most heavily insured “ life ” in Aus-
tralia.
Warren, South Australian M.L.C., and
one of the agricultural experts of the wheat pro-
vince, made a careful comparison of Westraliaand
Queensland before selecting in the former for his
sons ; and he has written to the W.A. department
setting out the reasons for his preference. His
only complaint is that the oountry is “rather
woody.” On the other hand, J. R. Kelly, ex-M.P.
of S.A., and another acknowledged agricultural
expert, has made choice of Bananaland.

BANK OF AUSTRALASIA.
(INCORPORATED BY ROYAL CHARTER, 1835.)
Paid-up capital .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £1,600,000
Reserve fund (£500,000, invested in consols at 95, the remainder being used in the
business) .. .. .. .. .. 800,000
Reserve liability of proprietors .. .. .. .. ~ 1,600,000
SIXTY-FOURTH ANNUAL REPORT of the DIRECTORS, with the Accounts for the Half-year to’ 11th OCTOBER
1897, Presented to the Proprietors at the ANNUAL GENERAL MEETING, held on Thursday, 31st March, 189 S.
R B F* O R T.
The directors submit to the proprietors the. balance-sheet as at 11th October last, with the profit account for the
half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and colonial rates and taxes, and for
all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year amounted to £40,278 7s. Id. The addition of £17,997 7s. Id
brought forward from the previous half-year, gives a disposable balance of £58,275 14s. 2d., of which the dividend
declared will absorb £40,000, leaving £18,275 14s. 2d. to be carried forward to next account.
When the last report was issued, it was thought that the then coming season held out hope of improvement, but,
unfortunately, most districts have again experienced severe drought, which has continued until a very recent date.
The consequences of the drought were slightly modified by the improvement in the prices of wool and com, and,
although the improvement has continued to the present time, the losses of stock have resulted in a material reduction
in the shipments of wool as compared with those of the previous year.
The frozen meat trade continues steady, but prices, during the period under review, ruled somewhat low.
Recent advices tell of a more or less general rainfall throughout the colonies, and it is to be hoped that the disas-
trous season of the last three years may now be followed by more propitious times.
The directors have to record, with regret, the retirement on account of failing health, after over thirty-nine years'
service, of the London manager, Mr. Prideaux Selby. With the desire of preserving Mr. Selby’s connection with the
bank, they have appointed him an honorary director. The post held by Mr. Selby has been conferred upon Mr. R. W
Jeans, who previously held the appointment of secretary.
The dividend declared is at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum, or £1 per share for the half-year, and will be pli-
able, free of income tax, in London and in the colonies, on the Ist April.
4 Threadneedle-street, London, 24th March, 1898.
0. G. HAMILTON, Chairman.

The N.S.W. Anti-Bill League is run mainly
by Ms.P. who fear obliteration. The Ante-Billet
League.
Austria supports Spain. It recalls the
Republican arguing with the Austrian Emperor.
“But,” says the Emperor, “royalty is my
business / ”
The Sydney Anti-Federal Leagues have
federated. What’s the matter with the Confederate
League for a handy name? Federals v. Con-
federates is a familiar shibboleth.
Who and what constitute the British New
Guinea Syndicate ? Has Albert Edward’s man
Friday, the fat and hungry Somers Vine, who has
been smelling round Australia, got anything to do
with it ?
The member for one New South Wales
constituency will be opposed at next election by
an individual whom he found a few years back
amongst the Sydney unemployed, and got work
for in his electorate —where he still is.
Politics make strange bedfellows. The
Sydney Confederate party embraces every Domain
Demosthenes and every Croesus of the crusted
Conservative clique—the National Ass and the
asses whom Champion declared led the big strike.
Gavan Duffy’s attention was first notably
directed to Australia through his having, as an
English M.P., opposed the Victorian Constitution
Bill with as much vigor as Tillett, Hancock,
Higgins, and Maloney are now directing against
the Federal Bill.
Considering all the awful things which
Sydney Telkoraph says it is discovering in the
Federation Bill, all of which its little tin gods,
Reid and Sunday Brunker, somehow overlooked,
what kind of unspeakable fools are Reid and
Sunday Brunker, anyhow ?
Q. : What have the Australian provinces
been subsidising the Government of Papua for
these many years? Answer: In order that the
country might be developed up to a point at which
it and all the work of all the pioneers might be
profitably grabbed by the British New Guinea
Syndicate.

Re Orange and Green. If these villainous
processions cannot be abolished, they should at
least be permitted only on the parties paying cost
of police supervision, and entering into bond to
defray the cost of any damage which may arise to
person or property.
That painful horror-monger, E. W. Knox,
writes to the Sydney papers to announce that under
Federation the Victorian railways might “actu-
ally enter into competition with our railways” in
N.S.W. Come to think of it, so they might.
And, of course, they don’t compete now, else Knox
would have heard about it.
One-eyed Freetraders hold that however
the Commonwealth Bill fares they immediately
score against Protection in N.S.W., inasmuch as
the hitherto probable association in one Protec-
tionist team of Lyne, Barton and R. E. O’Connor
has been rendered impossible by acute Federal
differences and biting personal estimates.
Marvellous how the Federal agitation has
temporarily affected parties. In S.A. even the
bitt<-rly-personal hospital towb have been sus-
S ended. On the affirmative side, Kingston,
ymon, Baker, and others, who only recently
never met except to glare at eaoh other, are now
working side by side. It is, however, amusing to
contemplate the opposition crowd, composed, as
it is, of the Single-taxers, Laborites, a few Tory
merchants, the Advertiser, and Ward, M.L.O. !
S.A. Labor-party is now organising
strongly against the Convention Bill. Polling,
by the way, happens on a big local race-day, and
the Laborites will suffer. When the Convention
delegates were elected last year, the failure of the
Labor crowd to elect a single representative was
attributed to the fact that the working man pre-
ferred punting to politics.
N.S.W. Premier Reid lately did a vigorous
reach-out for the beer-and-gin vote by telling the
publicans that he was emphatically against local
option. Yet Reid claims to be a theoretical
supporter of the referendum, andlocal optionisonly
the referendum on a small scale. What is really
wanted is not its abolition but its extension to all
manner of other purposes.
A rumor begins to go round that Premier
Reid is weary of cooking the finances and trying
to hold up the dead-weight of Sunday Brunker
and the dunder-headed S. Smith, and that he will
presently annex the Agent-Generalship and go
’Ome. Probably it is the best thing he could do.
Ten chances to one in that case that Reid would
be A.G. for the Commonwealth at a big salary.
Gem from the late oration of T. J. Byrnes :
“ The Govt, is not there to check the expressed
opinion of the country or to evade the necessary
consequences of such an expressed opinion, but
rather as trustees to formulate and advance a
policy which the whole of Queensland has agreed
to.” Just fancy a Govt, waiting to formulate a
policy until the whole country agrees to it. No
wonder Bananaland makes no progress when such
are its ministers’ guiding principles.
Dear Bulletin. —Godless v. denomina-
tional education. A notorious fault of the R.C-
schools is the ridiculous educational value attached
to everything Irish. At one school in my
suburb not more than half-a-dozen out of 100
pupils are Irish-born, yet the youngsters are
crammed with historical small-talk of the Green
Isle, from St. Columbkille down to Parnell.
T’other day I questioned a Catholic school-boy
regarding Alexander the Great, Napoleon, and a
couple of other lights of the historical firmament,
and drew a blank each time. But the same
youngster reeled off a complete list of the ancient
kings of Erin, and gave me the yarn of Wolfe
Tone and Coy in minute detail. So far as Aus-
tralian facts were concerned, he wasn’t “up” to
the gold-discovery period, and although he pre-
cisely located M‘Gillicuddy’s Reeks and the
Devil’s Punchbowl, he had never heard of Mounts
Cook or Kosciusko —they didn’t happen to be in
dear ould Oirland. But he knew the Yaucluse
snake-yarn off by rote.—T.D.
Tasmania is being used just now as a sort
of cool storage depot for new-chum Chows. When
an incoming vessel has more than her due allow-
ance a-board, she drops them at Tassy, and leaves
them to filter into Melbourne under the usual
tonnage regulations. Tasmanian law allows one
Chow to every 100 tons, and the poll tax is only
£lO. If Raspberry Land continues to take in

Agnes Smith, who resides at Melbourne
Benevolent Asylum, claims to be 116 last birth-
day. The asylum authorities didn’t receive a
birth certificate with Agnes, but they are disposed
to accept a good deal of her age as true ; also, they
have it on more-or-less unreliable authority that
their ancient lodger was the first lady school-
teacher in Victoria, likewise the first white woman
who came to the new settlement, in either of which
cases she must—by her own account—have been
an enterprising young female of 60 at the time.
S.M. Herald’s unwonted spurt of energy
on behalf of Federation has died out, and that
large, placid organ now only approves in its usual
slow, benign, fuzzy fashion. And, as the other
shoddy morning publication continues to blast
furiously and climb over the mangled corpse of
Truth in the Provincial interest, the small, scared
men of Reid’s weak Ministry begin to get down
off the fence on the Provineialist side. At latest
advices Ghould—the bloodless lawyer who declines
to let Suffield have a fair inquiry—has extended
the clammy hand of brotherhood to the Want
crowd, and Potato Young has done likewise. As
a rule, it is a pretty safe thing for any decent
Democrat to vote for the cause that Ghould disap-
proves of.
Over in Westralia, where John Haynes’s
brother lives, the Government grants “ free resi-
dence areas ”to miners. Free residence areas, be
it observed. The meaning of this is that when
the miner camps in the great, howling wilderness
of God-forgotten, where the blighted land is worth
about nothing per cubic mile, he is expected to
pay, in advance, £2 the first year and .£1 per year
afterwards for the right to occupy £-acre of
ground. And the Government often sells the land
right out to British shindykits for about 2s. 6d. an
•acre all told. In addition to this arrangement,
the miner may be fired-out at any time, only re-
ceiving the bstre value of improvements. It is a
great country, that land where John Haynes's
brother lives.
It is announced, that “ Tom ” Slattery will
stand for Fitzroy Division of Sydney “on the
one ground of his opposition to the Federal Con-
vention Bill.” This means that Slattery will ask
the people to put him into Parliament solely be-
cause he is against Federation. Therefore, he
must also ask them to put him in because he in-
tends to do something to squash Federation—•
otherwise the mere fact that he is against Federa-
tion wouldn’t make any more difference than the
fact that he is against the transit of Venus or dis-
approves of the equator. When Slattery asks the
people to put him into Parliament because he is
an anti-Federationist and prefers being a worm
rather than a butterfly, he must mean that he will
be able, in Parliament, to help the worm interest
along. Now, the Federal question has been re-
ferred to the people. If they say “ no,” that ends
the matter for the present and there is no use for
Slattery. If they say “yes”—then the only use
for Slattery is to vote that Parliament should refuse
to obey the will of the people, and disfranchise the
nation, and kickover the boasted principle of the
rule of the majority, and throw the Referendum
overboard. The only use in Parliament now for any
man who goes in on the. Anti-Federal ticket is to
vote that Parliament disregards the will of the
people. So that is the issue on which Slattery
appeals to the people. Slattery, by the way, is
solemnly pledged to fight Premier Reid in King
Division, and his decision to stand for Fitzroy in-
stead looks like an unobtrusive sneaking-out from
the big and hopeless fight which was in front of
him. If Fitzroy Division, which is mainly Wool-
loomooloo and Potts Point, does put in Slattery it
won’t prove anything at all in regard to Federa-
tion. Slattery will probably get in because he
was the bosom friend of the late M'Elhone, whom
Woolloomooloo loved for his lurid vocabulary ;
because he is Catholic and Irish, and Woolloo-
mooloo is largely both these things ; and because
he is personally acquainted with every Mike who
sells rum in the electorate and every retired Mick
who used to sell rum there, and the Moike who
sells or used to sell rum there is legion.

The Reason of it
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene: Any drawing-room, anywhere. Infuriated wife shows generally - amor-
ous husband photo, of flaxen-haired frailty, found im his coat-pocket.
SHE.
Scoundrel, villain, lying traitor
Turn your false gaze full on it.
On her face the imprimatur
Of the wanton clear is writ.
Smooth-tongued viper, base betrayer,
Answer: What is it you see
In this tntth-and-honor slayer
That you do not find in mb ?
HE.
Softly, calmly, quell this passion,
Kindly do not fret and rage
In that tense, dramatic fashion
Dear to mummers on the stage.
You, I own, than she are fairer
But—my partner now for life
And of all my cares the sharer;
She is someone else’s wife !


Melb. Age last week acknowledged the
receipt of a donation of fifteenpence, for the local
dogs’shelter, “from M.M. (deceased).”
Six months’ hard was what Richmond
(Vic.) Bench lately served out to one Rogers for
being inconveniently cleanly. Rogers was a Ya,rra-
banker who, when he had washed his only suit of
clothes in that balmy stream, used to lie in the
sun in the “altogether” until they were dry.
There was no allegation of wilful exposure ; and
the spot Rogers chose for his purpose was not
visible from any road or house. Yet, for not
electing to remain, like the rest of his fraternity,
filthy and malodorous and a promising germ-
centre, Rogers gets about as much as if he had
bashed his wife to a mummy, or half-murdered the
officious bobby who ran him in.
The present epidemic of typhoid in Melb.
is more or less due to the tardy introduction of
anti-typhoid measures. Germs are brought to
light by the street diggers employed in laying
underground sewer pipes, and the disease is bound
to crop up freely in place after place until the
excavation work has got beyond the thickly-popu-
lated districts. A few people must perish for the
benefit of posterity, and if Providence would only
give a power of discrimination to the germs
there’d be nothing to grumble about. Providence,
unfortunately, is too busy to attend to these
little matters. The old city councillors who made
Smellbourne what it is seem to be germ-proof.
They die, it is true, and worms eat them—but
not from typhoid. From overfeeding, as a rule.

J. It. Dickson, who threatens to sulk
because the callow Byrnes has climbed over him
into the Q. Premiership, acts as agent for Anglican
Bishop Tuffnell’s Brisbane property. The pro-
perty, which is chief!}' houses, is situated in a not
over-fashionable quarter, but the income from it
is considerable. The old Bishop, like Dickson,
knew the meaning of the word disappointment.
He, with seven well-groomed, saintly parsons,
landed one day in Brisbane in the certain belief
that the Anglican Church was the State Church
of Queensland, and that Govt, grants and titles
were, as in dear old England, the order of the day.
But while his ship sailed over the blue Queens-
land Parliament passed an “ Act for the Abolition
of State Aid to Religion,” and Tuffnell and party
found themselves in the “soup.” Contemporary
records say: “The Bishop was greatly dis-
pleased.” However, he was a man of thrift, and,
though his opportunities were lessened, he man-
aged to buy up land and houses, the business
management of which he entrusted to the pains-
taking, serious-visaged Dickson.
Van Dobson’s reach after dollars as com-
pensation for the loss of his mountain chalet by a
bush fire allegedly lit by a Govt, servant—a
prison-ganger—has been successful, and patriotic
Tasmanian taxpayers are called upon to again
take up a collection on behalf of the Ruling
Family.
Dear Bulletin; —Your par. anticipating
that a verdict against the Govt, in the Van Dob-
son bush-fire would magnify Govt, responsibility
to an anti-Socialistic degeee is all right. The case
has already been used as an argument against the
extension of the Govt-as employer principle. But
the trouble can be avoided by the simple method
of issuing “ instructions.” Had Tas. Govt.’s ser-
vant in this case received “ instructions ” not to
light the bush-fire, Dobson might have whistled
for compensation. Govt, couldn’t prove that such
instructions were given.—J.D.

TUESDAY, 21st JUNE.
BY AUCTION.
Tenham Cattle Station-
TO be SOLD pursuant to a judgment of the
Supreme Court of Queensland, made in an
action, Hammond v. Hammond and Others, num-
bered 55 of , 1897, with the approbation of the
Honorable IVU. Justice Cooper, by Messrs. B. D.
MOREHEAD & CO., of Brisbane, Auctioneers,
in conjunction with Messrs. HILL, CLARK &
CO., of Sydney (the persons appointed by the said
Judge), at Mary-street, Brisbane, in the Colony of
Queensland, on the 21st Day of JUNE, jB9B, at
Eleven O Clock in the forenoon, in one lot,
TENHAM STATION
(Outside Tick-Infested Area),
Situated in Gregory South district of Queensland, con-
sisting of 276 square miles of country (more or less), of
which 190 square miles are Leasehold, having 17 years to
run from the Ist July, 1897, and 86 square miles of country
held under Grazing Right, under “Crown Lands Act of
1884.” Together with
6500 CATTLE (more or less), Fifty (50) HORSES (more
or less), STATION PLANT, STORES and IM-
PROVEMENTS thereon or belonging thereto.
The Country is situated on Cooper’s Creek and Kyabra
Creek, about 40 miles south of Windorah and about 230
miles west of Charleville railway station. It is very rich
fattening country for cattle, consisting of lightly timbered,
open, mulga country, with occasional black-soil plains,
abundance of salt-bush, blue-bush, cotton-bush, and other
edible shrubs. TENHAM is splendidly watered by the
main channel of the Cooper and its many branches, be-
sides springs. The Cattle are quiet, and have been well
worked. In the herd there are about 1000 head of Bul-
locks and Cows fit to start for market. The Improvements
are sufficient for the efficient working of the station.
Particulars and Conditions of Sale may be had gratis at
the office of Messrs. Macdonahl-Paterson and Hawthorn,
Edward-street, Brisbane, Solicitors for the Plaintiff;
Messrs. J. F. Fitzgerald and Power, Queen-street, Brisbane,
Solicitors for the Defendants; or at the offices of the said
B. D. Morehead & Co., Brisbane; Messrs. Hill, Clark &
Co., Sydney ; Messrs. Ryan, Hammond & Mates, Mel-
bourne ; and Messrs. Barker Bros., Adelaide, and at the
place of sale.
Dated this twenty-fifty day of April, 1898.
(L.S.) W. A. DOUGLAS,
. Deputy Registrar.

Spain issues LETTERS of MARQUE.
The Spanish Government have decided not to recog'

Missing Friends.
TNFORMATION WANTED of NANCY M'CREADY,
a native of Tendooly, County Donegal, Ireland,
who left Ireland for Australia or New Zealand in the
year 1856 or 1858. Her father's name was Dennis
McCready ; mother’s maiden name was Rose Carr. They
died in the year 1854 or 1855. Nancy McCready’s brother
James died in Fort Smith, Arkansas, U.S.A., on the
30th day of August, 1896, leaving some property, which
Nancy or her descendants, as next of kin, are entitled to,
if they can be found.
Any information of her will be thankfully received
by me.
MAT GREY
Administrator of the Estate of
James McCready,
Fort Smith, Arkansas, U.S.A.

“I thought I had a Stamp.”
Many inquiries having been made at this office tor
the above humorous picture by “ Hop ” which
appeared in The Bulletin as a coloured supple-
ment some years ago (and which has been long out
of print) Mr. Hopkins has, by special permission of
The Bulletin, executed an etching of the work,
and a limited number of signed artist’s proofs
may now be had only on application to this
office. The proofs are printed on Whatman’s
“Creswick ” drawing paper, 14 x inches ; size
of plate, x 12. As every detail has been carried
out by the artist himself (including the printing)
these proofs have all the value of an original
drawing. This will be an exceptional opportunity
to possess an example of “ Hop’s ” work, which is
rarely to be seen outside the pages of this paper.
On receipt of Three Guineas we will send by
post or parcel delivery to any address.
