Miss Lilia Wilde, the very pretty girl who got very few
good chances in Australia, has done well since she re-
turned to G.B. At latest dates she was scoring heavily
as Berenis, in “Sign of the Cross” on tour.
Kowalski, the John L. Sullivan of the piano, played at
London Savage Club before the Prince of Wales, the
other night. In the first round the instrument was
severely punished and retired to its corner very limp ;
but early in the second round Kowalski landed a blow on
the point of the jaw which laid it out dead to the world.
It never recovered consciousness.
Organist Wiegand’s free recitals have been booming the
hitherto much-neglected Melb. town organ. The man
who was hired by the city fathers to grind out weekly
tunes at £3OO per year has deceased, after a long series of
performances often given to an audience of three hall-
keepers. Since then the organ has only boomed forth at
irregular intervals. Moils. W. must find playing on it a
much stiffer job than tackling his own “ kist of whistles ”
with all the latest improvements. Melb. organ is played
by main force with no improvements at all.
. Person interested in ladies costumed and uncostunied
informs The Bulletin that quite a piece of the trunk of
The Tree of Knowledge ” is bare.
An actress named Ella Sterling achieved a sufficiently
romantic climax at Liverpool (Eng.), last month. Iler
husband died under a surgical operation, and the widow
threw herself from an upper window of the Adelphi Hotel,
leaving a note addressed to her brother-iu-law. “My
dear Fred,—Tony asked me to go with him, and I am
going. Good-bye. Bury us together, and forget poor
Klla.
At Florence Schmidt’s concert in Brisbane. Mrs. Store-
kipper : “ She’s a soprano, ain’t she ?” Mrs. Haberdash :
“ Not at all, she’s Irish.”
Several Australian managers almost live in their
theatres. The restless and mercurial Bland Holt is one
of them. From 9.30 or 10 o’clock every morning until
midnight he seldom leaves the “ shop,** not even for
meals, which are frequently brought to him.
. The imbecilities produced by the Nellie Farren benefit,
in London, make it evident that, after all, the Englishman
can ill afford to sneer at the Frenchman as “ hysterical.”
After the close of the pantomime at Drury Lane the pre-
vious night, sundry enthusiasts were discovered
“ planted” under the seats, with the intention of getting
front places next forenoon ; and outside the theatre all
night was a huge crowd, reading, playing cards and
boozing, by the light of candles and Chinese-lanterns.
Different as was the expected event, it was simply the
execution-mob over again, and with just about the exe-
cution-mob’s opinion-value.
Francis Nesbitt (MeCron), the old-time Sydney trage-
dian, had a queer experience once in California. He tried
gold-digging, but was not strong enough to rough it, and
determined to return to ’Frisco. At the time many law-
less characters went from Sydney to California, and,
posting themselves on the route of returning diggers,
levied much blackmail. Nesbitt came in their way and
was stuck-up. While in the act of stripping him, he
thought he recognised one as an old Sydney hand, and
asked the highwayman if the latter did not remember
him. “ I am Nesbitt, the actor,” he said. They dis-
believed him, as he was travel-stained and dusty. “If
you are Nesbitt, give us a speech from some play.” He
gave them “Tell, to his native mountains,” whereupon
they believed him, expressed their delight, and, instead
of robbing him, offered him meat and drink and money.
California did not agree with Nesbitt, who returned to
Sydney. Some of the MeCron family still reside in Parra-
matta.
On a wall near Sydney Cyclorama some well-meaning
persons have had painted “ For the wages of sin is death,
and the gift,” etc. Thev have hired the wall, and intend
to hire others for a similar purpose, but the Cyclorama
proprietors, though they are raking in the shekels ever
the Holy City, object to the on commercial
grounds, and have erected to obscure the
Wurrd. “Damnation and the dollars ! ”
“ ‘ Tom ’ Haydon’s ‘ Sporting Reminiscences ’ has done
much better than either author or publishers expected,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ The
Times, Sportsman, Referee, Field, &c., gave it good
notices, and a second edition was soon exhausted. His
daughter, Ethel, is likely to be associated with Nellie
Stewart this Christmas at Drury Lane as principal girl to
the latter’s principal boy. An Australian panto, in Lon-
don 1 —‘ what ho !’ ”
“ Sievier” puts the sensible question : Why is it the
orchestra, when playing an overture, always leaves
off and waits for the ring-up bell? Why not continue
fiddling till the curtain does go up. This sudden
stoppage always reminds the audience that the perform-
ance is due, and in many cases leads to a perfect uproar.
Were the overture repeated, I am certain an audience
could be “ kidded ” to wait till nine o’clock for the cur-
tain to rise. ,
Nearly all our best musicians are “ made in Germany.’
Look at the Marshall Hall orchestra. The Professor him-
self was educated in Germany ; then in his band—the
very pick of the musical talent available—there are :
Dierich, Brennicke, Schacht, Wallenstein, Weinberg,
Wiedermann (two of ’em), Hattenbach, Winchelmann,
Ceschlna, Riihr, Pechotseh, and Liittick. There is a
plain British Smith in the orchestra, and he plavs the
flute. But even the drum is in the hands of a Nichoff.
This German prevalence being pointed out to a musical
expert at the Marshall Hall concert, he said : “ Do you
know that if Britain went to war with Germany, the
British regiments could hardly get a band to play them
on to the battle-field ? Nearly all the military band-
masters are Germans, and many of the best bandsmen
are of the same nationality.”
Nellie Farren, interviewed by London Sketch, claims
that she and Fred Leslie “practically introduced bur-
lesque for the first time to the Antipodes.” She might as
well claim that she discovered them. There is an inter-
esting article called “ The Burlesque and the Beautiful,”
bv the late “Orion” Horne, in the Contemporary for
1871, wherein he describes at length burlesque as played
in Melb. and Sydney. He speaks of the “ Great Plague of
London” burlesque as “this alternately alluring and
mocking siren, the anomalous seducer who, amidst all
her rude, bespangled charms, equally ridicules herself and
her besotted victims.” “In these dazzling abortions,”
Horne declares, “Melbourne, as usual in all thirig3 good
or bad, topped the climax.” That was in the pre-Firm
days.
Melb. Jewish Literary Society decided at
its last meeting that “ the Vic. newspaper press
is not failing to fulfil its true functions.” Also,
the Society re-affirmed a previous statement that
its third annual ball (which, by the way, has a
pawnbrokerish sound) will be hung up on July 4.
The great object of the M. J.L. Society is to create
intellectual occasions for calling public attention
to the annual ball. Every meeting concludes
with a further reference to the promised “hop,”
and the Melb. newspaper press fulfils its true
function by publishing a line to that effect.
The decision of the British Privy Council
in the case of Adams v. Young (Minister of
Works) —which virtually decides that N.S.W.
civil-servants appointed under the Act of 1884
have fixity of tenure in their positions—will
cause the issue of many writs from retrenched
officials against the Government. Nearly, three
years ago the present Lord Chief Justice of
England—then Sir Charles Russell and a leader
of the bar—privately expressed to a Sydney friend
the opinion that most of the retrenchments of
officials holding office under the ’B4 Act were
illegal.
Another point which the N.S.W. Pro-
vincial gang has worked very quietly is this : At
the Convention election a man could vote any-
where in the province on the production of his
elector’s right, and as a result about 146,000 votes
were polled. At the Referendum, bn June 3, the
voting is to be by the electoral rolls, and conse-
quently every man must vote in his own electorate.
This proviso, it is generally reckoned, will keep
about 10 per cent, of the population away from
the poll, and if so, 14,000 votes disappear in one
act, and the chance of getting the 80,000 mini-
mum is so much the less.
The shifts of the argumentative politician
seldom drive him into such a desolate byway of
ineonsequentiality as that into which antagonism
to the Commonwealth Bill drives a recent Daily
Telegraph writer. That writer exultingly points
out that, with the possible exception of W. P.
Crick, all the members of N.S.W. Parliament
who in 1892 actively opposed the raising of the
age of consent of girls to seventeen are now in
favor of federation on the basis of the Convention
Bill. Ergo. But then his statement of the Par-
liamentary fact is also wrong.
A certain Hogue, M.L.A. (of N.S.W.),
has a particular bee in his bell-topper to the effect
that Ballarat (Vic.) will be the Federal capital,
and he holds that every good N.S. Waler should
be agin Federation on that account. Why
shouldn’t Ballarat be the capital—if Victoria is
such a fool as to consent, which The Bulletin
doesn’t believe? The Federal capital is to be
ceded entirely to the Federal Government—to be
confiscated from the State which now owns it, and
become the joint property of all the States. If
Ballarat is the capital,'Victoria loses nearly 50,000
population ; one of its best cities ; a fine goldfield ;
one member in the House of Representatives,
which will make N.S.W.’s preponderance all the
greater ; a lot of revenue ; it cuts its small terri-
tory still smaller ; and it sacrifices an important
section of its great Western railroad. And
N.S.W., as one of the Federal partners, becomes
a part owner of the city and the goldfield and the
railway and the revenue and the territory, and all
the rest. So long as the Federal Government
doesn’t want to grab Goulburn or Bathurst or
Armidale, or any other N.S.W. city, what has
any Hogue to complain of ?
SPORTING NOTIONS.
English stewards are not so easy-going as the
A.J.C. of N.S. Wales. Timon, heavily backed by the
public for Liverpool (Eng.) Grand National, was scratched
an hour before the race. The owner of Timon, when
called upon for an explanation, produced a vet. certificate
that the horse was not fit to run. In N.S.W. a horse
rushed by the public has.actually been -taken on to the
course and scratched just before the race, but no such ex-
planation was asked his owner by the A.J.C. committee,
who invariably run on the lines that a big nominator can
do no wrong.
* * • «
It is on the cards that Lochiel will wind up the
Australian season as premier winning sire, as to the end
of April he leads Carbine (£10,708) by exactly £lOOO.
Next to “ Old Jack ” comes his expatriated connection,
Trenton (£10,327), whilst-Gozo (£8953) is a fair fourth,
w ith Neckersgat (£4485) and the others beaten off. For a
young sire, Bill of Portland has done well, £2830 standing
to the credit of his offspring. Of winning owners, W. R.
Wilson is easily first with £10,828, which was won for him
by seven horses, recording 21 wins.
*** ' •
Lillie Bridge, Sydney, if writer recollects aright,
was the first course to try the starting-barrier, since used
all over the racing world. The little course has now
another idea in force—a rope stretched across the course
at the rear of the ponies, giving plenty room to turn, but
not giving the boys the room necessary to rush their
mounts up to and through the webbing.
* * » *
The rival dailies of one Australian capital
abound with humor skilfully disguised as “ Turf Notes.”
One sporting editor is an authority on turf history of 30
years ago, but couldn’t remember what happened the day
before yesterday to save his life. A lovely blunder
of his was the announcement that Hopscotch had
been sold for a large sum to go to the stud. The rival
editor is chiefly remarkable for his inordinate vanity, but
he doesn’t know the difference between, say, “ hock ”
and “ gaskin.”_ Example : Noticing a horse return to the
paddock showing lameness, he asked a friend what was
wrong and was told the nag had strained his “ sarcastic
tendency.” He received the information with a dubious
“Oh, you’re joking.” “Not at all,” was the answer;
“but I should have said his ‘ gastric tendency.’” And
“ gastric tendency ” it was in the paper’s next issue.
• * * *
Up-country race-meeting. Protest entered
against first horse for running inside post. Mr. Public-
house (judge) loq. : “ I don’t care a damn. I see’d he had
it won half a mile away.”
* '* * *
At next annual meeting of the A.J.C. a motion
is to be submitted that members of the Press be in future
admitted to all “ inquiry ” meetings of the committee. It
is to be hoped that this be carried, as the public have a
decided right to know how and by whom “ the oracle is
worked.”

Such is equine life. Years since one could
scarcely pick up a paper without seeing Hogmanay’s
name as the winner of a race. The patriarchal horse,*l7
years old, was sold last week in Victoria for 22 “bob”
and a “kick.”
* * * m
Is the Caucasian played-out ? At Cassilis races
(Gippsland, Vie.), on Eight-Hours’ Day, there were eight
events, and Ah Sam, a little Chow, rode three win-
ners and twice ran his nags into second place. The
bush boys up in that district consider themselves amongst
the best on top of a horse, too, and the racecourse at Cas-
silis demands horsemanship.
* * • •
Charles Lea, of Lea and Perrins’ sauce, died
lately, leaving £1,700,000 personalty and £30,000 realty.
,He used to be a great coursing man some 20 years ago,
when he assured the writer that only a very wealthy man
could afford the luxury of a coursing stable—a racing
stable M as, he alleged, a cheaper luxury by 50 per cent.
* * * *
The news that Gilbert, the walking crank,
has broken down at Nhill (Vic.) surprises no one M'ho has
seen him. When in Adelaide he was completely worn
Out and feared a collapse. He is a short, weak-framed
man and suffers from chronic rheumatism—or neuritis,
which is a very different thing.
• • * *
The walking-round-the-world-for-a-wager “gag”
is growing monotonous. One of the latest pedestrians is
“the gentleman who gives his name as Captain Tre-
villyan,” and is just trotting round, the globe doncher-
know “ to decide a wagah of £lO,OOO between the Earl of
Dudley and General DeLaey.” Dudley, bai jove ! backed
feller named Smith to walk against him, but Smith got
knocked up in Melbawne and couldn’t go any further
donchersee, wherefore the gallant Trevillyan has—haw 1—
won wagah, already. Meanwhile there doesn’t
seem to be any public sympathy displayed for the pros-
trate Smith in Melbawne. Tins Bulletin would like to
hear how Smith is progressing. Perhaps he is dead.
* * * *
The feminine craze for naked male legs, fostered!
by cycle racing, reached its logical conclusion attheMelb.
Eight Hours’ sports, when a damsel M’ho had “ the habit ”
badly could contain her feeling's no longer, and jumped
the fence to rush across the track upon a group of bare-
legged riders waiting in the arena. What the lady’s in-
tentions M-ere will never be known, for a spoilsport “ cop-
per ” arrested the intruderess. Her pathetic explanation
that she yearned to examine those legs at closer range met
with no sympathy, and she was marched off the ground.
Outside, however, she broke away from custody- and in-
vaded the dressing room, where, it is to be presumed, her
curiosity was fully satisfied before she was recaptured'.
Next morning, P.M. Panton fined the worshipper of the
hairy- calf 20s. The disillusionment was worth 40s.
* * » *
The complaint comes from Hingland and other
parts that cycling robs the legs of their native elasticity,,
and unfits them for the mazy u-altz. It is even said to
impoverish their “ setting to partners ” in square-dances.
This is a matter for sassiety to enquire into, with the
assistance of medical science, and, anyhow, it should be
possible to restore elasticity to the bike leg by a judicious
process of greasing. But the bike is undoubtedly a
serious rival to the waltz, insofar that it serves to abate
the particular craving for dissipation which originally
prompted girls to force mugsome dances into vogue.
* * * *
A Wellington, M.L., cyclist missed his lamp.
In morning, reports loss to the police. Police show him:
his lamp. “How did it get here?” asks the cyclist.
“Oh, there’s a man before the court now for breaking
into an oyster-saloon early this morning, and this was his
dark-lantern.”
• * * *
A stone-deaf man won a committee-man’s race
at a recent M.L. sports-meeting. He beat the pistol, and,
when asked afterwards how he got away, he said he-
watched the tongue of the starter —“ old pal o’ mine—
alwa.vs sticks his tongue out when going to fire a gun.”
* * * *
The Westralian gold-fields feel that the time'
has arrived when they must declare that they are re-
spectable ; so this season some of the footbalbmatches,
which hitherto have been played at the Boulder and some'
other settlements exclusively on Sundays, are to be held
on Saturday afternoons. At one or two way-back places:
in N.S.W. matches are still played on the Sabbath—at-
White Cliffs, for instance—and lately a revivalist im-
proved the shining hour by warning the population that
the epidemic of typhoid was sent to scourge them for'
this sin. It sounded all the more impressive, too, because-
so many of the afflicted u-cre athletic young fellows.
* * • «
It is authenticated in Southern N.S.W. that a-
u-ell-known oarsman once tested a local hard-drinker in
continuous long-beer swilling. The game was started,
with single drinks, but after the person with a throat had
sucked down three without waiting for breath, the oars-
man ordered them in couples, and then in threes. “This:
is much too slow,” he protested at last. “ All hands to-
the pmnp. Fetch ’em in half-a-dozen at a time.” And
they did. When the score stood at 22 the sculler cried a.
halt, but his beery nominee looked up in astonishment
and exclaimed : “ Why, I ain’t nearly done yet! Thought
yer was going to keep it up until I stopped yer? ” Then
he accepted “ shouts "from everybody in the bar.-
* * * *
Tom Doyle, town-councillor of Kanowna, was
charged with, and acquitted, of inciting the miners to
assault John Forrest. He is a big, raw-boned Irishman
with the facult}’of doing, or saying, the right thing in a
most unconventional manner. “ Doyle’s latest ?” is the
prelude to hundreds of screaming goldfields’ yarns—
mostly unprintable. Had Doyle not made big money out
of his hotel he would have been one of Australia’s cham-
pion cyclists. Some Coolgardie sports once took a crack
rider to Kanowna with designs on the local lucre which it
was known would be planked on Doyle. The latter had
been into Kalgoorlie that morning on his machine, and
returning just in time to hear of the challenge, elected to
race right away—first man to report at Kalgoorlie P.O. to
win. Doyle beat the “good thing” by a quarter of an hour.
• * • *
“ The professionals of Stoddart’s cricket-team
declare that the last trip to Australia was a highly enjoy-
able one.” Which goes, at least, a little way towards
explaining why the said team was donkey-licked four
times out of five in test-matches. Also, it raises a ques-
tion as to the meaning of “ high enjoyment.”
* * * *
Great rivalry between football and lacrosse in
Adelaide this year. Although football declines, lacrosse
clubs cannot secure Adelaide Oval, the only enclosed
playing ground, as the Football Association has a lease
which doesn’t expire until next year. Then will come the
tug-of-war.
* * * *
Billiard-champion Weiss, after his London
draw with Roberts, took on C. Dawson, one of the best.
Weiss showed great form throughout, making one or two
breaks of over 200 and several over 100. Alt hough Weiss
has secured good matches, Memmott, his old conqueror,
seems to have struck comparatively bad times.
♦ * * *
S. : “ Foxes have come to Berrigan, N.S. W.—of
course from Victoria. Last year there was only one,
which I got with a shot-gun (howl! ye in pink !). This
year there are several, which, doubtless, are doomed if
Scotty the Rabbiter (Mr. John Bean, no relative of
Sawny) is about. Hon. Rupert Carrington preserves
kangaroo, but not Reynard.”
* * * *
Newhaven Jackson has taken up a challenge
thrown down by Body. The Swift man has a reputation
for bullocking work besides rare sprinting capacity ; and,
therefore, the Maorilander will have to chaw off as much
as he can masticate in order to get fit for the struggle.
The insignificant Provincialisb party in
Victoria has put up a world’s record for brass
front. It is hard to believe that such a small
party possesses so much cheek without being
entirely cheek. The whole 30 shillings which was
subscribed at the Trades Hall to oppose the Con-
stitution Bill having given out, the Provincialists
approached Premier Turner, and actually asked
that the date of voting on the question should be
postponed indefinitely, or, in other words, until
“they had had an opportunity of enlightening the
people.” No. 1 piece of metal face. Further, they
asked that facilities be given them by the Govern-
ment for organising opposition. When abruptly
asked by Turner what this meant, the little brass
crowd boldly stated that it was after free railway
passes. Bromley, by the way, joined in with this
Eush, and it is the first indication he has given of
is position in regard to the Bill. Evidently he is
opposed to it. Anyway, it doesn’t matter very
much if he is, for the party is so absolutely help-
less that Bromley will make no perceptible differ-
ence either way. Further, who is Bromley?
The Hospital-trouble is temporarily dead
in Adelaide, and the Paris Nesbit trouble is
taking its place with great applause. Paris
Nesbit is a clever lawyer and a thoroughly good
fellow with too much blood in his head ; and for
the last few months he has been spending his time
in going in and out of the local lunatic asylum
with more or less picturesqueness, and usually on
horseback. The last time he was asyluined the
letter of the law was not fulfilled, and Nesbit, in
a lucid interval, took a point so successfully that
the medical superintendent opened the door in a
hurry and let him out. Then Nesbit had a dark
interval and was taken back to the asylum ; but
he is really so little more than eccentric that thfi
other day he was released on parole. When asked
to come back Nesbit refused, and the last of him
was a gallant figure on horseback taking impos-
sible leaps in the Mount Lofty ranges or there-
abouts. The Kingston Govt, naturally and pro-
perly allows Nesbit every possible latitude, but
there is a growing feeling that his eccentricities
are becoming rather too obvious, and instructions
have been issued to capture the gallant figure on
horseback. The Bulletin irrationally hopes the
instructions won’t catch him.
The recent furious gale upon N.S-.W. coast
will for various reasons make a time-mark
in the province’s history. In the first place,
though only showing an absolute maximum wind-
velocity of 44 miles an hour, as against the 56 of
the “Dandenong” gale, last week’s visitation
showed—storm for storm, 24 hours against
18—a total wind mileage of 894 as against 849.
The resultant maritime disasters also have points
of special interest about them. The remarkable
good fortune which ran the ship Hereward
blindly upon the sand, instead of upon the rocks,
at Maroubra Bay, some distance south of Port
Jackson, was in strong contrast with the hopeless
“ ill-luck ” which ended in the s-.-s. Maitland’s
utter smash-upagainsttheßoat Harbor cliffs—after
:somebody had blundered in sending such a calm-
weather boat to court disaster in teeth of the gale.
Very strange also was the fact that the abandoned
«.s. Merksworth managed to' make Newcastle on
her “own,” and sank while the lifeboat was
making to the rescue of the crew, which was not
on board. The gallant way in which the s.s.
Duckenfield fought her way through the worst of
it is strong testimony to the necessity, for coasting
purposes, of such a compact and powerful type of
vessel as she is. The craft that could live through
those terrible three days from Newcastle to Nel-
son’s Bay ought to weather anything.

An asthmatic old N.S.W. lady recently
lost her pet cockatoo from what appeared to be
actual asthma, supposed to result from long imi-
tation of its mistress’s coughings and wheezings.
Sufficient account, by the way, is not taken of
parrots as possible disseminators of tuberculous
•disease—to which they are peculiarly liable.

While the young King of Spain’s English
I instructor has been receiving ,£lOOO a year and
free quarters .in the palace, the Government is

at best, miserably-paid official schoolmasters.

H. : “ One of the principal rev. gents in
England addicted to sermons ‘For Men only,’
Pastor Dawson, has just announced that he has
abandoned the practice of this Varleyan and
Grattan-Guinnessy line. He is convinced that it
is more likely to be an engine of demoralisation
than otherwise. Henceforth his young wethers
and ewes are never to be separated. More than
all, the Men Only line is ‘un-English.’ That, of
course, is a clincher.”
Dear Bulletin. —lnteresting to note how
hardly a single Sydney or Melbourne paper, in
describing Havana (off its own bat, as it were)
fails to ring-in Froude’s statement that the har-
bour is “polluted by the sewage-accumulations of
three centuries”—misprinted by one Melb. weekly
into “three countries .” James Anthony, as
i usual, exaggerated a bit; still, it is true that no
l skipper who knows the harbour and can get hold
i of a buoy cares to let go an anchor, by reason of
| the soulful odours which would attend the break -
! ing of the mud-hook out again. But as Havana
is, and always has been, a fairly healthy place,
the fact goes strongly to confirm a growing sus- i
picion that we have been hearing a lot too much
i about bacilli and bacteria and pathogenic germs
! a nd all the rest of it. If sewage had a-tenth of
I the power for evil it is credited with, Havana,
calmly sniffing-up the stinks of 10 generations,
ought to be to-day no better than a charnel-
house.— Sardonyx.

H. : “I have just been reading, in a
Melb. newspaper, of a boy killed by falling from
a ship’s yard in the dead of night. Not enough is
made of such occurrences. Think of it ! The in-
experienced nervous lad sent aloft on a swaying !
mast, in the black stormy night, to hand-in sail. !
In the case under notice, the poor boy fell 30ft., i
his head knocked the handrail, and he was pre-
cipitated into such a fearful sea that no, boat could
live in it. Horrible ! I contend it was no place j
for a boy at all. A case rises in my memory of j
such a lad who fell off a yard and was drowned ;
near Cape Leeuwin. His mother, at home in i
Melb., watched the progress of the ship, and I
when it came into port—at night—had sup- ■
per prepared, and there the poor creature sat i
listening—and waiting—and waiting. Horrible ! !
—I say again. Brunton Stephens had something
if my thought when he wrote —
live me a woman to strive with, a man, or a demon, or
angel ! j
When did I tremble or cringe when the proud and the
strong were my foes ? ]
But from the weaklings of Christ, from the delicate !
lambs of Evangel,
From the lorn looks of young innocents—save me, oh '
save me from those !
Likely enough the commiserated lad was a coarse,
onscienceless lout—likely enough, on the other
hand, he wasn’t! But, good or bad, give the boys
a chance.” I

Sydney bill-discounter Himmelhoch (Him-
melhoch in German means “sky-high”) was in
the witness-box at Hobart the other day in a law-
suit arising out of a gambling transaction. Book-
maker Drake was suing on some bills an alleged
victim had given him ; said bills the victim now
disputes. Defendant’s counsel stated that Him-
melhoch had been brought into the case in order
that proceedings for the recovery of the amount
of the bills—about <£loo0 —could be legally taken.
In cross-examination, defendant's counsel to Him-
melhoch : “You know Moses Barrington or Ben-
jamin?” Witness: “Yes, I haf seen him.”
Counsel: “Heis a pal of yours, isn’t he ? ” Wit-
ness (very quietly, though decidedly): “I haf no
pals.” (Laughter.) Counsel: “ You have no
pals—that is, you don’t trust anybody ? ” Wit-
ness: “It all depends.” Creed of the per-
shenter : “ I haf no pals ! ”

Public Notices.
Public Service Board, - •
50, Young-street,
Sydney, 21st April, 1898.
A COMPETITIVE Examination of Candidates for
Junior Clerkships in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney, and at various Country
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit
the convenience of candidates, on the 27th June, and
four following days. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made is 50. Candidates must not be less than
16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day of exam-
ination, of good moral character, and in sound health.
Persons already employed in the General Divisions of the
Public Service will be admitted to the examination, pro-
vided their age does not exceed thirty years. . The sub-
jects in which Candidates will be examined are Hand-
writing, English (Dictation, Composition, Precis writing,
Parsing, Analysis, and derivation of words), Arithmetic
(full course), Geometry (Euclid, first Book), • Geography
(outlines of physical and political geography, and the
geography of the Australasian Colonies in moderate
detail), English History from the Middle of the Eighteen
Century to the present time, especially with reference to
the Colonies and Dependencies. Candidates will also be
allowed to take any two, but not more than two, of the
following subjects .'—Mathematics, English, a prescribed
Author, viz.:—Tennyson’s “ Gareth and Lynet.te ” (Mac-
Millan), Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemisty,
and Elementary Geology. Application to attend the
examination must be made on a form for the purpose,
which can be obtained at the office of the Board, and
must reach the undersigned not later than the Ist June.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee of
10s., which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to
the Secretary of the Board with the form of application.
A list of registered candidates with centres of examination
Will be published in the Government Gazette, after the
time for the receipt of applications has expired, and can-
didates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined.
The Regulations and copies of the papers set at the
last examination can be obtained at the Government '
Printing Office, and the principal booksellers.
By order of the Board,
J. W. HOLLIMAN. Secretary.
0 a 70.
Public Service Board,
60, Young-street,
Sydney, 30th April, 1898.
rrHE Public Service Board hereby notify that a Com-
petitive Examination of Candidates for admission to-
the Professional Division of the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney, and at various Country
Centres which will be fixed as far as possible to suit the
convenience of candidates, on the 27th Jilne and four
following days. The estimated number of appointments
to be made is 20. Candidates must not be less than 16 or
more than 21 years of age on the first day of examination,
of good moral character, and in sound health. Persons'
already employed in the Clerical and General Divisions
of the Public Service, or who may have previously been
employed therein and retired for reasons other than an.,
offence, wi 1 be admitted to the examination provided
their age does no exceed 30 years, Candidates may elects
to be examined for either the Engineering, the Survey,
or Drafting Branches, but must state in their application
the Branch which they desire to enter. Candidates for the
Engineering Branch will be examined in the followingsub-
jects (1) Handwriting, (2) English (Dictation. Com-
position, Prhcis writing, Parsing, Analysis, and Derivation
of Words), (3) Arithmetic (full course), (4) Geometry
(Euclid, First Book), (5) Geography (outlines of physical
and political geography, and the geography of the Aus.-
tralasian Colonies in moderate detail, (6) English History
from the middle of the Eighteenth Century to the present
time, especially with reference to the Colonies and
Dependencies, (7) Mathematics (Euclid, Books 2 to 4 and
6, including easy exercises in these books, Algebra up to
and including quadratic equations), (8) Elementary
Drawing, and (9) Elementary Geology. Candidates for
the Survey or Drafting Branches will be examined in the
first eight subjects referred to above. Candidates for all
branches will also be allowed to fake any two, but not
more than two, of the following subjects (a) English, a,
prescribed author, viz., Tennyson’s “Gareth and
Lynette ’’ (MacMillan), (b) Latin, (c) French, (d) German,
(e) Elementary Chemistry. Any candidate for admission
to the Professional Division, who has previously passed
the examination for the Clerical Division, may, on appli-
cation being made by him, be credited on examination
with the number of marks obtained by him at the
Clerical Examination in the subjects that are common to
the two examinations. Application to attend the ex-
amination must be made on a form for the purpose,
which can be obtained at the office of the Board, and
must reach the undersigned not later than the Ist June.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee of
10s., which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefore forwarded
to the Secretary of the Board with the form of applica-
tion. A list of registered candidates, with centres of
examination, will be published in the Govrrnmbnt
Gazkttb, after the time for the receipt of applications has
expired, and candidates will also be advised direct of the
place at which they will be examined.
Copies of the Regulations in regard to this examination
can be obtained from the Government Printing Office.
By order of fche Board,
J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

Please mention this paper when writing.
THE LAST OF SIX, by Ernest Favenc; price,
1/-: nosted. 1/3.
Q. Labor papers seem to prosper. Accord-
ing to last balance-sheet the Worker is on tho
boom ; People’s Newspaper has made steady
headway ; Towers Eagle never looked healthier ;
Gympie Truth-the ablest of them all—has
blossomed into a semi-weekly ; Toowoomba
Democrat, at last sight, had been enlarged ;
Ipswich Standard has paid for its plant and is
now firmly established; Maranoa Advocate
grows fat with ads. But where, oh where, is the
Democratic Brisbane daily ?
The first faint beat of the electoral drum is
heard just now in Bananaland. The tough old
Tory M.L.A.’s have to face the Opposition of the
Democrats only but in the Democratic camp.
A multitude of aspirants, just free from addressing
the hens in the back-yard from the top of a wood-
heap, are hustling one another viciously wherever
a dubious seat gives promise of defeat to Fat
Man. The dry bones of the Old Opposition are
astir, and suggestions for a compromise with
Labor are in the air. Significant—the num-
ber of hints and invitations given to Charles
Powers in the last few months from various Demo-
cratic strongholds.
A favorite anti-Federal dodge to catch the
crowd is to purport to show that, in consequence
of the fixing of a minimum of five members in the
House of Representatives for any province, New
South Wales will in half-a-century have one
member for each 300,000 people or so, and Tas-
mania one for each 40,000 or so. “Therefore,”
triumphantly, “in a few years, even in that so-
called popular House, one Tasmanian will equal
eight men in New South Wales.” More nonsense,
of course, for it presupposes that the House of
Representatives will consist of 60 members
whether the Commonwealth’s population consists
of 40 million or four million people, while the Con-
stitution simply provides for 60 as a minimum
suited to present conditions.

Pink Pills for Pale People
Absolutely Cure these Ailments.
Aching Rheumatism.
IN BED FOR ONE LONG YEAR.
NOW STRONG AND ENERGETIC AND
WITHOUT A PAIN.
The cold days of winter are near at hand, if not
already here. The unhappy suffer from rheuma-
tism and such ailments dreads the return of his
or her complaint. There is one way, however, by
which rheumatism may be positively and per-
manently cured. In proof of this we append the
following statement: —
A reporter recently visited Mr. Walter Tye,
of 33 M'Arthur-street, Ultimo, Sydney, whose
marvellous recovery from a most severe attack of
acute rheumatism has been the subject of much
comment in that neighborhood. Mr. Tye readily
volunteered to give the following statement of his
illness and recovery :
“ I am milkman by trade, and ride on the cart
when distributing milk. We serve a very long
round. You can readily see that the very nature
of my occupation compels me to be out in all sorts
of weather, for whatever happens we must not
disappoint our customers. It is some 18 months
ago that after being out in very inclement wea-
ther, I commenced to suffer from aching agonies
in limbs and body, and flying pains and stiffness
in the joints ; and I also experienced sudden chills
generally succeeded by heavy sweats. The pain I
suffered was so severe that sleep was an impossi-
bility, and should I doze off I would be suddenly
aroused by spasms in the muscles of the joints in
which the rheumatism was worst. I had the best
medical advice, and the premises of a well-known
chemist were fairly ransacked in search of a
specific that would tend to alleviate my agony.
To make a long story short, I was laid up for 12
months.
“Relief, however, at last arrived, and from an
unexpected quarter.
“ Boiler-maker Jarvis and his wife are near
neighbors of ours, and I had read of his wonderful
recovery from locomotor ataxia by the agency of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Thinking they might
possibly have a beneficial effect in my own case, I
determined to try them and did so. It was not
until after the third box that I experienced any
decided change for the better, but you must bear
in mind that mine was a case of long standing,
and, it was feared, one that might become chronic.
My health then improved by leaps and bounds,
and I am now, as you can see, strong and hearty.
I took, in all, eight boxes.
“The nature of my business call for the display
of considerable activity, and I am pleased to state
that I have had no return of my old symptoms,
and can face the coming winter without any
anxiety.”
“Have we your permission to publish these
particulars, Mr. Tye?”
“ Certainly you have.”

Sciatica for Two Entire Years.
“I AM CERTAIN I AM COMPLETELY
CURED.”
Mr. George Hynds, a well-known resident of
Blayney, was recently interviewed by a Blayney
Advocate reporter about his serious illness and
remarkable cure. He obtained the following in-
teresting narrative, which Mr. Hynds said he
would be glad to have published
“ About two years ago I fell a victim to sciatica.
I was drawing wood at the time. The complaint
forced me every now and again to take a spell.
The ailment kept getting worse, until at last I
could not even work, and had to go to bed, being
unable to walk. It affected my right leg from the
hip to the ankle, the pain being worse in my ankle;
I also suffered from terrible pains in my back. I
was in bed for four months, during which time I
suffered excruciating pain. I had a doctor attend-
ing me all the time, but he gave me no relief,
although he used every means m his power
• ~ n /T 1.1/v inof o c if if morn Krnlron
tard and fly blisters on my leg, which left it in a
terrible state, and did not do me the least bit of
good. At last he told me that he could do no
more for me, and that the sciatica would have to
take its course. I then determined to go tQ the
Bathurst Hospital, but my medical adviser said
he didn’t think I would get any benefit there.
However, I said I would try it. After being
completely bedridden for four months, and on the
day before I was going to the hospital, I managed
to get into town, and I met a friend and told him
my intentions. This gentleman strongly advised
me to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People
before. I went to the hospital. As my friend spoke
with such confidence about the pills, I took his
advice, and went there and then to Mr. C. Fer-
guson’s store, and purchased the last three boxes
he had. After the first box I could get about
again, and I went to work again drawing in
wheat for Mr. Robert Marsden. When I had
finished the second box I felt no pain what-
ever, and considered I was cured. I then took
the third box at leisure, although there was no
occasion for it. I commenced taking the pills last
October 12 months. Mr. Marsden and Mr. Fer-
guson and a host of other people who knew my
previous condition could hardly believe the rapid
change which came over me. Some people said
that the sciatica would again return to me next
winter, but I am glad to say I went through the

terrible complaint. I have enjoyed excellent
health ever since I took the pills, and my cure has
been the means of a large number of people taking

“I am certain that the pills and no other

Influenza’s Dread After-Effects.
PARALYSIS AND ERYSIPELAS AS A
RESULT.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS PERFORM
A MARVELLOUS CURE.
“Yes,” said Miss Jamieson, in answer to a
query put to her by a representative, who waited
on her at her residence, Autumn-street, Ashby,
Geelong, Victoria. “ I know something about the
curative properties of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Some few years back, when influenza was so bad,
I was unfortunate enough to experience a very
severe attack, and the after-effects remained on
me for some time.
“Although generally experiencing robust health
previous to this attack, when at last I was able to
leave my room I was almost of ghostly proportions
—not at all like lam now. And I continued in
this state for some time. I did not mind the weak-
ness so much, feeling sure that would wear off in
time with good nourishment. But in March of
last year I commenced to experience great incon-
venience from pains in my right side. I consulted
L_
amedicalman, who
said that it was
merely the after-
effects of the influ-
enza, and I was not
to worry myself.
But, as the pains
continued, I did
worry myself, and
went to Melbourne
to consult a special-
ist. He informed
me that it was par-
tial paralysis. He
put me through a
course of treat-
ment that left me
as I was, and I returned home fairly worn out.
Whether the worry, or the heroic treat-
ment I had undergone was responsible or
not, I will not venture an opinion upon, but
a week afterwards erysipelas set in on my face,
and for weeks and months I suffered the greatest
agony. In January a friend (a Miss Evans,
of Eaglehawk, near Bendigo) came down to
see me, and one day she bought me a box of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. When visiting Mel-
bourne I took them, and four days afterwards the
pain commenced to go away. I continued taking
them for some time, and in all I used eight boxes,
discontinuing them in August. From then until
now I have not had the least symptom of a return
of either complaints—complaints that I had not
been free from for four years. lam 22 years of
age. Scores know my sufferings, and the means
of my recovery I am only too pleased to supply.”
“ Your friend, Miss Evans, must have felt very
pleased at the success of her experiment.” “ Yes,
it was the knowledge that a neighbor had gained
considerable benefit from the pills, in a very bad
case of debility, that induced her to buy me the
first box of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.” “ One can
scarcely realise the fact of your once resembling
what the departed are credited with, Miss Jamie-
son.”
“No (laughingly) there is nothing ethereal
about me now. lam nearer 12st. than 9st. Yes,
you can include that, if you like,” and with a
word of thanks the representative withdrew.
Neuralgia for Ten Dreary Years.
A MELBOURNE LADY’S SUFFERINGS.
“ Neuralgia,” said Mrs. Ross, of Rathdown and
Palmerston streets, Melbourne, “ was the com-
plaint I suffered from for a period of ten or twelve
years. During the daytime my sufferings were
painful, but on retiring to rest the agony I suffered
was excruciating, reducing me to a state of general
collapse. I felt it during the day slightly, but
most severely at night.”
“ Did you consult any doctors at all ?”
“Many; but received no permanent benefit.
They gave me draughts and powders, but the
effect was really nothing.”
“You, of course, ha’e tried other remedies?”
“ Yes; I tried everything, but obtained no
relief.”

Pills for Pale People ? ”
“An elderly lady, resident at Heathcote, 74
years of age, who had been a sufferer from rheu-
matism for 40 years, and who had been absolutely

Pale and Sallow Girls.
HOW DISEASE AND DEATH AWAIT
THEM.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth.
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, yellow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeaks disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched,
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at
the time their daughters are budding into
womanhood. If your daughter is pale, com-
plains of weakness, is “tired out” upon the
slightest exertion; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side, if her temper is
fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
for the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly ; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pills upon the approach of the period of woman-
hood, and thus avoid the risks of disease or early
decay.
4.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are
not a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly
scientific preparation, the result of years of care-
ful study on the part of an eminent Edinburgh
University physician, and they were successfully
used by him in his everyday practice for years
before being offered for general sale. They are
a perfect blood builder and a nerve restorer,
curing such diseases as rheumatism, neuralgia,
partial paralysis, locomotor ataxia, St. Vitus’
dance, nervous headache, general debility, nervous
prostration, the after effects of la grippe, influenza,
and severe colds, diseases depending on humours
in the blood, such as scrofula, chronic erysipelas,
etc. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills give a healthy
glow to pale and sallow complexions, and are a
specific for all troubles peculiar to the female sys-
tem, such as anaemia, poor and watery blood,
female irregularities, etc. In the case of men they
effect a radical cure in all cases arising from men-
tal worry, over-.vork, or excesses of any nature.
These' Pills are not a purgative. They are
genuine only with the full name, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold by all
chemists and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Company, Sydney, who will forward (post paid)

2s. !>d., or lialf-a-doz *n for 15s. 3d. They are

“ Tents are unhealthy. Better an open
bivouac, with the feet to a good fire.” —Napoleon.
“Country residents who get their living
by tillage,” is what Launceston, Tas., Daily
Telegraph calls farmers.
The coroner’s jury on the Dudley colliery
explosion, Newcastle, N.S.W. (15 lives lost), re-
turned an open verdict; and N.S.W. Mines Dept,
announces that the ventilation regulations will
now be strictly enforced.
At a N.Q. railway-station, recently, a first-
class carriage was seen suddenly to disgorge its
contents in a condition of much drench and
d n ! A shunter who had been directed to fill
the lavatory-tank on the carriage-top had turned
the water in at the lamp-hole !
According to S.A. Register, one of the
prisoners at Mt. Gambier criminal court was
sentenced to “six months’ with hard labor, to be
served in Mt. Gambier Hotel.” The meaning of
this is presumably that reporters’ thoughts still
have a bias in the same old direction.
The case of William King, of Pentridge
(Vic.), is interesting. William is a convict in
irons and deposited in a dark and lonesome cell.
Once in every few years William is let off the
chain, and out into the prison-yard, and he in-
variably seizes the first opportunity to fall bodily
on a warder, and try to blow his eye out, or belt
hi 3 head in with a shovel, whereupon he is seized
and returned to his lonesome cell, and presently a
J.P. drops in and gives him nine months in irons.
This happened for the ’teenth time, the other day.
Everybody admits that King is mad, but official-
dom has not the necessary sense to have him re-
moved to an asylum where he would be properly
looked after; and the chances are that William
will kill a warder one of these days, and be
banged.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Incompatibles.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the wharf, Brownlow Cole kissed his wife
tenderly, and went aboard the steamer as the
sailors began to loosen the ropes that held the
gangway. Leaning over the railing as the vessel
forged slowly sideways into the stream, his lips
were trembling and there was a film over his eyes.
He saw her standing near the edge of the wharf
with two other women, smiling in enjoyment
of the whole scene of departure—the vessel
with its uncertain, irregular movements; its
hissing steam, its clattering winding-engine, as
well as the churned and eddying water, and the
fluttering of handkerchiefs from the decks and
wharves.
For a moment it hurt him to think she was not
affected otherwise. They had been married two
years, and this was to be their first real parting.
At such a moment, he felt as if nothing could
possibly have distracted his attention from her.
And naturally, as she loved him, it seemed
to him but right that he should have occupied her
whole attention for the time being. “ But she is
such a child,” he said to himself.
The eccentricities of the screw ceased. The
long line that had served to warp the vessel into
the stream had been cast off from the shore and
drawn aboard, and a boat with two sailors in it
was being hoisted up into the davits. For a
minute there was a pause, as if the vessel were
inflating her iron lungs, waiting intent upon the
starter’s “Go !”
Cole took his glasses out in order to see his wife
more plainly. She sawtheact, and waved her hand-
kerchief back to Cole, smiling. He was impressed
with her beauty as she stood on the wharf in the
morning sun. The white muslin dress showed the
outlines of her splendid, full figure against the
shadow of the sheds. The fine velvety skin with
the peach-bloom of her cheeks, the crowning
masses of her dark burnished hair, the face, every
mood and expression of which he knew so in-
timately, all appealed to him, for the moment,
under the exceptional circumstances, with a
magnified, exaggerated charm.
It hurt him to think she was so beautiful. He
could not help wishing that she were somewhat
less attractive. It seemed to him as if her charms
must have impressed other men as exaltedly as
they had him, and that all other men desired her
with the fervency of his own desire. He scanned
the men upon the wharf to see if any of them
noticed her, and, if so, to detect their thoughts
from expression or manner. It was a habit he
had learned. In a glance he seemed to see her
beset by men who aimed at her undoing, while he
would not be beside her to counsel and protect her.
The thought made him shudder coldly.
For she was weak. Again and again he had
warned her that she put too much faith in men’s
honor.
She had not the faculty of fencing her sex
round with the difficulty and the reserve that
safeguards it against disrespect. In her inter-
course with men, she left unwittingly here and
there little loopholes, through which he had a
haunting dread that some skilful and unscrupulous
adventurer might enter and wreak upon her untold
wrong. There was no stability in her character.
Her impulses swayed her. Her mind was little.
Incidents that to hint were of the utmost triviality
appealed to her as serious and important. He
had despaired of ever being able to put himself
into her warped and narrow point of view, just as
he had despaired of ever being understood by her.
She had no conception of the dignity of his pur-
suits, or of his literary ambitions. It was incom-
prehensible to her that he should have any honor
* writer _ And yet when she heard h £ w?rk
praised, her love for him expanded, temporarily,
into idolisation. At first he had tried to encour-
age her to take an interest in his work, but find-
elfort vain, he had desisted, and returned
to the inner isolation of his bachelorhood. Ihey
had quarrelled; afc times bitterly-misinterpreting
each other. He had invariably been the first to
relent, and this, in a way, had spoiled her. It
had led her into doing blameworthy acts with
certainty of being forgiven—acts which
otherwise she might have been deterred from,
ghe had done ma * y thingB which were capable of
being wrongly construed, and which had tortured
him with suspicion often and often ; but generally
after more or less delay, and more or less trouble,
explanations and reconciliations had been effected,
But she was unreliable. She concealed her
transgressions in a jungle of misrepresentation,
wherein he usually lost himself endeavoring to
discover them, and so would give up the pursuit,
adopting the most feasible and condoning way she
would show him out of the tangle. He had never
in his heart felt that he could absolutely trust her.
Still, for all, he had never had any tangible trans-
gression on which to base a specified accusation ;
and perhaps she was right when she often told
him that he was of a morbidly suspicious tempera-
ment. On such occasions he was wont to reply,
“It is because of the intense interest I take in
you. If I loved you less, I would bother about
you less. Why can’t you deal openly with mein
all things? I do so with you.”
She was impulsive, passionate, wilful, unstable,
unreliable, trivial, childlike.
He was emotional, artistic, irritable, discon-
tented, and exacting.
She had many qualities finer than he, and many
baser, but he had the feeling latterly that, instead
of lifting her up to his level, she had all the time
gradually been dragging him down to hers,
, „ , *

** e 100^ ed a £am. He found her standing apart
from them talking to a man whom he had not en-
countered in his binocular survey. He recognised
h '™ as occupant of the room adjoining theirs
the hotel & German named Druck, tall and
full-blooded, dominant, triumphantly vinle-and
handsome. He had warned her-even quarrelled

uian, who bad more than once audaciously at-
tempted to offer her presents. Druck was an
amateur photographer and a painter m oils. He

, As the vessel rounded the bend of the river she
had her back turned, but he could see that she was
fidgetting uneasily on her feet while the German
stood looking down upon her through his glasses
exultmgiy.
* * * *
The steamer passed out of the river with the
afternoon’s tide, and, rounding the cape, turned
her head southwards. By that time he had
worked himself into a fever of uneasiness about
her. He paced about the deck restlessly and tire-
lessly. At night he could not sleep. His mind
had room for nothing but the one horrible
obsession which devoured it piecemeal. He
tried to cast it out without avail. He tried
to take the most favorable view of the matter,
but his mind had become too fevered to
calmly weigh the pros and cons. He thought
of the consequences of a downward step to a
woman of her nature, and he traced her remorse-
lessly through them all. He felt that he could
never again know any happiness while she was
adrift upon the world—“a dove that all God’s
hawks for prey had got.” He thought of the hold
she had upon his life, and, for all her faults, of the
loneliness, and worse than lovelessness, she had
replaced. In his hours of discontent he had
often compared his married life with his bachelor-
hood, and had resolved always that, even at its
worst, the former was the better of the two.
By night, as he watched the vessel tearing a
path through the hungry, clamorous ways of the
sea, another thought flashed often over his mind —
a thought on which his life hung. He wondered
what sustained the thought itself.
i-r , *, . * , * , * . ,
He left the steamer, buoyed somewhat with the
idea that, if she were determined to be unfaithful,
not all the safeguards in the world would prevent
her. At any rate, he told himself he had
done all that he was capable of, and his worrying
about her now could make no difference. He
knew, beyond all doubt, that she loved him as
much as she could possibly love anyone, and more
than she loved anyone else, and on that rock he
buißhis hope, and comforted himself as best as he
* * • •
She met him on his return, smiling. She
laughed at his suspicious questionings. She asked
him if he thought she couldn’t look after herself,
and if the German mattered anything to her.
Pooh ! He felt easier, but he had often noticed
how his suspicions became disarmed in her
presence. She answered all his questions as to
where she had gone, and what she had done,
readily. She had hardly seen the German at all,
and she had had nothing further to say to him
than the ordinary courtesies of the day She had
slept with her doors locked at night. One night
the key of her door was missing, and she had
jammed a chair against it, for fear. He felt that
he had been unjust to her, and he told her so.
She laughed
Three day's afterwards she presented him with
a gold pencil-case. He asked her, guardedly,
whence she had procured it.
“From Mitchell, the jeweller,” she said, “I
bought it for you there.”
Passing Mitchell’s in the afternoon it occurred
to him to go in and inquire.
They knew nothing whatever about it.
He was horrified at the discovery, but cooled
quickly into determination. At home he ques-
tioned her further, telling her eventually of the
discovery he had made.
She admitted that what she had said was wrong,
but that she had really and truly had it sent to
her by a cousin in Sydney.
“ She wrote to you also ? ”
“Yes.”
“ Where is the letter ? ”
She opened her private desk and procured it.
He devoured it in quest of some reference to the
present. He found it in a postscript, but in the
imitation of her cousin’s handwriting he recog*
nised, at a glance, the penmanship of his wife.
He burst out into a torrent of indignation and
accusation. She made no reply, but followed him
with her eyes as he walked about the room trans-
formed with fury.
Passing near her desk, his eye caught the edge
of a photograph which she had forgotten to hide.
He drew it forth astounded. It was his wife’s
portrait, in evening-dress, cut very low, with her
face and eyes turned upwards devotedly.
He had always dreaded lest his love for her
might conquer hi 3 self-respect and force him, in an
emergency, into condoning the unpardonable.
But now he was conscious of a strength altogether
unanticipated. His mind made itself up like
lightning. He went out of the house quietly.
She smiled to herself. “He will come back
again,” she said. “Besides, nothing wrong has
happened. ”
* • * *
A week afterwards she wrote to him : “ I am
going to England with Mr. Druck. Have you
anything to say ? ”
He answered : “Nothing.”

A Nightcap Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
True, there’s virtue in total abstaining
When it isn’t aggressively proud,
Nor addicted to solemn disdaining
Of jokers who shout for the crowd.
If your thirst be severely aquatic
You escape morning pains in the head,
But you taste not the pleasure ecstatic
Of a drink before going to bed.
Some people will never take warning
From the awful examples they see ;
1 hey start to get “ tight ” in the morning
And finally die of D.T.
With livers corroded by acid,
And noses condemnably red
They can’t fix a mind calm and placid
On the drink before going to bed.
Give me tea, if you will, with my dinner;
Deny me the solace of beer;
I’d as lief be a saint as a sinner
Till midnight and slumber draw near.
I abandon all claim to a wassail,
For I’d rather take whisky instead ;
Please grant me, cold temp’rance apostle,
One drink before going to bed.
Nay, preach me no arguments petty
’Gainst the evil of touching a drop—
Let the spirit be “ up to the pretty,”
And the water well-nigh to the top.
I would dream idle dreams of ambition,
Or look back on the years gaily fled,
With a sigh of contented contrition—
Good old drink before going to bed!
What memories sw 7 eet it is bringing
Of girls I had almost forgot;
They are laughing, and sighing, and singing ;
The blood through my veins runneth hot
As they pass in a misty procession,
Those charmers so easily led,
And I toast each delightful transgression
In my drink before going to bed.
Then the Best Girl of all—the deceiver
Who fooled me and maddened my brain—
She whispers, and O ! I believe her,
And Love holds me captive again.
My heart will be sad on the morrow,
All its tears even yet are not shed—
But the joys of delusion I borrow
From a drink before going to bed.
Melb.
EDMUND FISHER.
Dr. Forbes Winslow, the eminent Eng-
lish mental specialist, claims to possess a pair of
rubber-soled shoes used by “Jack the Ripper.”
He says that person was a well-connected medical
student who developed epileptic mania, was at
length confined in Broadmoor Criminal Lunatic
Asylum, and died some few months since.
The Selfishness of Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He turned his face on the pillow, so that the light
of the sunset played upon it and gave it color—a
face of almost perfect beauty, its features shar-
pened and refined by suffering. His eyes rested
upon the patient countenance of the woman at
his bedside, and something in its weary calm
seemed to irritate him more.
“ You take it so easily,” he said, with an im-
patient movement. “You don’t know what it is
to die. To die! —to leave life—warm, pleasant
life—the horror of what may be behind—the
terror of it—the terror of it! ” Sweat-beads stood
on his forehead, and his hand gripped the bed-
clothes hard.
Her eyes rested on the beauty of his face, and a
sudden passion that could find no warmth of
expression in her pale features leaped into hungry
fire in her haggard eyes.
“Do you think I feel nothing?” she said. “Is
none of the bitterness mine ? ”
He writhed again in the bed.
“ Your bitterness,” he said. “ What is yours
to mine? You’re only losing me; but I— l’m
losing Life. Life— do you hear? Life ! And
they tell me to bear it like a man. Fools !—it’s
because I am a man, and life is so good that I
can t leave it ” His voice died away in a quer-
ulous whimper.
She thought of the abject poverty to which he
was leaving her, and told herself that even death
was easier to face than such a life. By years of
scepticism, of mocking unbelief and keen ridicule,
he had destroyed her belief in a God, but she
wished now almost unconsciously that she could
pray. Not for her own sake—the misery of her
situation did not admit the possibility of comfort,
even from God—but for his.
She had heard and read of men dying like
heroes—glad to meet and grapple with Death—of
others flinging down their lives as men cast aside
a worn-out garment, and here was her husband
sobbing feebly among the pillows, at the thought
of leaving a life which had owed its pleasure and
its comfort to her hard and unremitting toil.
But she loved him—how she loved him ! And a
terrible yearning to hold him in her arms and
shield him from all the world—from Death itself
—seized her and shook her from head to foot.
But it passed, and she was calm and composed
when he looked up again.
“ Then there’s my book,” he went on, fretfully,
as if he were resuming a broken conversation ; “ I
haven’t heard from the publishers yet, but they’re
sure to bring it out. That will mean a good sum
for you, so you needn’t worry about money matters.
Oh, if I could live to enjoy my fame ! ” he broke
off again with a choking sob.
The worthless manuscript lay on the table in the
room outside, where she had placed it when it had
come back three days before—curtly rejected ; but
she did not tell him.
“ And little Lily,” he went on ; “ you'll be kind
to her ! ”
“I have been, so far; mothers generally are,”
she said, quietly, trying to steady the break in
her voice. No thought of her—only the child !
“ You'll marry again ! ” he said, after a pause,
and speaking almost fiercely. She shook her
head—the cruelty of it took away her speech.
“You will!” he insisted. “And that poor
little mite will have a stepfather. Perhaps he’ll
be kind, and then she’ll love him better than me—
and forget me. How can I lie here peacefully
with that knowledge ? My God, how can I die ? ”
His distress and weakness were pitiful. She
knelt beside him, her white face rigid in its lines
from the fierce effort of her will.
“ She shall not —I promise you. If it will com-
fort you, 1 will swear—by the God you have
taught me does not exist—that she shall not.”
His face brightened.
Her arms were round him, and her lips on his
for the last long kiss, when suddenly he drew
back again, and his lips quivered.
“ You will keep your word ? ”
“ I will keep my oath.”
He sank more heavily back on her arm.
“ That is better,” he muttered. “ How dark it’s
growing—l was doubtful—women are so weak—-
and—selfish ! ”
For a moment, a wild longing to shriek out
discordant laughter seized her. But then the
night came, and she laid the dead head gently
down.
Sydney.
NELLIE M. FIELD.
Fate’s Finger.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There were two bridges over the creek, yet people
would persist in crossing on the log which spanned
the stream about midway between them. This
log was withinahundred yards of Peter Broderick’s
hut, and one night, during a lull in the rain,
Peter opened his door and saw a lantern glimmer-
ing from the log. The light remained just long
enough for him to notice by its flicker that the
creek had risen since nightfall, till it was running
nearly a banker. Then the lantern seemed to
drop, and out of the darkness came a fearful cry.
Not the scream of a woman ; the frenzied cry of a
man in sudden peril. Peter rushed down to the
water’s edge and called,
“Who’s there? What is it?”
For answer, he heard only the rush of the
stream and the thumping of his own heart. Match
after match he struck, lout the wind blew them
out. Then he saw the lights of open doors on
both sides of the creek, and the shadows of people
moving about.
“Bring a light here, quick!” he shouted.
“ There’s a man in the creek.”
And then came the slamming of gates and the
splashing of boots in the pools, as men and boys
ran down to the bank. Then two or three fol-
lowed, carrying lanterns.
“Where are you?” someone called out in the
darkness.
“ Here,” Peter answered ; “ here at the log.”
When the lanterns arrived one of the men ex-
amined the log hurriedly. Sure enough, there
was the mark of something hard —such as a boot—
having slipped from the log. A piece of rotten
surface had been broken off, and the wood under-
neath was dry. Then somebody discovered a cap
caught on a snag, and when this was examined
everybody seemed to say together—“ Poor old
Kennedy! ”
It had happened at last! The neighbors had
expected it sooner or later. Kennedy was very
old and obstinate. Never could be persuaded
to go round by the bridge.
The news soon spread and half the township
gathered in the rain. Some had seen the old man
going home; some had spoken to him not an
hour before; and one could avouch that he had
not reached his house.
A man was sent to “The Crown,” where Dun-
can Kennedy the younger had been seen sitting in
the bar.
The messenger approached the son with natural
hesitation. “Duncan,” he said, “your father is
in the creek.”
“What! The old man?” Duncan slewed
slowly round and leant his elbow on the counter.
“ Yes. There are a lot of fellows down there
now trying to find him.”
“Oh! He’ll get out again,” said Duncan.
“ Trust the old cock for that! Don’t you chaps
bother. He’ll turn up all right to-morrow.”
But the police looked at the matter differently.
With meat-hooks fixed to the ends of clothes-
props they dragged the stream for half the
night, and when they went home at last to
wait for the dawn a light still burned in the
house across the creek where an aged woman
watched for her husband’s return.
When morning broke, Brightman, the local
undertaker, was the first to reach the creek, and
the first thing he saw was old Kennedy, shivering
on a stump on the bank--wet through, and slightly
incoherent. He had fallen in, but, after a struggle,
had got out again on the opposite side to Brode-
rick’s. Then he had made for home, but it
was very dark, and he had lost himself, and had
to wait for morning.
It was a touch ing picture that the sun rose upon.
Brightman was seated by the old man’s side on the
stump, holding him by the hand ; but there were
those who said that Brightman was a hypocrite.
They had heard his hammer during the night!
* * * *
However, in the course of years, Bright-
man did get the job, and people hardly believed
in the grief of young Kennedy, who had always
said that if they had taken his advice upon a cer-
tain occasion they wouldn’t have made such damn
fools of themselves !
“ Wait till his own turn comes,” they said, for
he had taken to heavy drinking—and the log re-
mained !
It happened accordingly, one day, that when
Duncan’s mother was seen in the township leaning
on her stick and asking everybody who passed
about her Duncan, who had not been home for two
days, the news spread widely through the little
community that Duncan Kennedy was missing,
and that when last seen he was drunk. So people
went again to the creek with their clothes-props
and meat-hooks—and this time they were success-
ful.
Everybody said he deserved it—everybody ex-
cept a poor woman who was very old and had no
other son.
